THAT SORDID MAJORITY!
Why sordid? All majorities are influenced by motives more or less sordid.
And are Labor members above these little weaknesses ? Smokers decidedly
are not. It is because they get the best value, and because they can appre-
ciate a pure, perfect, and fragrant smoke that such a large majority always
carry
CAMEO Cigarettes.
THE RED PAGE.
REVIEW Austin Dobson?’ writes Someone.
But I cannot; I like him too well. I have
a robuster mood for Praed ; and a tenderer
mood for Locker; and a jollier mood for
Calverley; and a more classical mood for Lang (I
want Lang ‘ collected’ too); but on sailor’s-pleasure
days and in my faded-rose) eaf mood I always
turn to Austin (we call him just ‘Austin,’
en famille; the Dobson assooiations breathe anti-
climax—and, priggish or not, one can’t utterly
contemn associations). And I have lost all sense
of ‘ quality ’ in companionship ; our minds have
grown alike as wife and husband grow alike in
feature and expression. But I fancy at the begin-
ning of our acquaintance I held Austin a pleasant
rhymer—sometimes a very pleasant rhymer—
but rarely more than a very pleasant rhymer.
Now we are friends he is just Austin, and
the rest goes hang. That smart fellow Juve-
nis tells me he has more craftsmanship than
inspiration; that he can navigate the shal-
lows, of sentiment, but founders in the deeps of
passion; and so on—with much antithetical
elaboration of metaphor. I agree—‘ Very likely ’
—and Juvenis, with his long pin and his label, is
off to classify the next poet for the critical
museum. I wonder what ‘A Gentleman of the
Old School ’ would think of Juvenis—he who
. . . lived in that past Georgian day,
When men were less inclined to say
That 'Time is Gold,’ and overlay
With toil their pleasure ;
He held some land, and dwelt thereon,—
Where, I forget—the house is gone ;
His Christian name, I think, was John,—
His surname, Leisure.
Reynolds has painted him—a face
Filled with a fine, old-fashioned grace,
Fresh-colored, frank, with ne’er a trace
Of trouble shaded ;
The eyes are blue, the hair is drest
In plainest way,—one hand is prest
Deep in a flapped canary rest,
With buds brocaded.
How well Austin pictures the old fellow I
He liked the well-wheel’s creaking tongue,—
He liked the thrush that stopped and sung,—
He liked the drone of flies among
His netted peaches;
He iiked to watch the sunlight fall
Athwart his ivied orchard wall;
Or pause to catch the cuckoo’s call
Beyond the beeches.
The mellowness of that verse, of the whole poem,
takes me: it seems to have grown ripe on the
same sunny wall as its subject and his peaohes.
‘Madame Placid’isa second part, and has not
quite the same appeal.
• » • *
Over ‘ Proverbs in Porcelain ’ I do not linger
so long (instead of ‘ reviewing ’ I’m telling you
what I like best); but I always linger over
‘ Babette ’ — she, I think, touches the climax of
Austin’s talent for tenderness - when he tries to
soar higher he ioses balance in the rare air of
pathos, and falls flapping clumsily (O, I see his
faults, but I feel only his oharins).

** Vignettes ” I never liked much—l fancy them
(with exceptions) immature and flat. Perhaps
Austin is always better at vehicle than burden ;
the burden is sometimes very light in “Vignettes.*
But lam fond of “ A Gage d’Amour with the
stiff but pregnant verse—
We shut our heart up, nowadays.
Like some old music-box that plays
Unfashionable airs that raise r
Derisive pity;
Alas,—a nothing starts the spring;
And 10, the sentimental thing
At once commences quavering
Its lover’s ditty.
And then the girl—and then the sum-up—
Well, well, the wisest bend to Fate.
My brown old books around me wait.
My pipe still holds, unconfiscate,
Its wonted station.
Pass me the wine 1 To Those that keep
The bachelor’s secluded sleep
Peaceful, inviolate, and deep,
I pour libation 1
* • ♦ •
The ‘ldyll of the Carp’ is nicely handled,
surely ? And ‘ The Sundial ’ I always read twice.
The wife’s favorite, she thinks, is ‘ Before Sedan,’
with the familiar —
Ah, if beside the dead
Slumbered the pain !
Ah, if the hearts that bled
Slept with the slain 1
If the grief died But no
Death will not have it so.
I admit a virtue, yet find the art overdone—
imagine I hear Austin blowing for his own organ.
What do you think ?

THE LADIES OF ST. JAMES'S.
A PROPER NEW BALLAD OF THE COUNTRY AND THE TOW».
* Phyllida amo ante alias.’ —Viro.
The ladies of St. James’s
Go swinging to the play ;
Their footmen run before them,
With a ‘ Stand by ! Clear the way i*
But Phyllida, my Phyllida !
She takes her buckled shoon,
When we go out a-courting
Beneath the harvest moon.
The ladies of St. James’s
Wear satin on their backs;
They sit all night at Ombre,
With candles all of wax ;
But Phyllida, my Phyllida !
She dons her russet gown,
And runs to gather May dew
Before the world is down.
The ladies of St. James’s !
They are so fine and fair,
You’d think a box of essences
Was broken in the air :
But Phyllida, my Phyllida !
The breath of heath and furze,
When breezes blow at morning,
Is not so fresh as hers.
The ladies of St. James’s
They’re painted to the eyes;
Their white it stays for ever.
Their red it never dies:
But Phyllida, my Phyllida!
Her colour comes and goes;
It trembles to a lily,—
It wavers to a rose.
The ladies of St. James’s!
You scarce oau understand
The half of all their speeches,
Their phrases are so grand:
But Phyllida, my Phyllida !
Her shy and simple words
Are clear as after rain-drops
The music of the birds.
The ladies of St. James's !
They have their fits and freaks;
They smile on you- for seconds ;
They frown on you—for weeks :
But Phyllida, my Phyllida 1
Come either storm or shine,
From Shrove-tide unto Shrove-tide,
Is always true—and mine.
My Phyllida ! my Phyllida 1
I care not though they heap
The hearts of all St. James’s,
And give me all to keep ;
I care not whose the beauties
Of all the world may be,
For Phyllida—for Phyllida
Is all the world to me !

THE CURE’S PROGRESS.
Monsieur the Cur 6 down the street
Comes with his kind old face, —
With his coat worn bare, and his straggling hair,
And his green umbrella-case.
You may see him pass by the little ‘ Grande Place, 9
And the tiny • Hdtel-de-Ville’ ;
He smiles, as he goes, to the fieuriste Rose,
And the pompier Theophile.
He turns, as a rule, through the ‘ Mar chi' 0001,
Where the noisy fish-wives call;
And his compliment pays to the ‘Belle ThArbse
As she knits in her dusky stall.
There’s a letter to drop at the locksmith’s shop,
And Toto, the looksmith’s niece,
Has jubilant hopes, for the Cur 6 gropes
Iu his tails for a pain d’epice.
There's a little dispute with a merchant of fruit,
Who is said to be heterodox,
That will ended be with a ‘ Ma foi, ouiF
And a pinch from the Curd’s box.
There is also a word that no one heard
To the furrier’s daughter Lou. ;
And a pale cheek fed with a flickering red,
And a “ Bon Dieu garde M'sieu’ l "
But a grander way for the Sous Prtfet,
And a bow for Ma’am’selle Anne ;
And a mock “ off-hat ” to the Notary’s oat,
And a nod to the Sacristan :
For ever through life the Curd goes
With a smile on his kind old face—
With his coat worn hare, and his struggling hair.
And his green umbrella-case.’
* • * •
Delicious picture ! And some I pass to absorb
the personal suggestion of ‘ A Revolutionary
Relic,’ and that charming child-rhyme of ‘Little
Blue-Ribbons ’; and many I cannot mention.
But you, Sir, will appreciate that reinforcement of
Red Page doctrine, ‘The Lost Elixir’—on Lowell’s
text that * One drop of ruddy human blood puts
more life into the veins of a poem than all the
delusive aurum potabile that can be distilled out of
the choicest library.’ ‘The Fables’ I read with
interest, but hardly with gURto : the ‘ Tales ’ are
better —very clever that ‘ Story from a Dic-
tionary ’ ! And on over the pages—the course that
crowns the feast! —are the ‘ Essays in Old French
Forms’ —ah! Is not ‘Circe’ best of English
triolets—in the lighter mood, at all events? And
which of Austin’s is the best English rondeau ?
one of them must be. And is there a better
English ballade than ‘The Pompadour’s Fan’—
for the art ? —though I prefer ‘ The Barmecide ’
for the idea. And what flavour in Austin’s vil-
lanelles — e.g., ‘ When I saw you last, Rose ! ’ I
hesitate among sweets, and ... give you the
in-English-inimitable chanson- fragrance of
AFTER WATTEAU.
• Embarquons-nous ! ’ I seem to go
Against my will. ’Neath alleys low
I bend, and hear across the air—
Across the stream —faint music rare, —
Whose ‘ cornemuse,' whose ‘ chalumeau’l
Hark ! was not that a laugh I know ?
Who was it, hurrying, turned to show
The galley swinging by the stair?—
‘ Embarquons-nous I ’
The silk sail flaps, light breezes blow ;
Frail laces flutter, satins flow ;
You, with the love-knot in your hair,
‘ Allans, embarqnous pour Cythere';
You will not? Press her, then, Pierrot!
‘ Embarquons-nous! ’

THE WANDERER.
Love comes baok to his vacant dwelling,—
The old, old Love that we knew of yore t
We see him stand by the open door
With his great eyes sad, and his bosom swelling.
He makes as though in our arms repelling
He fain would lie as he lay before:
Love comes back to his vacant dwelling,—
The old, old Love that we knew of yore.
Ah, who shall help us frun over-spelling
That sweet forgotten, forbidden lore 1
E’en as we doubt in our heart once more,
With a rush of tears to our eyelids willing,
Love comes back to his vacant dwelling.
That has always haunted me, and the ‘ over-
Bpelling ’ does not matter when you’re used to it.
‘Leal Souvenir’ has a touch of stiffness, but
* Ah ! but the back-look ’ glorifies it.
Leal souvenir.
“ For old sake’s sake 1 ” ’Twere hard to choose
Words fitter for an old-world Mu a
Than these, that in their cadence bring
Faint fragrance of the posy-ring,
And charms i hat rustic lovers use.
The long day lengthens, and we lose
The first pale flush, the morning hues—
Ah! but the back-look, lingering,
For old sake’s sake !
That we retain. Though Time refuse
To lift the veil on forward views,
Despot in most, he is not king
Of those kind memories that cling
Around his travelled avenues
For old sake’s sake !
* * * •
And, for a last word, take Austin’s own—
In after days when grasses high
O’er-top the stone where 1 shall lie,
Though ill or well the world adjust
My slender claim to honored dust,
I shall not question or reply.
I shall not see the morning sky ;
I shall not hear the night-wind sigh ;
I shall be mute, ns all men must
In after days !
But yet, now living, fain were I
That some one then should testify,
Saying— 1 H- held his pen in trust
To Art, not serving shame or lust.”
Will none ? Then let my memory die
In after days!

But few of noted specialists have sprung into pre-emin-

resident and practitioner at 121 Collins-street, Melbourne.
His name has become almost literally a household word"
almost every other man you meet has a wonderful story to

physicians have failed. He addresses himself in particular
to cases that have baffled the skill and science of other
doctors. His name is a sufficient guarantee of a perfect

It may save you much mental and physical suffering, and
add golden years to your life.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ON THE STAGES THAT ARE DRY.
Slowly o’er the salt-bush ridges see the moving
cattle feed;
Ne’er a gilghi-hole they break for, not a clay-pan
do they heed ;
You can hear contented breathings through the
waning afternoon,
For they’re lately from the waters of a never-dry
lagoon.
It is grand to be a drover when your stores and
roads are right,
But your very heart-strings tighten when the boy
reports* at night:
“ There’s no water for the cattle till you get to
Ginnagay—
To the troughs just past the station, over sixty
miles away ! ”
There are wrinkles on your forehead, and your
heart’s a lump of lead,
Thinking of three double stages and three troub-
lous nights ahead ;
And up-swells an anguished moaning to the
sullen autumn sky,
As you force the dumb brutes forward through
the stages that are dry.
Onward through the choking dust-clouds, down
the twining, blinding track,
To the front the strong are moving, whilst the
weak are falling back ;
Closely guarded by the mud-tank just abreast the
western wing,
Tailing off and stretching forward, in a far-extend-
ing string.
One by one they lift their faces in a pitiful appeal,
As they scent the private waters that the coola-
bahs conceal;
Then it’s “ Back, you sniffing brindle ! there’s a
boundary-rider there,
If he had the river Murrav he would still refuse
your prayer.”
Onward o’er the blazing sandhills to the clink of
bit and bar ;
Flanked by barren flats and ridges where the well-
stocked warrens are;
All the pangs of thirst concentred in each dumb
brute’s sunken eye—
Oh, it’s hell to be a drover on the stages that are
dry!
Some are crippled, some exhausted ; all are lowing
sadly weak ;
There are many left to perish where a sand-streak
marks the creek ;
You can see the front lot stringing, but the tail is
out of sight,
They begin the stage at dawning that the strong
ones made last night.
There beneath that rising dust-cloud are the leaders
miles away,
Rushing wildly in their madness to the troughs at
Ginnagay.
Says-your true and thorough stockman, “ I could
just sit down and cry ”
For it’s hell to be a drover on the stages that are
dry !
E. S. SORENSON.
HERE’S TO YER!
Ring, bells, to the high, high wind !
For this is the morn of the year
When care is cast behind
And we drink to the world good cheer.
A flagon fill first for the sailor who thinks of the
girls ashore,
Fill a mug for the stunted tailor, for the maid a
goblet more.
Fill a cup for the sighing lover, a glass for the
bushman brown,
Bring the teamster, reaper and drover ; come, men
from the busy town.
Bring the axeman down from the ranges ; come,
counsel and lawyer lean,
Ye brokers and agents on ’changes, come up to
the world’s canteen.
Come, miners and merchants and judges ; come,
pressman, parson and priest.
Bring the shepherds in from the ridges to join in
the cheer and the feast.
The wise men, the vain and the mean men, the
statesmen looking profound,
The dull men, the fat, and the lean men, all help
the world to go round.
Yes, come all ye foolish and wise ones, and bury
your petty feuds ;
Come heavy and light and good-sized ones, come
belles with your mincing dudes ;
And gather around all the kind ones, and those
who have had hard fare—
The laine and the halt and the blind ones ; let all
in the season share.
Yes, gather in too from the byeways the friend-
less poor and the sad,
Ye rich who know but the high ways—bring shekels
these hearts to make glad.
Sing hey for Smiling Pleasure ! drink deep to
Dimpled Mirth !
Pour forth the foaming measure—“ Good Luck to
all the Earth ! ”
Tas.
W.B.P.
BURIED AT SEA.
I would be still —
Oh, mighty Power, be still and let me sleep !
“Is there no rest ? Must I forever stray
In this blind, aimless, all-unknowing way?
Was life to me so calm, so quiet a thing
That Death must keep me ever hurrying
Through this vague world of waters ? Wide,
wide world
Of dead monotony 1 And Death is full,
Full of its awful voice, its restless roar,
The booming thunders of its ceaseless war
With wind and shore. Oh, mighty Power, be still,
Be still and let me sleep !
There is no rest 1
Strange scaly things and grim leviathans
Do nudge and nuzzle me in wonder whence
This shapeless, long white-shrouded thing hath
come—
I may not rest. Oh, Mother Earth, to lie
Deep buried in thy breast !
A. G. WOOD.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 1, 1898.
The Partition of John Chinaman.
The partition of Poland was an interesting
circumstance, but historically it will not be
reckoned a patch upon the partition of
China—if that event comes off as suggested
by the Ivolnische Zeituno, a German news-
paper generally very well informed as to the
proceedings of its Government. Europe has
suffered a good deal from Asia : but if, in
addition to what it has swallowed already,
Europe devours an Empire of 4,218,401 sq.
miles and 402,680,000 people—or even
if it contents itself with China Proper,
which contains merely 1,336,841 sq.
miles and 386,000,000 people —it will
have very effectually squared the account.
Then the recollection of the time when
Asia was lively, and the Mongols drove
the Turks so far that there was great trouble
in preventing them from getting into Vienna,
will grow so faint that it will be practically
invisible. And there is no expectation
that the descendants of the men who licked
the men who for three centuries were a
terror to Europe will make much more than
a pretence of effective resistance to the
annexation by European powers of the most
fertile and important provinces of their
country.
* * * »
Apparently the partition has already com-
menced. It is announced that by an agree-
ment between Russia, Germany, and France,
Russia—-which has already stolen a good
half of Manchuria—proposes now to steal
the other half; also the quasi-independent
peninsula of Corea, and “ the whole of the
noz’th-eastern province of China down to
the province of Shantung.” This seems
to mean that the Russian grab will
include part of the coast of the Gulf
P e ; Chi - li, so. that Pekin—if his
capital is left to the Chinese Emperor—will
altogether 1< >se its direct connection with the
sea via Tientsin. At a rough estimate,
Russia will add to her territory and popula-
tion a trifle of 470,0005 q. miles and 25,000,000
people—and with no trouble at all but the
trouble of putting out her grimy hand and
taking them. This brings the eastern wing
of Russia-in-Asia down to the 40th parallel ;
and, thanks to the previous annexation of
I urkestan, her western wing is even lower,
so she can move her centre forward when-
ever it suits her to absorb Mongolia and its
desert desert or pasture is all one’ to
Russia.
* * * *
Germany’s share of the loot is the valuable
province of Shantung, with four large coal-
fields and good prospects of iron, gold, and
other minerals ; with many fine harbors,
splendid agricultural valleys, and with the
Hwang-ho and its floods to regularly wash
the surplus population out to sea. Shan-
tung’s area is only 53,762 sq. miles, or not
quite half as much as Prussia’s ; but it is the
most thickly-populated province in China,
bar one, its total of 36 millions beating
Prussia’s by five. France’s share, the
Fukien province, is a little farther down the
coast, on the way to Anam ; and, if France
can see her way to annex Kwangtung later
on, her Asiatic territories will join hands
along an elegant strip of seaboard. Probably
Britain would object, however, for Hong-
kong is on the Kwangtung coast, and Canton
is the Kwangtung capital ; and France, by
collaring Fukien, has already jumped the two
treaty ports of Amoy and Foochow. Fukien is
the most thickly-populated province of China,
going 22 millions to 38,500 sq. miles, or
574 per sq. mile. Fukien is one of the
principal tea-provinces ; and its exports
total about £5,000,000 per annum. And
France is to take Formosa—Formosa, ceded
to Japan by China after the recent war, and
if Japan objects, Russia, Germany and
France are to combine to smash her ! The
partition is worthy of Napoleon, with a
dash of Metternich and Peter the Great
thrown in.
* * * *
Japan and Britain are very much out in
the cold. The most important territorial
grab of the century has been carried through
without them. The seizure of Formosa is a
severe blow to Jap vanity, swollen to
arrogance by her victories over China ; yet
she can only sit and fume. To fight three
Great Powers at once is a contmct from
which even the rashest Little Brown Man
may well shrink. And the Little Brown
Man can reflect, as he impotently bites his
fingers, that he himself is in great part
responsible for his humiliation. Before the
Chino-Jap war it was with very good reason
surmised that China was worthless as
a fighting power; but the proof was
not absolute—or, rather, though it was
mathematically absolute, it was not visible to
the naked eye. Japan conclusively showed
that the Chinese army was a nonentity, the
Chinese navy a cipher, and the Chinese
commissariat a chaos. Then, doubtless
at Russia’s suggestion, the curious trio of
Powers decided that, as the joint was hot,
they might as well carve it. The carving
has been done with admirable skill ; and the
shock to the British Cabinet may be
imagined. It is always a shock to Britain to
find that other Powers are capable of devour-
ing weaker peoples piecemeal in the fashion
of which she has been the chief modern ex-
ponent ; and one may safely wager that, at
this moment, the British press is howling the
whole gamut from surprise to rage and in-
dignation. Britain, who grabbed India, who
grabbed North America, who grabbed Aus-
tralia, who grabbed a hundred other items of
territory, large or small, and missionaried or
murdered the aboriginal inhabitants out
of existence, is convinced that she alone
grabs by right Divine, and that the grab
of every other nation is directly impelled
from the Pit. But Britain no more than
Japan dares tackle the three Powers.
Europe is beginning to do without Britain,
much to the latter’s astonishment ; and
though foolish Britons bellow defiance,
sensible Britons by no means relish the
“ splendid isolation ” —of an old wolf at the
tail of a hungry pack. And the partition of
China without Britain’s knowledge or con-
sent is an ominous answer to the bombast of
Queen Victoria’s jubilee.
* * * *
Yet it is not very clear what benefit Ger-
many and France ai’e going to derive from
becoming Russia’s catspaws—beyond the
certainly immense benefit of conciliating
Russia for European-concert-purposes in
the present. Russia is at home in Asia :
a Tartar at heart, she knows how to
rule a Tartar; and the Tartars have
long known how to rule the Mongols. But
Germany is far from home, and France is
as far. Both nations have learnt that their
permanence as first-rank powers, in view of
the ever-increasing might of America and
Russia, depends upon expansion at home or
abroad ; and as expansion at home means
bloody war both nations are making sacrifices
to establish colonies abroad after the British
fashion. But neither French nor Ger-
man colonies seem to prosper, and the men
and money taken to maintain them can ill be
spared from the armed camps in Europe.
Possibly, with no Chinese opposition, it
may be possible to farm the most fruitful
Chinese provinces at a profit ; but if the
partition goes on, as is its inevitable ten-
dency, it looks as if Germany and
France might find they have bitten more
than they can chew. That was the mistake
Spain made with America. For she tried
to spread herself over not Mexico only,
not Peru only, but over the whole of South
America; and she spread herself out too
thin. She did not keep South America,
and the struggle cost her so much that
she fell from a first-rate to a third or
fourth - rate power, and looks perma-
nently fixed at that. And just as Eng-
land gained by Spain’s loss, so Russia
will gain by the loss of France and Germany
—if they eventually have to fall back from
Asia as Spain fell back from America.
Then, having assimilated China as she
assimilated Poland, and her Caucasian
and Armenian provinces, and Siberia, and
Turkestan, and all her other wonderful ac-
quisitions since the days of Ivan 111., Russia
may turn to the East and then ?
* » * *
This is vague and far-away speculation, for
Russia will agree to much in Europe and
Africa for the sake of a free hand in Asia,
and there is enough of Asia left to mono-
polise her energies for a good many years to
come. What seems reasonably certain is
that the aggregation of peoples called the
Chinese Empire, after having preserved a
separate individuality and history through
ages which have seen a score of first rate
nations rise from darkness and perish in
oblivion, has commenced to fall to pieces
under the most favorable' auspices, and with
the greatest possible chance of continuing
to fall until the pieces are unidentifiable.
As a vast historical institution, the Chinese
Empire is the most aged and most respect-
able item on earth. It was living under just
and enlightened laws centuries before Solon
saw light, and when Justinian’s ancestors
of the fiftieth generation had never been
dreamt of. Its literature was classic when
the Eastern world could neither read nor
write ; and its philosophers speculated and
experimented when the prehistoric propa-
gator of Isaac Newton was a hardshell
savage making barely-articulate noises when
he wanted a bone. Away back in anti-
quity it invented printing, and gunpowder,
and similar notions, and grew tired
of them long before modern science was
in its swaddling-clothes. The mind may
travel back past the glories of Rome, and
Carthage, and Greece, until it comes in
Europe to a dead wall, on the other side of
which is utter darkness, and still there is a
settled government in China, and arts
flourish, and books are written, and theatres
are open, and young men with slanting eyes
are running over willow-pattern plates with
moon faced young women, through a country
where fantastic pagodas lean to canals flow-
ing under impossible bridges, and dragons fly
under trees clipped like whales looking out
of fifth-storey windows. And, in the after-
dinner hush of Christmas Day, The Bulletin
beckoned to a passing vegetable John and
laid these solemn matters before him, and
enquired if he was acquainted with Mene,
Mene, and likewise with '1 ekel, Upharsin,
and whether he knew his ancient empire was
about to be swallowed by the Medes and
Russians, and the Flowery Land was going
to be trodden flat under the iron heel of a
foreign militarism. And that vegetable John
sadly and emphatically replied, “ No savee ! ”
Maoriland's Middle-Class Women.
“ A Rabid Englishman ” to The Bulletin :
The extension to the masses of a university education,
never abundantly successful in Europe, reaps disaster in
countries remote from the large centres of the civilised
world. The seething- discontent in Maoriland feminine
circles is the digestion of the pudding that many politi-
cians h ive evolved out of women's education. Maori-
land is suffering acutely from over-education of its
women. Parents of all classes concentrate their energies
to the end that the girls of the family should gain univer-
sity honors —to what purpose ?
* * * *
The children of the past decade are growing up, and,
with the children of this, constitute in themselves an
educational revolution. The girls are better educated
than the boys. Consumed by a conscious egoism, the New
Maoriland woman is devoid of the elegances of affectation.
Her ambitions are petty, her views of life strictly limited.
Her emotions are the ferment of the brain, arid shq has
ceased to believe in the existence of heart. Possessing
neither tenderness nor sympathy, she is capable of doing
a kind action without,believing it to be such. The train-
ing she undergoes is not calculated to make her appear to
any great advantage. She rides with spirit, plays lawn-
tennis, golf and croquet energetically, and dances prettily.
She thrives best beyond the trammels of civilisation, and
mopes under restraint. Her spirits are light, her
laughter joyous ; in the backwoods she is eminently com-
panionable ; but when fettered by the conventionalities
of a town she rings artificially, and originates an impres-
sion in a stranger’s mind that may not be altogether
groundless.
♦ * m *
Practical is written across M.L. women’s brows, and it
spoils their faces. A little less study and a great deal
more daintiness would blend better with their component
characteristics. Womanliness appeals to the world, and
the world of a woman in Maoriland is principally mascu-
line. Yet a want of tact is worse than a want of virtue.
• * * *
The sincerity of the Maoriland girl is the frankness
emanating from a life that points to Nature as its main-
spring. All paths touch somewhere the pastoral and
agricultural pursuits of the province, and by this contact
the freedom of the country influences the artificial con-
ditions paramount in towns. The contrast on a change
of circumstances is accepted with great pluckiness, and
in the marriage of the period in Maoriland such a con-
trast is usually present. The wife assists her husband in
his fight, and by her sole exertions settles the conduct of
the establishment. In England, the principle of marrying
under straitened circumstances and struggling to im-
provement is viewed askance ; but in Maoriland, girls
reared in comparative comfort seize the occasion, and,
striving pertinaciously, slowly float, to stages of affluence.
This trait in a character so complex suggests that their
selfishness is superficial, and their generosity dormant.
» * * *
Entertaining is treated with supreme indifference. The
hospitality is charming, even lavish in the generosity ex-
tended. No request is untoward. The guest comes and
goes, and advent or departure creates no diversion. The
even tenor of the way steadily progresses, and is in-
variably dull. Dinners are rather terrible ; the clever
people’never listen, the stupid people never talk.
Courses are generally eaten in dead silence. The hostess is
nervous, the host sleepy, and the dinner a ghastly gastro-
nomic muddle. The humble chop is elevated to the posi-
tion of a chef-d’ceuvre, and at that has no appreciable
evidence of tenderness.
* * * *
Possessing patience without ambition, self-confidence
without self-esteem, and devoid of the dignity conferred
l, v experience, the M.L. woman of the old school remains
the embodiment of a kindly soul imbued with most
generous instincts. She moons quietly along in the de-
parting footsteps of the age, content to have eradicated
from the education of her children all that was advan-
tageous to herself. In consequence, the Maoriland
maiden is a farrago of inadequacies—energetic without
elegance, active without grace, loquacious without wit,
mistaking bustle for style, raillery for badinage, noise for
gaiety. It is difficult to determine the responsibility for
the conduct of the provincial minx. Morally culpable the
parents may be, but the influence of systematic studies is
the more potent factor. The ethics of pleasant conversa-
tion they have never acquired ; the principles of dressing
are too intricate. It is wrong to imagine that the women
do not know how to dress ; they do, but they don’t do it
properly. Some women obtain their clothes from England,
and, when they are well-groomed, do credit to their
tailors. Principally, however, they are at a loss to know
what to wear, or how to wear it. The crisis typifies a
wholesale slaughter of existing fashions. The result is
crude, occasionally bizarre, seldom artistic. Of English
poets the ordinary Maoriland woman knows little; of
Continental writers in any capacity she knows less.
She may perhaps pay greater attention to music. Her
artistic appreciation is neither deep nor fervent. When
she plays, her music has no soul; and when she sings,
her soul has no voice.
• * * *
As a country' delivered to the tender mercies of the
advanced woman, Maoriland attempts to exploit any
movement that will keep her subdued. The allotment
of the franchise was signally weak. The sop to Ckr-
bkrus is only temporary. If a dog growls and is fed,
on the morrow he wiil growl again. Franchise has
raised a particular species of rabid femininity in Maori-
land, and what conjuration will exorcise it only Satan
knows. The section preaches partial impact, and at
gatherings of partisans talks volubly of political rotten-
ness and social reform. These views of reform induce
their holders to wear bloomers under skirts, to vaunt
their independence of men and even manners, to
blatantly nurture “prohibition,” and to irritate their
party with imbecile criticisms and hamper it by womanly
mutations. The political woman has become a nuisance.
She is anxious to retain all the privileges of her sex
and Secure besides all those of man. She wants to be
a man and remain a woman. She will not become a
man, and it will be a public blessing when she ceases
to be a woman.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Approaching Fall.
Apparently the mana of Richard Seddon is
a diminishing quantity. The Maoriland session
just closed has been the most disastrous one
since the beginning of the Liberal regime seven
years ago. Seddon’s absence in England was
the beginning of the trouble ; he came back
with a title and the trail of the Jubilee grovel
upon him, and the ill-luck which seems to
attend so many Premiers who go wandering
BIJLL-wards has been more or less with him
ever since. He came back to find that his locum
tenens, big “ Jock ” M'Kenzie, had been run-
ning amok in his absence, and had lost no
chance of saying something foolish, or doing
something large and honest and clumsy and
elephantine. It would have taxed the resources
of a far more diplomatic man than Seddon to
explain away “ Jock ” M‘Kenzte’s sayings and
doings, and the M.L. Premier didn’t even try.
The tales of the Jubilee grovel followed
Richard of Kumara back to Wellington, and
made a bad impression on the democratic
Maorilanders. The bill for that same grovel
was a subject of bitter contention, and the
row over the ill - fated account made
more trouble. Then Joseph Ward, the
ex-Treasurer of the Seddon Ministry, re-
appeared in Parliament, an uncertificated insol-
vent, with a somewhat doubtful standing in the
Legislature, and with the moral stench of the
Farmers’ Association and the Colonial Rank
hanging about him like an atmosphere. Sed-
don’s fierce loyalty to his old colleague alienated
much of the snulllesome element of the House
of Representatives, and the rumor that Joseph
Ward might even resume his old place at the
Treasury was a cause of bitter anger. The pur-
chase of the Bushy Park estate from theB.N.Z.
by Minister M‘Kenzie’s sons aroused a storm
of peculiar intensity. The proposed vote of
censure on Seddon himself for holding a paid
billet with a British mining syndicate was
almost carried by an Opposition, several of the
leaders of which themselves hold paid billets
with British and other land syndicates, and,
though the Government held its own, it did it
by a very narrow majority, and the Liberal
party split badly over the question. All through
the session a bitter, continuous stonewall was
kept up by the Tory party and the fragmentary
sept led by Stout, the renegade Radical of
Dunedin. And the Legislative Council rose
definitely in revolt, and deliberately re-
jected every progressive measure sent up
to it. The Old Age Pensions Bill was lost
in the nominee chamber ; also the measure
for the restriction of alien immigration, the
Wages Protection Bill, and the Apprentices
Bill—a measure designed to put down some of
the worst forms of sweating. The prospects of
all these, and of the State Fire Insurance
scheme, look quite as hopeless for next session—
in fact, it has become evident that either the
Upper House must be reformed or the Liberal
movement must stop short. Therefore, Pre-
mier Seddon has formally declared gory war
against the Council, and has practically staked
the existence of his Government on the issue.
But his majority in the House of Representa-
tives is comparatively small now ; there seems
a chance of the Opposition being strength-
ened shortly by the substitution of Stout as
leader for the impossible Russell ; the down-
fall of Joseph Ward has weakened the
Ministry; it is weighed down by the dead
weight of M‘Kenzie ; and Seddon’s title is
against it; and the Jubilee legends are against
it; and Seddon himself is a weary Titan
struggling under the burden of too many port-
folios (he carries six of them in all), growing
old and a little broken, soured by the bitter
politics of a province where politics are more
bitter than in any other of the Australias, and
with his many good works already beginning to
he forgotten. Judging by present appearances
the mana of old sturdy Digger Dick will just
about last another session.
A European Trouble.
It has become a kind of legend in Europe that
centuries have a habit of closing with a huge
political convulsion, and there is a vague feeling
afloat that the break-up of the Austrian empire
maybe the boss convulsion which will celebrate
the end of the 19th century. Nobody really
knows how that harlequin state has held to-
gether so long. It consists of eighteen distinct
nations and tribes, and a few fragments that
are not large enough to be called tribes. It


includes more languages than it is easy to count.
Almost every one of its multitudinous clans
hates the other seventeen with a fine old crusted
hatred whose beginnings date back to the days
of the Roman empire or thereabouts. They
came together by accident ; some of them are
there because they were driven southward by
the old Gothic irruptions ; some because they
were elbowed eastward by the Romans and
Franks ; some because they were chased north-
ward by Greeks and Turks and others ; some
because the Tartars harried them out of the
East. The debris of a multitude of races came
together with a clash in the centre of Europe,
and remained there for want of any other place
to go. Hardly any of them are strong enough
to stand alone, and for several centuries the
Austrian Kaisers have tried to make them into
a nation—sometimes by diplomacy, brute force,
and persecution ; sometimes by granting liberal
political institutions, when they couldn’t well
help it, and trying to promote education, and
endeavoring to rub off the sharp angles of racial
hatreds. The unwieldy empire always seemed
to totter on the verge of a precipice. For a
century or more the real Austria was a strag-
gling fly-speck on the map, holding down
Bohemia with one hand and trying to hold
down Hungary with the other, and fighting for
its life against the stupendous power of Turkey
at the same time. It was twice all but wiped
off the map of Europe by the Ottomans, and
each time it came to life again, and after
standing the whole brunt of the great Ottoman
invasion for 200 years, it finally sat down hard
on its enemy and got him into a better frame
of mind. For very many years it lived in a
state of periodical civil war; the revolt of
Bohemia was only a shade less regular than
the uprising of Hungary, and the uprising of
Hungary was only a shade less regular than the
sunrise, and the yearning of Hungary and Tran-
sylvania to tear each other to pieces was the next
most reliable thing to the chronic struggle for the
possession of Servia and the chronic row about
the Bavarian frontier. Even now, the motley
empire seems as far from being consolidated as
ever it was. It has apparently settled down
to argue out once more the old soul-filling row
between the great German and non-German
elements, and, if the latter comes on top, its
many component parts will then have to argue
things out among themselves. The general
idea is that something will burst when Kaiser
Francis Joseph goes to sleep with his troubled
fathers, and Kaiser Francis Joseph is very
old now, and is the head of a mentally-deranged
family, which badly needs to marry its cook,
and bring new blood into the veins of the
debilitated Caesars. The Austrian empire, if
it once bursts, has a fair show of being the
biggest problem Europe has had on hand for
two centuries.
The Appointment of Justices.
The proper mode of appointing justices of the
peace, now that the present political back-door
system stands condemned, is by no means clear.
Broadly speaking, there are two courses open—
a nominee system and an elective system. The
present is a nominee system, but is susceptible
of much improvement without the necessity of
adopting the other. This may be done either
by restricting the nominating class, or by pre-
scribing qualifications for the nominated per-
sons. Various proposals have been made in
these two directions. It has been suggested,
for instance, that the power of nomination
should be confined to Supreme Court Judges, or
to the Attorney-General, or Minister of Justice.
The objections of want of knowledge of local
requirements, and of a tendency to select people
of “society” standing, may be urged against
the one proposal, and the stronger objection
that it is what the present system is in theory,
against the other. As to the idea of qualified
nominees, there are no great difficulties. What
test should be applied to ascertain the
qualification? Who would apply it ? What is
a qualification ? The last difficulty raises the
old question as to whether justices of the peace
should be learned in the law, or merely
possessed of average common-sense and intel-
ligence, and should be honest. The great
danger in the “ qualified nominee” proposal is
that “social standing” would gain the day,
and that, therefore, boodledom and its variants
would acquire a secure tenure of the justice-
ship of the peace. The elective system has
much to recommend it theoretically and as a
democratic proposition. But there are serious
practical objections against it. The people are
unused to the idea of elected dispensers of jus-
tice ; there are dangers of corruption to ensure
re-election or to reward services on polling-
day ; and incompetent money-bags will have
undue chances of election. But probably most
of these objections would vanish with a magis-
tracy paid for its services. Unfortunately, every
Treasurer would see in this proposal an insuper-
able objection. Justicemustcontinuecheap,even
though it have to continue nasty. So many ob-
jections may be urged against every proposal
that it is hard to come to a conclusion : but in
the end it will probably be found that a choice
will have to be made between the nominee pro-
fessionally-educated justice, paid or unpaid,
and the elective paid justice. Higgins’ oues-
tion at the Vic. Law Commission, the other
day, w-as significantly indicative of what that
Commission will probably recommend. It was :
“ Do you see any reason why the maintenance
of justice should be left to amateurs and volun-
teers, any more than the maintenance of rail-
ways or waterworks ? ”
Rough on Drink.
A N.S.W. public servant was charged last
week with embezzlement upon his own confes-
sion, and made the usual statement that “the
whole thing had been brought on by Drink.”
The teetotal party alw'ays does a shriek of joy
over this regular and rather monotonous “ con-
fession ” ; almost everybody who does anything
nowadays says—when he is found out and has
to say something—that it was all the result of
Drink ; and The Bulletin, being a trifle weary
of that shriek, desires to say something on the
other side of the case. In the first place, the
first instinct of anybody who has done any-
thing illegal is to say that it was done by
somebody else. Even Adam of Genesis said
that the other fellow did it. When that falls
through, about the only other thing to say is
that it was all caused by the Der-rink. In
former days, the same kind of offender used to
say he was bewitched ; the only reason why
he doesn’t say that now is that no
modern jury believes in witchcraft. In
still earlier days it was the custom to
say that the devil called on him personally
with his visible tail hanging down behind him,
and drove him to crime, and he would say that
still if there was any reasonable chance of the
court believing in the personal visit of the
devil. If scientific knowledge were more gene-
ral than it is, he would probably tell the arrest-
ing constable when the attempt to lay the
crime at the door of the other fellow had failed
7 that he was to all intents and purposes as
innocent as the new laid snowball, and that the
crime was caused by cocaine, or ether, or mor-
phia, or hypnotic suggestion by some person or
persons unknown, or by the interference of
astral bodies or elementary spirits, or it arose
through male hysteria, spinal meningitis, gene-
ral paralysis of the insane in its early stages,
heredity, temporary mental derangement arising
from the fumes of green coffee, or something
else equally profound. But, as the witch and
the devil have gone, and the other things
haven’t yet fairly arrived, the Drink is, for the
moment, the oidy thing that a man can ascribe
his crime to, when the attempt to shift it on to
someone else has failed. The jury wouldn’t
understand the other things, and the arrest-
ing constable couldn’t spell them when he put
down tiie remark on his note-book, and the
accused himself probably never heard of them.
Moreover, by putting it down to the Drink the
accused individual enlists the sympathy of the
large teetotal section of the community, and
there is a fair chance that the teetotal part of
the jury will insist on a strong recommenda-
tion to mercy—whereas the cocaine or hysteria
theories appeal to no large community of
cranks, and pay no dividends worth mention-
ing. And, though the accused may have lied
in the first place by saying he didn’t commit the
crime, and in the second place by saying the
other fellow did it, when he makes his third
excuse to the effect that it was all caused by
Drink, the teetotal community always regards
t lat third shuffle as a matter which requires no
proof whatever. However great a liar the
arrestee may be in other matters, his absolute
truthfulness, when he puts his offence down to
Drink, is always accepted. Which is pretty
rough on Drink.
Judge Hood, in addressing a Melb. jury in a
recent seduction case: “In assessing damages
they would also bear in mind that the parties
were not in very high-class society. A pound
or two to a person in humble circumstances was
of more importance than £lO to a person in a
high social position. In dealing with wealthy
people the jury would give heavier damages.”
And again : “ They would consider, in this
case, what they would give the mother as com-
pensation for her injured feelings and the dis-
grace to her family.” It is satisfactory thus to
have an authoritative declaration from the
Supreme Court bench that the feelings of a poor
woman are not worth so much as those of a
“ person in high society.” Also, the honor of a
daughter of wealthy parents is worth more
than that of a poor girl, which explains why a
poor man who steals a few shillings gets 12
months, while the “ person in high society,”
who swindles bank-depositors, is discharged in
great haste. If it had been the defendant, in-
stead of the plaintiff and her family, who was in a
humble position, an order to pay £1 might be a
heavier punishment in his case than the loss of
£lO in someone else’s. That would at least
have been a logical statement, and on that
basis the poor man who seduced the rich man’s
daughter might, with some show of fairness,
get off more cheaply than the rich man who
seduced the daughter of humble parents. But
the theory that the rich man should get off
more cheaply if he seduced the daughter of a
plasterer than if he led astray the daughter of
a banker, simply on the ground that a plas-
terer’s daughter doesn’t matter much, is an
undisguised acknowledgment that there is one
law for the rich and another for the poor

Mr. Jno. M. Creed, M. L. C., writes :
Ed. Bulletin, —I have carefully considered the'case of
Suffield, now undergoing imprisonment on conviction
for a criminal assault upon Anne Chambers as set forth
in the pamphlet issued from the office of Tiie Bulletin
newspaper. *
. the essential point in the case against the prisoner
is his identification by the prosecutrix alone, uncor-
roborated by any other witness, and contradicted by
the evidence of Mrs. Warren and her daughter.
But for the evidence of Anne Chambers herself there is
nothing to connect the prisoner with the case, and her
statements in relation to the assault vary so materially as
to render it, in my opinion, quite unreliable.
There is no evidence other than that of the prosecutrix
to show that the inquiries disclosed by the medical exam-
ination were caused by the assault of any human being,
whilst the very similar bruises and abrasions found by
Dr. Nash on the person of Mrs. Cole, which were unques-
tionably caused by the bull, which was loose in the
grounds at the time Anne Chambers was injured, render
it within the range of probability that her injuries were
caused by the same animal.
In my opinion, there was not evidence of a sufficiently
reliable character to justify conviction.
In a civil action the verdict of a jury is frequently set
aside on appeal to the Full Court on the ground that it
was not supported by evidence, and the enquiry authorised
by section 383 of the Criminal Law Amendment Act
should in criminal cases take the place of the Full Court
in Civil ones.
It is an anomaly of the most objectionable character
that there should be power to upset the verdict in a case
relating to money or property, but not when the liberty
of a possibly innocent man is involved.
I can see nothing in the section which, in my opinion,
justifies a demand for new evidence or facts before such
an enquiry is directed.
I fully realise that the administration of justice should
not be lightly interfered wfith, but cases may arise in
which review of a verdict is necessary in the public well-
being, and it appears to me that the Suffield case is
one of them.—Jjto. M. Creed. Sydney, Dec. 17, 1897.
Kleptomania in well-to-do circles is chronic
theft among the working classes, and gets
punished accordingly. The Argus, in a recent
paragraph, artlessly tells the story of a klepto-
maniac who is evidently doomed to become a
“ habitual criminal.” Some extracts from this
quite unconscious satire on the law. “There
is a prisoner in the Melb. gaol who some months
ago was sent to serve a sentence for theft—a
nice long sentence, evidently. Many charges
were brought against him, which demonstrated
that the man was a lorn thief. . . . Since
li is incarceration he has continued as big a
thief as ever. The other day, when called
before the governor, he stole a pencil
from one of the warders while Captain
Burrows was lecturing him about his con-
duct. . . . He has, while outside prison,
practised the art of theft to such an extent that,
whether he is being watched or not, he always
resorts to strategy to achieve his end.” In fact,
the unfortunate devil whose brain-disease has
brought him to Melbourne gaol is evidently as
expert a kleptomaniac as the late Earl of
Derry and many other high-born thieves—
privileged to practise their art without the
least fear of ever getting inside a prison.
The Seddon Government’s Masters and Appren-
tices Bill, just thrown out by the Legislative
Council, was a praiseworthy attempt to deal
with a really serious evil. In Maoriland, as in
Australia, the apprentice system has almost
died out. In former days masters took appren-
tices for a fixed term of years, at wages which
increased with each year, and with a definite
obligation to thoroughly instruct them in a
trade. The result was that skilled workmen
grew up who knew their trades in every branch.
The newer idea is to engage boys at small
wages for no definite period. There is no obli-
gation to teach them anything, and, as a rule,
they only learn one small branch of the busi-
ness, and even that they learn just sufficiently
to make them useful to their employer for the
moment. In a year or so, when they want
more wages, they are fired out to make room
for other small boys at next to nothing
per week, or often at nothing at all—-merely
a promise of something per week in their
“ second year.” In such cases they are very
seldom kept on after the first year. The re-
sult is that the new local generation seldom
really knows any trade ; in most factories
where skilled labor is required it comes in its
grown-up state from Europe—just enough of it
to boss the temporary Maoriland small boy,
whose fate is to be a Touseabout all the days of
his life. The Seddon Government proposed to
meet this evil by absolutely prohibiting the
employment of the small boy at no wages, and
compelling masters to take apprentices instead.
In other words, it proposed to provide technical
instruction for the rising generation, and at the
same time to revert to a fine, ancient, respectable
system. But the Council called the ancient
respectable system a “dangerous socialistic ex-
periment,” and bounced the fine old precedent
down the stairs by a large majority.
An item of evidence in a recent Melbourne suit
is calculated to make the public get away by
itself in a dark corner and shudder. A doctor
was being sued for £5OOO damages for the
alleged unskilful botching of a patient, and the
usual professional brother got into the witness-
box to swear on behalf of defendant. Under
cross-examination this brother acknowledged
that there is a standing arrangement among
doctors to give evidence on each other’s behalf
when necessary. He didn’t say exactly how
hard they agreed to swear on each other’s
account, but the view that is likely to occur to
the public is this : If the profession is only
going to swear the truth and not a hair’s-
breadth more than the truth on behalf of the
sawbones who is in difficulty, then there is no
apparent need of any arrangement. Every
witness undertakes to tell the truth, and it
doesn’t require arranging. Then what does the
profession arrange ? As a rule there are only
two things about evidence that really need to be
arranged—one is to suppress the truth more or
less, and the other is to tell the truth as in-
accurately as circumstances may call for. Sup-
posing a doctor, by hideously unskilful treat-
ment, permanently disables a brick - laying
patient, and the latter wants damages. Appa-
rently the arrangement implies that the pro-
fession will either actively swear in favor of the
doctor, or if the case is too awful for that, will
at least swear as little as it possibly can on
behalf of the bricklayer. And if these are the
conditions, what show has any bricklayer ?
That alleged arrangement in the medical pro-
fession seems, in the interests of justice, to call
for a whole lot of investigation.
The five points of the People’s Charter fought
for by Englishmen when Australia was a penal
colony, were universal suffrage, vote by ballot,
equal electoral districts, triennial Parliaments,
and payment of members. The Chartists meant
by “ universal” adult male suffrage—and they’re
still far from it. Their Parliament also runs
seven years and payment of members is still in
the distance. Equal electoral districts in the
beneficial Chartist sense is not understood, nor
likely to be for some time. Australia, on the
other hand, can be fairly said, in one portion
or another of its territory, to have won the
whole five points. On universal suffrage it, in
some divisions, has “exceeded the wildest
dreams ” of Chartism, for it has given women
votes. And still how much remains to be done.
The five Chartist points are mere portions of
the machine of Government; skill and honesty
are wanted to work the machine.
Not very wise in their generation are the
people who have just hatched-out a fearful and
wonderful scheme for making the gay Gallic
bachelor give hostages to fortune, whether he
likes it or not, by privileging the more prolific
at the expense of the less. Government-licenses
only to fathers of at least three children ;
Government-scholarships only to children of
such fathers; Government posts only to fathers
of more than three children. All which penny-
iu-the-slot business is moonshine, for the simple
reason that if people won’t populate they
won t —and the more they won’t, the bigger
majority there will be of childless and sinall-
familied men to decide what the law shall
be. Moreover, it doesn’t seem as if this
French-Population-Increase-Alliance knows al-
together on which side the national bread is
buttered, else why should it so despondently
compare the French annual population-increase
of 50,000 with the 500,000 of G ermany. What’s
Germany going to do with her swarms, unless
she parts with them for the benefit of newer
nations, or cuts her own throat by adding her
increase to the monstrous military burden
which is already straining to the utmost her
industrial back ? And, as France isn’t going
backwards at all events, she might very well
cease from troubling and let the revanche slide.
She had her own day of invasion and glory and
arrogance, and the 1870 war was, after all, only
a German revanche. So that honors are easy.
Now that the minimum affirmative vote re-
quired to adopt Federation in N.S. W. has been
raised to 80,000, will the Reid Government give
the cause of Australian nationhood at least
some kind of a show by .taking the Federation
vote and the General Election on the same day ?
If the Melbourne Convention prepares a consti-
tution it will be ready to be voted upon by
March or April. The Assembly must dissolve
early in August in any case—probably it will
have to dissolve before the end of June so as to
prevent the Government going to the country
with another deficit. The two votes could
easily be taken simultaneously. It would save
a large sum in expenses, and it should make it
comparatively easy to get 80,000 votes, or even
more, on an abstract question, where otherwise
the odds are very much against anything like
that number being obtained. The Federal
party in Parliament, if there is one, should try
the effect of a real hard kick on this issue.
The S.M. Herald, in the unusual role of law-
reformer, remarks that the sole qualification
for a juror should be his elector’s right. The
idea is not new. The franchise is the test of
jury-qualification in several parts of Europe.
Many of the evils attributed to the jury system
of Britain and Australia spring from the cur-
rent notion as to what constitutes the qualifi-
cation for the office of juror. It is not
maturity, judgment, or learning that is
sought; the sheriff (or the police) draws up the
list on a strict property basis. This actually
vitiates the primary idea of a jury of one’s
peers. The “ twelve good men and true ”
should be the average men met on the street,
and the only way to make sure of getting this
principle into operation is to regard citizenship
as the sole qualification. A jury picked on any
other qualification is virtually a sheriff or
police-made body.
♦
Editor Buzacott, M.L.C. (Q.), is quite right
in protesting against the present absurd method
of amending clauses in Bills, by moving little
changes one by one till everj'one is hopelessly
bewildered. “Mr. President, I-beg to move
that, in the above sentence, the word ( right ’
in line 2be struck out.” That being done, an-
other motion is made to insert “ wrong,” or
“ astray,” or anything. Next, somebody bobs
up to insert an “and” or an “or” or a “ but ”
somewhere or other, and, if something has been
missed in line 4 and the Committee has dealt
with an “ if ” in line 5, line 4 can’t be gone back
to until the whole bill has been right through
Committee. Buzacott tells Row, in his days
(which was about the time wlien “ the Assyrian
came down like a wolf on the fold ”), if they
wanted to amend a clause, they simply threw
the thing into the w.p. basket and produced a
whole new clause, ready-made, just as a con-
juror produces a pumpkin from your left ear
and you wonder how you never knew it was
there. Buzacott’s plan is the plan. Yet none
of the Australian Parliaments adopts it.
The Suffield Case.
PRESS COMMENTS.
Says Albury (N.S.VV.) Daily News of Nov. 29:
‘ Having- read the whole of the evidence and the reports
of the manner of the trial, we have come to the conclusion
that this case embodies one of the most cruel and mon-
strous miscarriages of justice ever perpetrated in any
part of the British dominions where trial by jury is in
force. . . . And the only possible explanation is that here
again the late Judge Windeyer brought his dominating
mind into force and reduced the jury to that mental con-
dition of acquiescence in his callous will which was
responsible for so many brutal travesties of justice in this
colony.”
Writes “ Saladin ” in Orange (N.S.W.) Leader of Dec.
4 : “ A calm, dispassionate consideration of the evidence
would lead any ordinary person to conclude that the case
against Suffield was very weak indeed.”
The Beacon : “ The case is emphatically one for en-
quiry.”
The North Rockhampton (Q.) Times, in a leading article
on Dec. 4, says : “ We have read the pamphlet from its
commencement to the end, and rise from its perusal with
the thorough conviction that the man who is suffering
penal servitude was no more guilty of committing the
offence than the writer of these lines." And the w l iter
adds : “ The case under review is by no means the only-
instance of the miscarriage of Justice in N.S. Wales. In
the chaplain’s book in Maitland Gaol there was, and pre-
sumably now is, a quaint but significant entry : ‘ G. K.
Rusden, to take leave of Roger, an aboriginal - , who has
been pardoned for the crime it was proved he had never
committed.’” The writer goes on to relate how some
years ago he was chaplain of Maitland Gaol. “At one of
the semi-annual Assizes a Chinaman named Ah Veil was
convicted of the murder of a countryman at Port Stephens
and sentenced to be hanged. The gallows was erected!
the hangman had arrived from Sydney, and the execution
was fixed to take place on the following morning. On the
previous evening something occurred which raised grave
doubts in the chaplain’s mind as to the guilt of the
prisoner. He wired to Mr. Charles Cowper, then Colonial
Secretary, and asked for a respite of three days. A
favorable answ-er was returned. Next morning, accom-
panied by two sergeants of police and the interpreter, Go
Pong, the chaplain was in the saddle by daylight, and
after a dreadful ride —for flood waters were out—the place
was reached, and a searching investigation held, with the
result that the unfortunate wretch was found to be the
victim of a conspiracy, and no more guilty of the crime
for which he was convicted and about to be hanged than
anyone who reads these lines. The man is believed to be
alive to this day. It is worthy of note that a then bar-
rister and afterwards a Judge of New South Wales, i n
commenting on the circumstance, observed: 4 Well ’you
had a frightful journey; you ruined a valuable horse;
and for what l Only a poor devil of a Chinaman.’ ”
Yarrawonga (N.S.W.) Chronicle writes on Dec. 7
“ T^ e evidence . . . impresses us with the belief that
there has been a gross miscarriage of justice . . . that
there should be an enquiry is obviously beyond question.”
Sydney Stock and Station Journal, Nov. 30—“ The
circumstances look like, in the first place, a gross miscar-
riage of justice ; and, in the second place, a defiant, pig-
headed refusal of the authorities to re-open the case.”
Sydney Suburban Times, in an article on 4th Dec., 1897,
says “ Perusing the evidence dispassionately, one is
forced to admit that it is so inconclusive that the re-
luctance of the Crown to have further investigation
seems inexplicable.”
Clunes (Vic.) Guardian, in leading article, Dec. 14 -
“The man was tried, convicted, sentenced, when all (he
testimony goes to show that he was not guilty of the
crime imputed to him . . . The evidence, however, col-
lated in proof that Suffield is not guilty, is so conclusive
as to warrant the belief, very strongly expressed in Syd-
ney, that the Govt, dare not reopen the case in fear that
the. proof of their inactivity in the true interests of
justice should be unmistakeably exposed.”
Bundaberg Leader., Dec. B—“ Taking the evidence as
published in this pamphlet it is clear that Suffield was
sent to gaol on the bare assertion of an apparently hvs
terical female who, even in her own statements concern'
mg the matter, contradicts herself repeatedlv n
will appear to everyone reading this ease that'justice his
miscarried.’ J nas
