Harmston’s Circus.
One of the largest and hottest audiences
that have ever filled a theatre in a perspiring
city graced the opening of Her Majesty’s Panto-
mime on Boxing Night. The stories of “ The
Babes in the Wood ” and “ Bold Robin Hood and
his Merry Men ” have been inextricably mixed by
the man who wrote the “book” for the enter-
prising firm, and through three long acts of
spangled splendor and a scintillating wind-up,
and tangled twin tales meander behind the foot-
lights. All the title rdles are Well played, espe-
cially the Wood, which had a great reception on
its first appearance, and the business of the even-
ing includes a flitterjr-twittery flutter of robin
red breasts and a practical waterfall. The Babes,
represented by Lily Titheradge and Carrie Moore,
are well-grown infants whose principal act con-
sists in not being murdered in the aforesaid Wood.
Miss Carrie, as the boy babe, has nothing to do ;
and Miss Lily, as the girl, still less. Talent
must be plentiful when two such clever girls
are wasted on these tiny parts. The cruel
Uncle, who is also the Sheriff, is a joyful inebriate
consistently and cheerfully' drunk all the time.
Mr. Bert Gilbert, who makes his first appearance
in Sydney in the part, shows a fine artistic sense
in getting to the exact state of muddle likely to
be kept tip through a whole lifetime, for Uncle
looks as if he would never be any drunker than
just that and yet never be quite sober. Also, he
sings in the Wood and quarrels noisily with the
orchestra, and brings on a melancholy dog, and
sools him on to the conductor, and takes strong
convulsions on the stage so that his coat is rent in
twain and turned front-side back, and then he
abruptly flays his dog and leaves Snatcher’s bare
bones bleaching in view of the audience. He also
hires two bravos to slay his relatives, and inserts
both legs into one trouser, and is wheeled home in
a barrow to his ancestral door-way by a strong
bobby, and sleeps in the Wood, and does
a variety turn, with Robin Hood, and takes
part in many siqg-songs, and yet never gets
any drunker and never gets any soberer.
* * * •
Ada Reeve looks good enough to eat as
Robin Hood, and, as a first-night tribute, the con-
tents of so many florists’ shops were handed up
that they had to be lined in front of her along the
stage. Her principal business is singing, with a
shapely band of Merry Men as chorus, and her
figure as a boy is a triumphant success. Alice
Leamar is her sweetheart, Maid Marian, and has
nothing much to do but stand about coyly and
be kissed whenever the interest is flagging. De
Lara, as Dame Durden, makes the Babes’ nurse
an angular Anglican old maid with bloomers and
a bike ; and J. J. Burke and Coleman are two
original villains of the deepest and dustiest hue ;
they look like Dusty Rhodes and Weary Walker,
and have all the heavy tragedy to do in the Wood,
where they have a fight to a finish with gloves on,
and then grope round on their hands and
knees, apparently seeking the thread of the
narrative, which by that time is lost altogether.
They have the best duet in the panto., and a well-
worn practical jokelet, which also is worked up in
the Royal show, of black-chalking each other’s
shirt-front. But they are refreshingly villainous
villains, and the life and soul of the piece. The
wedding-breakfast scene is the poetry of feeding ;
the animated meats and drinks and table-fix-
ings form a gorgeously - swagger bean - feast,
and the other ballets are soul-satisfying so far as
they go, but there is too little dancing and not
one topical allusion. A Xmas panto, ignoring
Federation, and never once referring to the
coming elections, or the Bennett divorce-case,
or the John Norton libel-suit, has an unfinished
aspect. There is one concerted ditty that gives
signs, with careful nourishing and dieting, of
growing into a topical song, but it is very anaemic
and feeble as yet. L. Caron has made some
pretty birdy music for the robins to hop to. There
is plenty of go in the Firm’s effort this year, and
the comedians are bright and clever. With one
or two rattling good songs and a dancing troup
inserted the show would be one vast, solid success.
** * m
“ Charley’s Aunt ” rose again at Sydney
Criterion on Monday night. There was a heavy
sea of humanity breaking over the ticket-office,
and it blew a fierce gale of population on the
stairs, and manager Lohr, in a dismasted condi-
tion and laboring heavily, was visible for a while
trying to direct things, and then foundered in
deep vvater. Apparently the “ Charley’s Aunt ”
boom is a long way from extinction even now.
Frank Thornton comes back the same crinkly
little joy as aforetime, and his two brief
twinklesome comedy legs age not at all. And
as Thornton is, barring the beery Stephen
Spettigue with his hiccup and his dropsical
umbrella, the whole comedy-element of the play,
the fact that he is still in good repair is a sufficient
guarantee of success. The “ Aunt ” can never, as
a work of comedy-art, be quite the triumph that
the “ Private Secretary ” was ; that sad and bun-
struck teacher is a creation such as only comes in
a comedian’s way once in a lifetime; but still the
lady from Brazil is a vast hunk of mirth and glad-
ness, and her struggles to find her rear pocket
are a whole comedy in themselves. A more
detailed notice of Thornton’s deserving show and
the company is regretfully held over till next
week. This devastating red organ has, for various
reasons, to go to press on Tuesday night. And
when Xmas falls on Saturday, and more new
shows than the paper can hold open on Monday
night, and they are all so crowded that sudden
death waits on the stair, the situation is difficult.
Therefore, it is merely necessary to mention that
the co. in general gives satisfaction, and the parts
of it that are more satisfactory than some other
parts will be particularised in a future issue. Also
any parts which are not distinctly satisfactory
thus get a chance to improve, and the chastening
axe of criticism is suspended for one week.
* # mx
It was a very hot and abundant audience
that took a violently perspiring interest, on Mon-
day night, in the new and amended version of the
history of Richard Whittington, Esquire, and his
alleged Oat, as presented at Sydney Lyceum. In
fact, said Cat, of the dimensions and color of an
elderly leopard in extreme disrepair, pervades the
piece so autocratically that even Harry Shine —as
a Dame Durden of much tonnage and a mani-
fest determination to boss the entire show —is
fain to take a second place of the most second-
ary kind. Despite the overshadowing char-
acter of the animal, however, Shine gets a
fair show to assert himself as a dancer and
takes the bun from all competitors—panted or
petticoated. The dancing throughout is so good
that it makes the musical side of the entertain-
ment look comparatively one-horse and apologetic.
A special exception is made with regard to the
chanty ” voiced by about the most silver-piped
and apple-bowed and liberally-pooped ship’s com-
pany that never bunked for’ard with the ’roaches.
The said company coolly—contrary to all nautical
statutes in that case made and provided—retires
into the saloon of its good ship, the “old man”
being presumably just then perched upon the
mizzen-royal yard with a revolver trying to hold
his own against an aggravated mutiny.
* • * *
‘ 4 Dick Whittington ” and his tremendous
Cat go well throughout, so far as can be judged
amid the storm and stress of an opening night.
When the waits get a little shorter and the Cat
shakes down somewhat into its new surroundings,
the show will probably go still better. It is long,
but not lean ; the staging is reasonably gorgeous ;
and the “ book ” basis of the agony doesn’t too
urgently cry out for the prompt assassination of
the perpetrator thereof.
** * *
The Rickardspantomirneatthe Royal began
its earthly career on the 23rd Dec., and is therefore,
at date of publication, a flourishing infant of a
week old, and doing as well as can be expected.
By getting four days’ clear start of both the Pitt-
street shovvs, it was enabled to peg out as its own
special claim several dear old familiar chestnuts,
which the first-night audience greeted with cheers
—for auld lang syne. This panto., which is billed
as “ Jack the Giant-Killer,” and keeps as near the
title as any reasonable Xmas production is ex-
pected to do, will be remembered, among a host of
others, by the dazzle and glitter of its dresses and
by the most realistic stage-giant on record. Grim
Gobbler is a large and hairy Titan, with the newly-
arrived Story Gofton concealed somewhere in
his works. He is slaughtered by Jack (Addie
Conyers) some time during the evening, but at
11.30 on opening night G.G. was still alive and
full of original sin. The original Jack, by the
way, came to grief with a sprained ankle very
early in the season, and the understudy, Miss Edie
Haytor, butchers the giant pending Miss Conyers’
recovery.
* * • *
Lottie Moore, as Jack’s sweetheart, is as
nice as pie, particularly in the final display where
all the characters come on in their very best
clothes and do a turn at the Court of King Arthur.
That early aboriginal British sovereign, in the
person of A. Bellman, is as heavy as a slab of
cold Xmas pudding, except for two brilliant
flashes ; one where he plays a unique card game
with Merlin and co., and another where he
and George Lauri coruscate in a bur-
lesque of Napoleon and Josephine. The
Huline Brothers, with bells on their fingers
and toes and capes, ring tunes all over them-
selves, and an exquisitely dressed ballet also
jingles like a glorified ice-cream cart. The four
specially - imported dancing girls are graceful
skirt artists with one sensational exit which alone
is worth the admission money. T. Woottwell,
the Haytors, Ivy Scott, and Marietta Nash are
all in the cast and seem to want elbow-room. Alice
Simmons warbles effectively as the Fairy Queen.
Some of the jokes are of the same tonnage as
Governor Phillip’s monument, but, on the whole,
the panto, is a dazzle of loveliness, and it’s a
wonder how the management does it at the price.
The Bulletin has nothing but praise to bestow
on Story Gofton’s Giant; but even if it had, it
would smother its feelings. This paper must
respectfully decline to criticise adversely any
gentleman over Bft. high carrying an ironclad
telegraph-pole as a walking-stick.
** * *
Though the bulk of Rickards’ stars are
away pantomiming at the Royal, that resourceful
manager has enough left to keep the Tivoli pro-
gramme well up to the mark. The eight Kellinos
arrived just in time to fill the gap, and they filled
it to the apparent satisfaction of everybody. The
Royal Standard co., byway of an unusual effort
of originality, staged “Uncle Tom’s Cabin” for
the holiday season, and a few small casual shows
are raging temporarily in various parts of Sydney,
and calling loudly for no particular notice.
* *■ sie *
The Fitzgerald’s Circus, at Redfern (Syd-
ney), is so far doing good business, and it deserves
all the luck that has come in its way. It is a very
large and honest circus with an immensity of
horse in it, and though the present writer, in his
own private capacity, absolutely loathes horse, he
fully recognises the fact that for a community
which likes the kind of horse that the Fitz-
geralds put on that kind of horse is just the kind
of horse that such a community would like. The
programme is a very long one, and all the items
are good—some of them excellent. All the people
who undertake to turn somersaults over an alarm-
ing quantity «f horse do it neatly and cleverly,
and the equestrians perform some remarkable
feats. John Stewart, who does a surprising
balancing act on a tottery pile of chairs, and
La Belie Rose, the nice-looking female who travels
uphill and down on a revolving globe, are dis-
tinctly above the average, and the performing lion
and elephant are two beasts who deserve ail the
moral support this paper can give them. The
latter animal, when he gets on his tricycle and
Bilmartins at a good pace round the enclosure
with the lion on his back, may be a trifle
laborious, but his conscientious devotion to busi-
ness is most praiseworthy. There are sundry
dazzling girls in the eo., and much spangled cos-
tume, and altogether it is about as much in the
way of a circus as anyone could ask for.
** * *
Of two pantomimes that came to pass in
Melbourne, on Monday night, the smaller one at
the Bijou was the less inconvenienced by the heat.
“ Robinson Crusoe ” is a mere extravaganza with
very little meat on its bones, and a very great deal
of bones. Five chief performers, Martin Hagan,
Lucy Fraser, Willie and Amy Gourlay, arid John
Forde supply 90 per cent, of everything worth
mentioning. The balance, which is made up of
scenery, legs, and limelight, deserves a certain
amount of praise, but not nearly so much as these
five industrious and capable grafters. The result is
not much of a pantomime, anyhow. Perhaps it
would have been wiser to bill “ Crusoe ” as a bur-
lesque and leave it to battle fora living on its bare
merits.
** * *
“Dick Whittington,” at the Melbourne
Royal, presented a first appearance of being likely
to settle down into a satisfactory pantomime
before the end of the week. It lasted till mid-
night, and the audience was beginning to melt off
the premises when some of the best items fell due.
Earlier in the proceedings a flower-pot ballet
had given the crowded house at least one
large spectacle to swear by. With a lot of book
material to work upon and a flock of disciplined
infants at command, the stage management should
make “Dick Whittington” go for several weeks.
Unfortunately, Maggie Moore on Monday was
nearly reduced to a standstill by persistent re-
hearsal and a violent attack of weather Her
spiritless performance in the name-part must have
hampered the good intentions of Roberts and
several other people who will steadily improve
their opportunities as time goes on.
** * *
Thus far, business at Melb. Princess’s
suggests that Wilson Barrett is being taken seri-
ously by the female mind. “ Women don’t
reason, they only feel,” and they dearly love to
feel sorry for a majestic hero in flesh-colored
tights. Therefore the doleful unreasonableness of
“Claudian” delights them, as a rule, whilst an
average man, sitting down to consider the cause
and effect of Holy Clement’s Curse (an article in
no way resembling somebody’s patent tonic), gets
oppressed by a sense of unbelief in the things
that harrow his best girl’s heart. Even a
churchwarden couldn’t find a decent excuse for
Clement’s Curse. It is a disgrace to Early Chris-
tianity, and a satire on the name of its holy
utterer. Also penitent Claudian, after 100 years
of devastating public life, is an impossible person,
for he talks to himself about his malign influence
as though it were quite a recent discovery, and
appears to be armed with the current emperor’s
authority to execute any minor potentate on the
spot. The picture of this wandering Pagan living
on and on, and still sticking to his billet under a
succession of Roman emperors whose power was
decaying all the time, is an unconvincing work of
dramatic art. As a highly-colored costume show,
with much blood and thunder and pretentious
dialogue to keep matters moving, “ Claudian ”
may be admired, but as a play, or a sermon, it
lacks the healthy vigor of Truth.
* * * *
The all-round acting of Wilson Barrett’s
company can’t avoid comparison with local
“ talent,” and the comparison is rough upon the
new arrivals—just at first. Presently, no doubt,
they will seem a fairly well-balanced crowd, well-
posted in their solemn duties. There are so many
of them who play dull parts in an experienced
manner that The Bulletin had better maintain
impartial silence concerning everybody save
Wilson Barrett and Miss Jeffries—until one or
other rises above the level of mediocrity. Touch-
ing the two principal performers, last week’s
remarks will keep fresh for another seven days.
** * *
At Melbourne Alexandra the new Dan
Barry season made a boomsome start with “ A
Gilded Sin,” a drama in four large acts and thir-
teen awful situations. “ A Gilded Sin ” contains
a good deal of old Hinglish Christmas in the first
place, after which the girl who was once pure as
the beautiful snow falls among gilded sinners and
destroying angels, and is sorely troubled by visions
of the past until circumstances bring the boss
murderer to bay. Dan Barry’s dramas never fail
to end happily when they get the blood-shedder
at bay. _ By way of a change, The Bulletin
would like to see the curtain drop on a villain who
was doing as well as ever, but the Alexandra
audiences probably wouldn’t roll up if the wages
of sin were beer and skittles, instead of sudden
death. They want Retribution—plenty of it—at
the Alexandra. Manager Dan. 8., as Dennis
O'Toole, covers considerable ground in “A Gilded
Sin,” and 30 other names decorate the programme.
None of them are quite so valuable for decorative
purposes as Lottie La Verne and Doris Ventry.
All the same, Rita Aslin and Tottie Laing read
very nicely.
** * *
Harmston’s Circus, having recovered from
a brief attack of stormy wind, flourishes exceed-
ingly. It is a bright, cheery show, suitable for
parents and guardians as well as the cynical
infants who sit around matinee performances at
half-price. S'mi - lady acrobats are calculated to
bring a warm glow of satisfaction to the baldest
head, inasmuch as they display their strength and
agility in a sentimental light, and smile sweetly
into vacancy—the smile of love’s young dream.
For an aged parent, with a glistening circus on
the top of his head, there is exceptional charm in
a flexible blonde who seems to be thinking of her
sailor lad far across the seas, whereas if she were
to beam upon a beastly Johnnie fellow in the ad-
joining chair, he (the hairless one) would feel his
age keenly. Ballet girls, addicted to smiling at
young Augustus in the stage-box, might make a
business note of this observation. Harmston’s
circus clowns are apt to do something funny once
in a way, although up to now the humorous
supremacy of the baby elephant remains unim-
paired.
The Christmas bill at the Opera House is
a strong muster of attractions, with Austin Rudd,
the Waldrons, and Frank Leon in the middle
distance, and the Hegelmanns to the fore. The
three Hegelmanns, if not actually the “Greatest
Gymnasts on the Face of the Earth,” are some-
thing very like ’em, but The Bulletin must study
the literature of gymnastics before coming to a
further opinion on the magnitude of their per-
formance.
At Poverty Point .
Cockaigne : “As Holy Clement, in ‘Claudian,’ Willard
got his first lift out of the rut of stage villainy, the despair
of every clever actor, from Irving to the Australian
Baker.”
H. : “ Wilson Barrett appears in Melb. without any
essential diminution of power. Time has not thinned his
flowing hair —much. His voice is finely resonant, his
diction manly, with only two slight faults. The first is a
tinge of the London affectation in pronouncing, which is
disliked even in the English provinces, and brings down
the sledge-hammer in America, where, indeed, the
superior classes do talk English. And he is apt to mis-
pronounce ‘ch ’ for the soft ‘ g,’ thus ‘ chem ’ for ‘ gem,’
‘chenius’for‘genius.’ But he takes splendidly—apart
from his piece.”
Shifter: “The books of the Melbourne Theatre Royal
show that the following subjects have been dealt with in

‘Arabian Nights,’ ‘Babes in the Wood,’ ‘Cinderella,’

Strong man Sandow, the other day, in San Francisco,
had a three-round contest with a 5301 b. lion, which, a
week before, had killed his keeper. In the first round,
Sandow got the beast round the throat with one arm and
round the middle with the other, hugged the wind out of
him pretty well, and then banged him down on the floor.
The second round was a repetition of the first. In the
third, the lion leaped on the man’s back, but, before he
could get his teeth to work, up went Sandow’s arms round
his neck, and he was pitched right over Sandow’s head,
and fell with a resounding thud "on the solid floor. Then
the lion gave in, and put his tail between his legs as a
sign that he had handed in his resignation.
Binoc : “ One part in which poor William Terriss was
never surpassed by anybody was that of Chateau Renaud
in the ‘ Corsican Brothers.’ ”
Holdar M. : “ ‘ Why does the comedian choose Sunday
for his burial-day?’ put in a previous issue, has a wider
application extending to the ‘perfesh.’ generally. The
mummer lives and dies for advt. and, though all his other
yearns gradually fade, this one is always fresh. The re-
mains will annex more ad. if planted on the Sawbath
when his brother pros, can attend in great swarms. And
consider the ‘ extry ’ impression conveyed by the cortfege
as it does a Sunday crawl in sight of the ‘common classes,’
who are more at home on the Day of Rust. Even William
Terriss carefully perished in time for Sunday burial.”
Charles Godfrey, said to be utterly broken down when he
left W.A., is going again as strong as ever at the London
’alls. He gives them “ Dreams,” a clever serio-trashy
song written for him in Australia, of which somebody
wisely remarks that “ only a genuine artist could treat it
with any hope of success.” Godfrey is a genuine artist,
but his art is a minor consideration when one thinks of
his marvellously tough throat.
Writes a London correspondent : —On a recent Friday
Madame Melba gave her final concert in England before
starting for her American tour. Brighton was the locale,
and the diva’s colleagues were Ada Crossley, Ben Davies,
Douglas Powell, and John Kruse. Melba, of course, got
a great ovation, and she looked superb in a wonderful
French frock with an array of magnificent diamonds. The
Gippslander also got plenty of applause, and sang so as
to deserve it. Early next morning Madame Melba started
for America accompanied by her sister, Miss Dora Mit-
chell, her secretary, two maids, a wonderful dog, and a
few tons of luggage.
Dear Bulletin,— l have just come on a waltz, having
for frontispiece a cow with large brand on near side, and
dedicated to the Scottish Australian Investment Co. It
is a grand idea, isn’t it—the Branded Cow Waltz? On the
off-chance that corporate bodies in difficulties pay for
having music dedicated to them, I am at present bringing
out “ The Fixed Deposit Galop,” dedicated to the Queens-
land National Bank.— Harry Monck.
“ Of Nellie Stewart the Chronicle complains that she
‘ too frequently suggests the first boy of pantomime or
burlesque;’ the solemn Standard say's she ‘appeared at
first to lack refinement ’ (our dainty Nellie !); the Globe,
‘ her vocal powers are hardly sufficient to justify the large
share of music assigned her ’; and the Daily Graphic
asserts her ‘singing and voice are not of a very high
class’; whilst the Pall Mall and St. James, in a broad
half-column notice, perform the marvellously malicious
feat of not even mentioning her name ! !! True,” says
The Bulletin’s correspondent, “ the original ‘ I was
dreaming ’ voice is, alas ! not there, and its substitute I
would rather forget, but in verve and vivacity it was the
same favorite Nellie much improved as an actress. Per-
haps she tried to do too much, was over-anxious after her
long rest, orin faceof the unusuallydemonstralive greeting
of a largely Australian audience she may have over-acted
her songs and part, but, whatever it was, at the curtain
most of us were as sad as wet hens when the chicks are
lost."

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Old C. H. .Tames, the hairy “father of the
Doom,’ has filed his long-expected schedule in Melbourne.
James s schedule has been anxiously waited for ever since
his bloated child burst a few years back. The main
trouble m his case was the Dominion Banking Co., in
which concern James owned 68,789 out of 70,000 shares.
When the Dominion Co. broke with a fearful odor of
ancient egg, James was requested by the liquidator to
pay £171,399 in calls, but he only smiled in his long beard
and paid no attention whatever. Other boomers went
own by dozens, and paid their lonesome fink or more in
the J, but James made no sign. The court rained orders
on him to file his schedule, and issued a warrant for his
arrest for contempt, but the tough old man simply stayed
over the border out of its jurisdiction. Now that the
i, 11 ”? l ,a,r * ;lr( 'i l has got tired of the joke and sent his
schedule along, there is nothing very surprising in it,
after all. It sets forth that liabilities are £851,000;
assets, £532,000 ; shortage, £319,000. The Commercial
an j* 'ty Melbourne banks and some huge private
creditors seem to have a lien on about everything,
so the Dominion creditors will probably crush old
James for only a color. The old man says indignantly
that one cause of his insolvency was the “ unnecessary ”
calling up of the capital by the liquidator of that bank,
so apparently the last of the boomers reckons that it was
a piece of sheer insolence on the part of the beastly
depositors to want their money and to keep asking what
James had done with it. Still, considering that the bank
kept calling at the top of its voice for some years and sjot
nothing, its action probably was unnecessary—in one
sense. It was quite unprofitable, anyhow.
• * » *
The National Bank of Tasmania got through
the half-year ended 30th November with a profit of £4127.
Previous half’s profits were £4012, and at 30th November,
1896, they were £3534. The usual 5 per cent, dividend
(£3991) is paid, leaving £136 to the good. There is nothing
special to report, except that the business looks all right,
and the cash-reserve is large and solid, and the National
seems to earn its dividend with a very small margin. The
institution is 12 years old ; it has a paid-up capital of
£152,040 and a reserve of £22,500, which hasn’t had any
additions made to it for a good many years, and it leads a
solid, respectable and blameless existence with apparently
no prospect of doing anything very large for many years
to come.
* • » *
Of the four purely Sydney banks, the shares of
the B.N.S.W. show a drop in the last 12 months from
£34 7s. 6d. to £34 ss. ; Commercial of Sydney from
£24 17s. 6d. to £22 12s. 6d. ; City of Sydney from £4 ss. to
£3 ; A.J.S. from £1 3s. to 3s. 9d. The restoration of con-
fidence seems further off than ever, somehow.
* * * •
One utterly foolish and utterly unaccountable
feature in the constitution of the S.A. State Land Bank is
the proviso that all money received byway of repayments
from borrowers must be used in paying off bonds. The
business is constantly getting larger, and the bank is con-
stantly selling bonds to raise the money for more loans,
yet, instead of lending out again the money that comes in
as interest and instalments of principal, it has to use
it for the compulsory repayment of bond-holders, who
don’t want their money back and who only swear when
they have to take it and skirmish round in search of new
investments. The result is that there is a lot of needless
expense and worry, and that the bonds don’t sell so well
as they otherwise would, simply because the public
doesn’t care for a security where it is liable to have its
interest stopped and its principal violently thrown back
at its head at any hour of the day or night.
* * * *
Tasmania is also thinking of starting a State
Land Bank in a very small way, begining with a capital
of £lOO,OOO. A State Land Bank with only £lOO,OOO
capital would be a microscopic affair even for Tasmania,
but, if the system was once introduced, it could, of course,
be enlarged indefinitely. But the Braddon-Fysh Ministry
totters along with a very small majority now, and no one
believes that it seriously wants the proposed Land Bank,
and it is doubtful if it could carry the measure through
the Assembly in any case, and the House of Slow Land-
lords would throw it out to a dead certainty, so no one is
much interested.
• » * •
B. D. Morehead, ex-director of the Queensland
National Bank, thus explained why he knew nothing of
the utter rottenness of the institution he was paid to
look after, even when its stench shrieked aloud all
through Australia : “ I did not consider that a director of
a bank or public company would be justified in going
behind a certificate of the accuracy of a balance-sheet,
unless he had grounds for suspecting collusion between
the auditors and officers of the institution ; and in this
case he had not the shadow of a suspicion.” But the
point which Morehead dodges is this : the Q.N. Bank
auditors didn’t for many years past, anyhow, certify to
the accuracy of the balance-sheet, so he had no certificate
of accuracy to go behind. And The Bulletin pointed
this out at intervals for years, and yelled the fact in
Morehead’s large, incapable ear, and he wouldn’t hear it.
Couldn’t Morehead see that the auditors only said every
time that the balance-sheet agreed with the books, and
always avoided saying whether the books were accurate
or not ?
* * * *
Sydney Telegraph keeps two separate sets of
financial opinions on hand. When the N.S.W. Govt,
floated its 3 per cent, loan in Sydney at £lOO it remarked
proudly that the credit of the Freetrade province was the
best in Australasia. When the Victorian Govt, went one
better by floating its 3 per cent, loan in Melbourne at
£lO3, it considered that Protection must have reduced
Melbourne to an awful state, and made the profitable
employment of money practically impossible when people
would accept such rates as these.
• * * •
Last year the Northern Territory budget
showed : Revenue, £70,710; expenditure, £143,251 ; de-
ficit, £72,541. In the way of white elephants, the Terri-
tory takes the Australian bun easily.
* ' * * ♦
The Intercolonial Investment, Land and Build-
ing Co. (Sydney) reckons its year’s profit to 31st October,
1897, at £5123. A 10 per cent, dividend (£3295) is paid :
£1650 is added to reserves, making them £10,950, and the
balance at credit of profit and loss is raised to £lB6l. The
Intercolonial always seems to struggle through in good
times and bad, and its 10 per oent. dividend is about as
regular as the sunrise. The fact that it keeps very clear
of the dangerous deposit business, and can’t be seriously
rushed in times of panic by scared people wanting their
money back on the instant, is probably one cause. Another
probable cause is that it doesn’t buy more property than
it can pay for with its own money.
* * * *
The Mutual Life Assurance Co. of New York
announces that in future it will base its estimates on the
assumption that the rate of interest to be realised on in-
vestments is 3J per cent, instead of 4 per cent., as hereto-
fore. Thismay mean rather smaller bonuses for a year or
two, but it is a wise provision, and implies a lot of addi-
tional security to policy-holders. Several Australasian
offices are also coming down to the 3£ per cent, rate, and
the others ought to do likewise.
* * * *
The Derwent and Tamar Fire and Marine In-
surance Co. (Hobart) has just finished its 59th year with a
profit on the 12 months’ work of £B4Bl, as compared
with £8175 last year. In view of the bill to be met over
the recent Melbourne fire (where the co. has £14,000 at
stake), the board wisely cuts down the dividend from the
usual 15 per cent, to 74 (£3750). The Derwent and Tamar
is a fine old concern with £50,000 paid up capital, £50,000
reserve, and £19,204 at credit of profit and loss ; and
though its business is very small for its age, it pans out
much better for its shareholders than some concerns
which hustle a great deal harder after premiums. Its
investments in particular, judging by the steadiness of
the interest returns, seem remarkably safe.
* * * •
Melbourne is still patiently cleaning up the
delris of the almost-forgotten boom. One of the most
hopeless wrecks of the crowd the Freehold Assets
Realisation Co., which is really the old Freehold Invest-
ment and Banking Co., disguised and in liquidation—
reports the results of a year’s work. Properties to the
book-value of £76,982 were sold, and realised £12,422 ;
loss, £64,560. On this basis, it may be inferred that the
£1,063,817 (book-value) of properties, which form the
bulk of the assets, are really worth some £160,000. On
the other hand, the concern paid off £138,455 of its de-
posits during the year, the holders taking £29,105, or about
4s. Id. in the £, in full settlement, and being darned glad
to get it. The Freehold Assets Co. has a peculiar mode of
liquidation : when it sells £60,000 worth (nominal) of pro-
perty for £lO,OOO it spends it in buying back its own deposits
by Dutch auction—the depositor who will take the smallest
sum in the £ getting the preference. Most of them take
about 4s. The depositor who holds on to the last may get
20s. in the £—or he may get one-eighth of a penny; it is
an interesting gamble for the last depositor, and every-
thing depends on whether the assets or the deposits de-
preciate faster. At present, however, the co. owes
£1,135,000 to depositors, and has about .£’1,140,000 of
paper assets to pay them. with. And as the assets are
realising about 3s. 3d. in the £ on their nominal value,
and the depositors are being paid about 4s. in the £, the
natural inference is that unless things improve the last
depositor will be badly left.
* * • •
Some years ago, when banks were going bung
and many leaves in the book of revelations remained as
yet uncut, a certain Australian paper was in the habit of
submitting advance-proofs of damaging statements to a
certain boomster, and the boomster would promptly sup-
press the threatened article with a little cheque. At last
there came a time when the bleeding financier had no
more character to lose. He was tired of struggling to
keep things dark. A representative of the same old rag
came round with another sheet of damaging statements,
and asked in the same old insinuating voice, “ Will this
look well in our next issue ?’’ The boomster leaned back
in his chair and shrieked, “ Ye s, you blanker, it will look
blanky well,” and the editor’s ambassador slunk away,
muttering that the other fellow was “ evidently dead to
all sense of shame.”

The little crowd of Melb. Frenchmen who got
into deadly conflict with Louis Cayron re the sale of their
precious Westralian mine to a London company are now
fighting their late solicitor, G. H. Neighbour. They don’t
love their Neighbour any more. He is pursuing them for
payment of £998 costs, and they claim £2OOO damages for
his alleged negligence—in losing a case.
* * * *
An insane plumber, who escaped from Kew
Asylum, Melbourne, recently got to Ballarat and set up
as a mining expert and a representative of British capi-
tal. Nobody noticed that there was anything wrong
about him as a mining expert until he was recaptured ;
the glare in his eye was simply set down as the naturally
wild expression of a man who represented British capital.

“ Credit accorded here to respectable children
attending State school ” is now a familiar notice
posted up in many of the lolly-shops near some of the
suburban State schools around Melb.
* * » •
A rare chance to flood foreign philatelists with
“ scarce issues” is afforded those people who had unus"g
penny Victorian stamps in their safes during the late big
Melb. fire. The writer saw a number of these stamps, the
brick-red oolor of which had been turned jet-black, whilst
the white portions of the stamps were unchanged in any
way. It is probable that the new printing ink used to
defeat chemical “treatment” contains some ingredient
that changes color on exposure to great heat.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin,—You were talking the other
day about record weights of pieces of opal, and quoting a
270 z. piece unearthed lately at White Cliffs as an exam-
ple. The winner of that piece has been fortunate enough
to find two larger pieces still, weighing respectively 43
and 330 z. I fancy those are the record. The opal con-
tained in these stones had very fine streaks of color, but
two-thirds of the stones were “ bastard stones” or
“ potch.”—Pom Pom, White Cliffs.
* • * *
Silver Hill, one of the shows boomed on the
strength of the Junction’s western development, has been
given over to the tributors. And some people, who still
try to think they haven’t ridiculously exaggerated the
value of the uncertain little show, wink knowingly and
whisper that it’s all a job.
• * * *
Broken Hill’s mineral output for the year was
valued at £1,755,155, as against £1,751,528 in 1896. The
principal falling-away is in bullion ; here are the figures
for the last four years : ’94, £1,543,038 ; ’95, £959,562 : ’96,
£788,530; ’97, £615,874. And, of course, the decline in
this item has lately been more pronounced than ever. In
the first quarter of 1897 the value of bullion exported was
£174,459 ; by the fourth quarter it had gone down to
£110,900. That fall is, however, more than counterbal-
anced by the increase in the export of crude and concen-
trated sulphide and other ores. In 1895 these materials
were or xport valued at £587,255 ; in 1897 at £1,028,363.
|* * * *
Rumors went round that the B.H. Junction
directors, sick of the mess in the mine resulting from the
creep which the manager reported was a special mark of
Providential favor, had determined to make a change.
But the rumors are denied. It would be gall, anyway,
for directors whose rash policy forced on the disaster to
punish a manager for carrying that policy into effect.
* * * »
B.H. Consols, the ridiculously over-capitalised
English co’s property, on the flat east of the B.H. lode, is
on another run of phenomenally rich stuff, some of the
ore (of course, the vein is small' and irregular) assaying
30 per cent, silver. Long, long ago, the co. paid £50,000
in divs. for similar stuff. The largest piece of silver-ore
found weighed 16cwt.; it was practically pure metal. That
was five years ago ; since then, prospects have, until lately,
been very sick.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — In your “ Wild Cat ” column
of 18th inst. appears a report on the Grong Grong reefs,
in which it is stated that no crushing has been done at
the Harry Smith mine for 12 months. Now, we are crush-
ing at the present time, and have been doing so for some
time, and have just cleaned up 120 oz. for the past four
weeks, and only running the battery half-time at that,
for want of water. As to the statement that £l2OO worth
of gold has been won from the Harry Smith, if that amount
were multiplied by 10, it would be a little nearer the
truth. Furthermore, the above mine never ceases crush-
ing, except when compelled to do so for the want of water,
which, unfortunately for the shareholders, is too often.—
L. Abrahams, Secretary, Harry Smith G.M. Co.
• * * *
Yet another mining disappointment for poor
N.S.W.—Victory, Forest Reefs. For many months the
public has been reading that the stuff would certainly
crush over an ounce to the ton, and calculations as usual
were made on that basis. Result: “ Crushed 50 tons for
350 z. amalgam estimated to contain 17ioz. gold, or 7dwt.
per ton.”
* * * *
The latest N.S.W. mining float in Bull Land :
“ The Great Mount Phoenix Gold-mine,” somewhere in
the Shoalhaven district. Some ore has been treated
going three and four ounces to the ton, and the lode is
said to be from 7to 10ft. thick. Capital, £110,000; pur-
chase price, £90,000 ; £5OOO cash, £25,000 either in cash
or shares, etc ; £60,000 in shares, leaving £20,000 for
working capital. It sounds like some old resurrection.
Anyhow, 10 to 1 there is no mine in that district worthy
of such a capitalisation, and if any attempt were
made to so place it locally it would be ridiculed out of
existence.
* * • •
An ably-written report on the Wolumla field,
N.S.W., has been furnished by J. E. Came, the geological
surveyor. He finds that while at Pambula, the neighbor-
ing field, the gold is entirely confined to felsites and
rhyolites, at Wolumla it occurs with altered sedimentary
rocks, crushed granite, and felsite. The output from 118
tons of picked first-grade stone from several shows was
6550z5. gold and 1812 oz. silver. Gold average, soz. lOdwt.
12gr. ; silver, 12oz. 17dwt. 14gr. per ton. The seconds
remain for treatment. At the same age Pambula had no
returns equal to these, yet it enjoyed a boom, the death-
like gloom of which extends even to Wolumla !

clared up to 17th) totalled £68,500, which is £2OOO better

Business people on the Towers, whose property
was damaged by the recent explosion at the Mills’ United,
intend proceeding for damages against the co. Mean-
while the co. and the Mine-owners’ Association offer
rewards of £5OO respectively for “information that will
lead to the conviction ” of the person or persons who
caused the said explosion. Towers mining magnates are
always ready and willing to attribute accidents and other
things to dynamitards : it helps the cause of the Fat Man
in more wavs than one.

Bowes Kelly, the Mt. Lyell magnate, dined
about a hundred guests at Hobart recently. His speech,
boiled down and shorn of extraneous champagneful trim-
ming :—“ Charge glasses ! . . . Toast Parliament Tas-
mania. Inducements necessary develop mining. Mt.
Lyell lease was too short; spectators wouldn’t put money
in it. Came Parliament; Parliament extended lease.
Did good thing. I dropped £20,000 Tasmanian mining
shows. Got it all back at Lyell . . . big mine . . . good
enough. Giving members Parliament dinner for helping
me. Jolly good fl’ers, but must make more tracks so
prospectors can develop mining ’sources.” (Musical
honors and responses.) Later —“ Health City Council.
Congratulate new Mayor. Citizens ’Obart and aldermen
good sort people. One word advice : They must make
tracks ! I’ll give £5OO towards cutting track to Port
Davey if Government give ’nother £5OO. Must make
tracks develop mining ’sources. Gentlemen, City Coun-
cil. ‘ For thev,’ etc.”
* * * *
Premier Forrest, in his budget, announces that
the Perth mint will produce “a thrill of joy” in Wes-
tralia by opening on Ist March next. There is no doubt
now that the mint is needed. The local product of gold
is quite enough to justify the existence of an indigenous
coinery—and as the banks have been charging 2s. 6d. to
3s. 6d. an ounce commission for transhipment, &c., to
Melbourne, the saving will be considerable. But how is
the mint to be managed ? The Deputy-Master, who will
have the responsibility of running the show, was taken
from the Royal Engineers, and figures as Lieutenant John
Francis Campbell. No mention is made of his capabilities
as a coiner, other than that he has spent three months
in the London mint since his appointment to Perth, and
that he is now thoroughly conversant with every process.
One would hardly expect this to be conclusive—unless,
indeed, he is somebody’s cousin or nephew. Moreover,
the London mint does not do the class of work which will
be the chief trouble in Perth. It deals with refined gold
received systematically from the Bank of England. The
Perth mint will have to take any sort of gold the publio
likes to bring, and at the public’s own time. The public
brings its gold in parcels varying from one ounce to
10,000 ounces, and there is only one certainty about it all
—it will never be refined. Cyanide gold will come for-
ward, with heavy admixture of zinc. Certain districts
will send gold with its local alloy of iron—and the
officials will have to struggle with each separate parcel,
refining it independently, and preparing it for the valua-
tion by assay. This is no child’s game ;in fact it is a task
that is quite unknown to the London artificers. Deputy-
Master Campbell has associated with himself two London
mint men, who come to their task blissfully unconscious
of the trouble it involves. Further developments are
awaited with interest.
* * » *
Even the Plunderderry mine, Coolgardie,
looks like becoming a steady producer. Last crushing,
330 tons for 6070 z. Quite good enough if it can be main-
tained. On the advice of the manager this prodigious
co. recently bought the adjoining lease to the south. The
mine was working within 80ft. of the boundary of that
lease, and the gold chute was dipping that way. This
and the northern block were peggad by the finders of the
“ golden hole.” Both were jumped and forfeited, but
lawyers kept a legal fight until the collapse, and the
political crowd in Perth, whose interests were with the
jumpers, fell in, whereas by compromise the £50,000
which only awaited the completion of the title could
have been lifted. They will know better next time, but
the chances are that none of the crowd will live long
enough to see Bull again make such an ass of himself.

Since January the big mines of Hannan’s have
paid nearly £400,000 in divs., including the extra big one
of £125,000 by the Lake View Consols. The total is about
three times as great as the divs. from all the other W.A.
mines. Menzies follows with about £50,000, while
£15,000 covers the divs. of all the great mines of the Cool-
gardie district!

Decided to wind up the General Gordon South,
which the chairman declared had been found to be abso-
lutely worthless. The other “ pups ” are in voluntary,
forced, or some other kind of liquidation, and the G.G.
itself is rapidly decaying. Some 20,000 forfeited shares
recently offered in Adelaide had to be bought in by the
co. What has become of the Vic. University Professor
who went out to run this show at a big salary, and whose
reports were worse than a Chinese puzzle ? Glass, the
first manager, died out there recently.
* * * *
From a reliable mining man at Kalgoorlie:— The
water difficulty will, I am afraid, be serious, although
most people here say there is not the least fear of a
failure in the supply. Seems to me that all the water is
in the oxidised zone of the country, and, as some of the
mines have got into the sulphide zone at 70ft., while
others hare had to go 200 ft. or more, it looks as though
the sulphide zone would be represented by a line in a sec-
tion of the belt in a N.W. and S.E. direction. As such a
supply can only be the accumulation of ages of rainfall, it
must necessarily be limited, and, as the number of stamps
increase, must necessarily diminish. There has been no
rain to speak of this year, nor does it look a bit like it
just now. The Govt, water scheme, even if it is ever
carried out to the letter, cannot be in operation for some
years—probably five—so the chances are that water will
be short in six months’ time, anyhow. As this matter has
a very important bearing on the almost immediate future
of the mines of this great camp, I propose to give it much
closer attention, and at some later date will give you the
conclusions I arrive at. Business is remarkably quiet,
but compared to Coolgardie it is lively. The banks have
put the screw on to a good number, and everybody is
praying for better times with the new year.
* * * *
Time that .the law in W.A. respecting the rights
of alluvial diggers on gold-leases was either more clearly
defined or set aside altogether. Called upon to define
the extent of his lode, the manager of the Ivanhoe Ven-
ture mine pegged out the whole lease as the boundaries
of the lode ! The Warden then ruled that the onus of
proving that the lode did not extend to the four corners
of the lease rested with the diggers, and he was compelled
to refuse their application to dig within the lease. The
example is sure to be followed by other managers ihe
moment any trouble from diggers is threatened.

knows.”

dpal inhabitant of that sandy district, told Gov.

At Melbourne District Court a few days
back one Fanny Cooper charged a man with the
paternity of her unlawful infant. Fanny was
quite positive on the point. She had made the
man’s acquaintance in 1889, she said, and in
January, 1890, a child was born. Whereupon
h anny took out a warrant for her lover’s arrest,
but he disappeared, and for six long years she
sought him vainly. Then she recognised the base
absconder in Melbourne Exhibition Gardens, and
Eut the law upon his track, and finally brought
im to the Court aforesaid, where she was pre-
pared to swear to him among a million betrayers
if necessary. It was a good yarn until the alleged
Lothario put in his defence. He proved that from
November, 1888, until April, 1891, he had led a
life of strictest celibacy in Darlinghurst Gaol. And
the only alibi that is better than being in gaol is
the alibi of being dead.
* * * *
Melbourne has got a lady burglar whose
experiences seem to show that the woman house-
breaker has some advantages over the male one.
A Richmond citizen found her ransacking his
house at midnight, and sprinted down the street
after her, the lady, who was barefooted, making
remarkably good time round the corner. When
he caught her, and tore most of her dress oti in
the process, she fell on the pavement under a gas-
lamp, and wept bitterly at his feet, and told him
that she had three starving children at home.
The householder was in a partially clothed state,
having rushed out in great haste, and that made
him feel embarrassed. Moreover, the burglar was
young and rather good-looking, and as she lay on
the pavement in the abandon of grief, showing her
bare feet and a lot of white ankle, she made
the situation all the more difficult. Moreover,
the man was painfully aware that he had torn a
great deal of her frock off in capturing her, and
that it wasn’t gentlemanly to thus violently un-
dress a lady in the street. Just at this crisis some
other men came along, and it being a man’s
natural instinct to take the part of a young woman
with bare feet they interceded for her, and the
only thing that citizen could do was to wipe away
a tear, and tell her to go home and be a good girl
and an ornament to society for the future. A male
Bill Sikes would never have got out of a scrape in
that fashion. Then the penitent girl went away
and apparently committed seven or eight more
burglaries, till she wore out public sympathy and
was arrested with <£ls worth of stolen under-
clothing in her possession.

SEEMS FEASIBLE, TOO.
(Overheard at the Divorce Court.)
MISS GIDDY : “ It’s curious how one sees a, lot of
divorced women around, but rarely a divorced
man —I wonder what becomes of them?”
HE (a married man) : “They die of joy.”
The woman who drives home her cruelty charge
in open court with an admission like this must
have a wholesome contempt for the sneery neigh-
bour.
* * * *
One B. Douglas propounds in the columns
of the Age an unexpected conundrum—“ Why is
Victoria retrograding ? ” Douglas gives, at con-
siderable length, the brief answer—George Tur-
ner and co.” ‘ How,” he asks, “ can we possibly
improve our condition under the present do-noth-
ing policy of the Government ? What we want to
resuscitate our deplorable condition is a bold and
Liberal policy, such as would set the wheels of in-
dustry in motion ... a practical and progres-
sive Government . . . one that will raise the re-
quired means for the development, &c., &c. By
this means we may soon restore prosperity, and
make this grand country blossom as of yore.”
A Melb. lawyer, prominent in criminal
practice, has decided to shut down for ever on
Hindoo cases. He avers that he wouldn’t believe
any Indian whatever on his oath or on anything
else. Prior to a recent case in which one of these
“ British subjects like ourselves ” was implicated,
the client rounded up a dozen of his “ cousins ”
to the lawyer’s office. “What did you bring all
these men here for?” demanded the man of
parchment. “All right,” said the son of the
Himalayas, with a sweep of his hand; “ me pay
you ; you tell him what to swear.” The lawyer
decided to let some other rising member of his
profession have the job, and fired the procession
of yellow scum down the steps. “ I’m not over
particular,” he says, “but those fellows are a bit
too strong.” It would really be worth while to
keep a record of Hindoo evidence in Austra-
lian courts. Kipling relates that nobody pays
the slightest attention to native evidence in
India, where you can get a murder charge (corpse
included) trumped up against an enemy for 10
rupees.

* » * *
Sundry S.A. brewers offered to come
down handsomely with beer on Xmas day so as
to gladden the hearts of the poor old derelicts
and incurables in the benevolent asylums with one
last look upon the foaming pewter. Whereupon
the Christian Endeavor Union requested Kingston
to prohibit the distribution, and keep the aged on
weak tea and cold water at the festive season. It
is gratifying to note that the S.A. Premier re-
fused, and the pity is that he didn’t refuse with
anything like the violent language that the case
called for. As the ancients in the asylums can’t
get out to go on the bust even if they do contract
a love of drink at the age of 80 through having
one beer at Xmas, and as they have no money
to bust with if they do get out, and as
there will be no more gratis beer for 12 months,
the Christian Endeavor Union looks a pretty mean
crowd of bigots, anyhow. What does it matter
if the toothless patriarch does get a chance to fix
his bleared eye on just one beer before lie dies ?
The Union would supply him instead with cold
water and exhortation ; but water is thin and ex-
hortation is very flatulent, and when a man is aged
75 and gone in the legs he prefers creature com-
forts that warm his innards for the moment to all
the spiritual comfort that ever grew in Palestine.
And probably, if the choice was left to' him, he
wouldn’t give up his shaky hold on that lonesome
pot of consolation to save the souls of all the
Christian Endeavorers in S.A.

Divorce and advertisement. From a recent

kola nut tonic ?
Witness : I may have. I think he was taking it at the

Witness : Yes.
Do you find it good for you ?
Witness ; it is fine and fattening. (Laughter.)
On the endurance of Government Depart-
ments. Years ago, in the principal Baratarian
Post-office, an imported youth was appointed,
through political influence, to a position carrying
■£2oo per annum. There was no work for him to
do, and the postmaster set him to copy the printed
slips (on which were the names of the’recipients of
registered letters) into a book. This duty was
altogether too arduous, and the gilded youth, con-
trary to instructions, began to cut off the printed
slips and paste them in. The postmaster remons-
trated, was defied, had to report the matter to
headquarters, and was politely informed : “The
Department had no objection to the pasting pro-
cess.” And, for all that is known to the contrary,
that youth, now grown-up, is pasting in slips to
this day.

YARDED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
"* AI J in order now save one thing !” Heaven heard the
Super’s voice.
“ One thing left to make all perfect, and the Boss’s heart
rejoice.
There are spirits roaming w ildly through von forest land
of stars—
Build a yard wherein to tame them ! Bind it round with
brazen bars.”
So they built the gateless earth-yard, and the pick of
heaven’s host
Rode afar on white-winged horses ordered out to round
the lost—
Brumby souls that knew no sorrow, nor the curse of har-
ness scars—
And they jammed them on the ring-fence—forced them
in between the bars.
Saddled them with human bodies, girthed them up with
cords of sin ;
Bridled them with bits of sorrow, drew the curb-straps
tightly in ;
And a cry of direst anguish heard from Neptune unto
Mars
Told of millions stamping, foaming there behind the
brazen bars.
Then the breaker-in, Convention, mounted some with
• steady grip,
And though thrown and battered badly, kept a stiffened
upper lip ;
Mounted o'er and o’er till broken, covered o’er with
wrinkled scars—
Fate could guide them where he listed ; then Death took
them from the bars.
Led them off to placid pastures, where the cow-like spirits
graze, .
Underneath the scornful pity of the Godhead’s stony
gaze.
And they called this paddock Heaven—and the free far
land of stars
Faded from their recollection—stupefied behind the bars.
W.A.
L.M.D.
THE CURE FOR DEPRESSION.
When would-be toilers groan aloud
For work or occupation,
And leaders of the land-boom crowd
Are lying low behind a cloud
Avoiding observation ;
When rains are spoiling farmers’ crops,
And small suburban drapers’ shops
Can’t take enough to pay the rent,
And stock, instead of rising, drops—
Then is the time for giving “ slops”
To Turner and the Government.
As rulers of a trustful State,
And pillars of the nation,
Their duty is to legislate
A means of raising profits great
From general stagnation ;
To “ put their shoulders to the wheel,”
And turn up trumps at every deal,
And borrow’ cash at four per cent.
Wherewith to feed the public purse ;
Or else the “country ” breathes a curse
On Turner and the Government.
The life-blood of enduring trade
Is boomsome legislation,
The “ country ” needs the earnest aid
Of Gillies. Why thus long delayed
Victoria’s salvation ?
His policy would soon restore
Lost confidence for evermore,
And banish gloomy discontent.
Rise, Duncan, rise ! the gods command ;
Thine be the task to rid the land
Of Turner and the Government.
DIVORCED.
(A postscript to “ Wedded,” by M.J.C.)
’Twas us two ere we married,
An’ us two when ’twas done—•
’Twas fully time we parted,
’Twas never once “ us one ! ”
H.C.C.
FEET OF CLAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
In my youth the pure Ideal
Shone as proudly o’er the Real
As the silvery torch of Dian o’er a petty candle ray ;
But as years dispelled Illusion,
Fell my gods in dire confusion,
And I wept—to think my Idol’s feet were only feet of clay :
And as time goes on the Real
Grips the throat of things Ideal
With the grip of wolves that fasten on the flank of fleeting
prey;
And as Days advance I falter
In my search for fane and altar,
And would gladly find an Idol —that had only feet of clay.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
Here is an interesting English mystery
which is somewhat relevant to a cursory discussion
that once flitted through The Bulletin columns.
A man lately disappeared from a large London
hotel. When last seen he was entering his bed-
room. This room was found exactly as a man
would leave it on turning into bed. All his
clothes were there, and it was evident that if he
had gone off, as he undoubtedly had, he had de-
parted in his pyjamas and a dressing-gown, and
bootless, hatless, and penniless. A search was
made, of course, but not the faintest clue came to
light. The river was dragged, the detectives
poked about, and interest flickered and went out.
I hen, seven weeks after the man’s disappearance,
his body was found by an eccentric foreign visitor.
It was shockingly decomposed, and lay under an
accummulation of cobwebs in the hotel bath.
The Revue Medicale (Paris) states that
An English ladies’ tailor advertises that he is the
sole possessor of a fabric which resists the passage
of the Rontgen rays, so that ladies’ charms can-
not be disclosed to the curious without their
consent.

There are many quaint ways of dying in
Australia ; the backblocks are full of unconsidered
side-tracks leading to destruction. But a bush-
man who lately pegged-out near Chiltern (Vic.)
met with an end which was even above the Aus-
tralian bush average for shuddersomeness, and,
from a shuddery point of view, the bush —with its
stories of death from thirst, and of timber-getters
slowly burnt to death or gradually crow-eaten
while held down alive by fallen trees- -is a place
where the nice fresh horror is always waiting on
the half-shell, so to speak. This particular bush-
man took a fit in a paddock beside the Murray.
Then the ants ate him for 24 hours, at the end of
which time he was discovered still alive, but
dying. For plain, homely gruesomeness that
episode would be hard to beat.

In Brisbane, the other day, six sailor-men
from the Buteshire marauded a Chinaman’s shop.
Five shore-policemen started to arrest them.
Twenty-six sailor comrades came to the rescue-
-32 against 5. Nevertheless, the five constables
lumbered each a man ! Twenty-seven sailor-men
then threatened to wreck the lockup, if their
mates were not freed, but four policemen charged
down on them and dispersed the lot ! The British
sailor-man is degenerating.

“The only Greek church in Australia ” is
to be erected on high ground at Waverley, N.S.W.
According to rule, the rising and setting sun must
shine on the building, in which weird priests,
wearing long black cylinders on their heads, will
discourse to the purveyors of the immature
banana and the elderly mullet in a language com-
posed mostly of noughts and crosses.

go klankee —kleppymanee ! ”
A quaint Bulle-
tin correspondent
(alas, most Bulletin
correspondents “run”
to Drink) :
I have been taking too
much whisky. I was con-
scious of intense malaise
if I could not get it. I
drink only between 8 and
10 p.m.—but was shocked
at last to find it took J of
a bottle to satisfy me.
What to do ? By a mere
accident I took to elder-
berry wine of very low
alcoholic strength. It is
very nasty. I find that a
J-bottle will replace the
whisky and I suffer hardly
any malaise. This is curi-
ous. To experiment fur-
ther, I tried about J-lb of
plum jam in 1| pints of
boiling water. Same re-
sult! It seems, then, that,
in some cases, where there
is strong- desire to reform,
the craving may not really
be for alcohol, but merely
for sugar and water. Or,
any readily diffusible and
assimilable tonic, food, or
stimulant. This is worth
noting. By the way, a
thing I like very much is
kola comp, elixir. It con-
tains celery seed, cocoa,
kola ; 10s. a bottle here in
the wilds of Maoriland.
It does not create a habit with me, but gives me great
joy. It makes me feel light and peckish and charitable.
You can mix it with alcohol, hot water, lime-juice, tea,
sugar, and Van Houten’s cocoa, all together, and you
won’t want to call the Queen your aunt. Iced, with
cocoa and lemon-squash, it would be grand. Or treat it
like vermouth gommee. Mix with sugar, acid, and strong
gum, scrape a lot of ice in, and suck through a straw. A
very nice hot drink is sugar, lime-juice, V.H. cocoa—
boiling water. Lemon-and-melon jam in boiling tea is
also good.

HE (to stage aspirant ): “ What do you expect to appear in first? ”
SHE: “Oh, tights, I suppose!”
About the most agreeable row any Bulle-
tin man ever saw took place on Boxing Day in a
Sydney banana shop. The Roman proprietor had
sold to a stout Sussex-street matron a bag of
bananas with the bad ones at the bottom as usual,
and she was just bringing them back as this scribe
went by. She commenced the campaign by land-
ing Marc Antony in the eye with the oldest fruit
in the bag. While he was trying to get the
banana out of his eye the lady filled the place
with abusive expressions, and pounded the stock
generally with an umbrella, and before the Roman
could see and had finished a few observations on
his own account she had burst almost everything
except one unripe melon, which refused to give
way to her assaults. Then an Italian reinforce-
ment seized her from behind, but she knocked it
out in one round, put her foot into a tray of
tomatoes, wrecked the fly-catcher, aimed the
melon into the back-room at the Italian’s wife,
who was having hysterics all by herself, missed
her but broke some crockery, which rebounded on
to the cat, and then tramped over the ruins into
the street, where the loafers at the adjacent pub.
cheered wildly as she departed.

Adelaide Hansard, in reporting a recent
speech by Premier Kingston, made him say that
in S.A. the percentage of legitimate to the total
births of the province is 3'44. The report con-
tinues that he is “delighted at this state of
things, which reflects infinite credit on the pro-
vince.” By-and-bye this beautiful misprint will
get to England, and the hair on the head of the
Nonconformist conscience will stand up in horror.

Melbourne City Council was discussing the
question of street-watering the other day, when
Alderman Carter, M.L.A., whisky-merchant, and
high-priest in the temple of Respectability, put in
a word for sprinkling the city streets on the
blessed “Sawbuth.” Said Carter: “The Council
can get plenty of infidels to water the streets on
Sunday, and so obviate the necessity of well-
dressed gentlemen and ladies who have consciences
being compelled to attend their places of worship
in a very undesirable frame of mind.” Observe :
The man who waters the streets on Sunday is
necessarily an mfidel ; an infidel has no con-
science ; he would be lost anyhow ; why not put
him on to sprinkle the well-dressed gentleman’s
way to church, in order that the well-dressed gent
may arrive at the tabernacle in a desirable frame
of mind, and not mad with Providence because
there is grit in his eye, and his Paris hat looks as
if it had been dragged through a dust-storm by a
horrid dog ? The unconscionable infidel has his
uses—his duty is to enable Pat Man to go to
church in comfort.
* * * *
One Sarah Ann Somebody, who recently
sued for a divorce in Melb. and failed to get it, is
another victim of the unaccountableness of the
law. Sarah Ann Somebody and her husband had
their home and domicile in the backblocks of
Queensland. The evidence alleged that the
reason why she didn’t sue for a divorce in Queens-
land was that her husband threatened to “do
for her” if he ever found her in that province
again ; also, he gave her 20s. and threw her off a
steamer (then in Hobson’s Bay) on to Victorian
soil. This was eight years ago, and the lady had
lived in Victoria ever since, nevertheless judge
Hodges decided that Sarah Arm Somebody hadn’t
an “independent domicile”in Victoria,and hadn’t
been “ domiciled in Victoria at the time of the
desertion,” so her petition was refused. Yet, if
Sarah Ann Somebody, who purported to be fired
out of a steamer in Victorian waters, with 20s. in
her pocket, and warned off at the risk of fatal
personal stoush, wasn’t deserted in Victoria, where
was she deserted ? She must have been deserted
somewhere.
When unfortunates “go balmy,” or see
more things than usual, on the Westralian
frontier-fields, they are considerately chained up.
Whereupon a Magnet jury has talked this way in
a verdict: “We find came to his death
while of unsound mind from exhaustion, the re-
result of shameful want of proper accommodation
for such cases at both Geraldton and Mt.
Magnet.” Also, it added that what accommoda-
tion was provided was “ a disgrace to civilisation.”

What is left of that ancient Melb. hostelry,
the Albion, Bourke-street —a pub. redolent of
memories of the golden coaching days—was
before the Licensing Court the other day, charged
with not having sufficient entrances, i.e. , one to
the premises and another to the bar direct. The
old Albion was pulled down a year or more ago,
and the name now stands only for an underground
bar and feeding-room to which there is but one
front door. The Albion pleaded that a back-door
from Little Bourke-street fulfilled the conditions
of the Act, and the residuum of the ancient ren-
dezvous was discharged without a stain on its
counter.

Gunnedah, N.S.W., Titbit Bros, in charge on
bikes.
Tozer wanted, the other day, to appoint a
protector of half-castes—half-caste girls in particu-
lar. . . . Western, M.L.A., remarked that he
knew one half-caste in the West who owned 1000
head of cattle ; two others, who were drovers,
employed white men and possessed hank accounts.
A protector for these men would certainly be an
anomaly. . . . The treatment of aboriginals in
the beche-de-mer trade is a matter that calls for
speedy remedy. It is no uncommon thing for
men to be taken to the islands by beche-de-mer
fishers, and left there to starve or to drown by
way of settling the wages question.
According to the statement of the prose-
cutrix in the Suffield case, she first saw her
assaulter sitting on a gate, smoking. She recog-
nised him. He bade her good evening. Being
frightened, she did not reply, but ran quickly
past him. Yet it appears that all she had
ever heard of Suffield was of his kindly and
inoffensive disposition. His gentleness towards
children had been specially instanced to her. She
herself appears to have been anything but un-
willing to listen to the relation of these traits of
Suffield’s character. Yet the mere fact of meet-
ing him, sitting at ten o’clock of a summer’s night,
upon a gate, smoking, within hail of her em-
ployer and his family—the mere sound of this
gentle and inoffensive man’s voice—so frightened
her that she rushed, panic-stricken, away from
him !
THE WAY OF A MAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
She was a maid of notions strange
Concerning man.
Her fancies took a goodly range,
As woman’s can!
She said she loved an eye of gray—•
My own are blue.
I would I could in any way
Have changed their hue.
The sort of hair that pleased her best
Was brown, she said.
Which caused me oft supreme unrest—
My own is red !
Her taste in “ nose ” was aquiline ;
Oh, cruel rub!
In vulgar parlance one like mine
Is termed a snub !
She liked a stately man and tall—
Alas! my fate
Has blessed me with a stature small
Not five feet eight.
She doted on a figure slim :
Without a doubt
I could not satisfy that whim—
For I am stout.
And, knowing this, how could I dare
(A boor like me)
To ask that maid her life to share—
My wife to be ?
And yet I did ! egregious ass,
I must confess ;
I popped the question—and, alas 1
She answered “ Yes ! ”

EPITAPH.
(For a pig and a brother).
“ The pig which swam ashore with the rope from the
wrecked steamer liameruka, and was instrumental in
saving the lives of 45 persons, has since been killed and
cured in its entire state, and is now on view. —Daily Papier.
You did what men were loth to do ;
Alas ! that mankind is so fickle.
Through wrath and wrack the line you drew,
And did what we were loth to do.
Oh, shame ! that they should martyr you,
And put your noble corpse in pickle.
T.D.
THE CREATION.
God made the world. But all who see the light
Have to create again this universe,
And color it, apportion day and night,
Make it a paradise, or else a curse.
And so I shaped it with the tools I had,
And 10, I looked at it—and it was bad !
It happened, as it mostly does to men,
That I began to rave and rhyme and flirt;
To cut it short : I fell in love, and then
I thought a reconstruction would not hurt.
I shaped the world anew in Love’s sweet mood,
And 10, I looked at it—and it was good !
