A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 28, 1897.
Mr Dear Mtee —
Desolation will flap its sable wings for about a
month longer at Govt.-House, and then the ’appy
’ome will be filled again. His Ex. and party will
turn their faces Sydney-wards on the 20th January,
and Her Ex. has booked her passage to this be-
nighted country by P. and O. of the near future.
A brother of Gov. Hampden, by name Thomas
Brand, has arrived here and been convoyed to the
vice-regal summer retreat by Aide. Sloane-Stanley.
Capt. and Mrs. Ferguson will flit to London for
the season, and Mrs. F. will shake the creases out
of her wedding-dress and give it an airing before
H.M. at a drawing-room. Brides of the “ hupper
suckles ” are always expected to toe the line at
Buckingham Palace within a reasonable time after
their marriage.
Boxing Day meeting at Randwick brought out
a fair collection of notables, local and interpro-
vincial. As the day was cool, highly - polished
toppers and long coats were worn by all the poli-
ticians, who all looked their sprucest. The veteran
Reggie Bright trotted wearily about with a “ has-
been ” air ; Emily Soldene was a sketch in black-
and-white. On the well-filled stand were many
bumt-up holiday-makers from up North, and some
new and startling styles, which mostly adorned
the backs and heads of visitors from the country
and other provinces. The drunk-and-disorderly
hat was a feature of the millinery; with a bashed-
in crown, and slapped on to the wearer’s left ear,
it lends a riotous appearance which would justify
any magistrate in giving the wearer 14 days’ with-
out the option at sight. A pink girl had a bright-
rose straw tile thus defying the laws of gravity by
standing on its brim on her shoulder; and another,
a golden hat which crowned a yellow-and-white
poem, was hanging on its owner’s left ear.
To be up-to-date now, every woman uses
one ear as a hat-peg. Three gaudy girls in bilious
orange, blue and brick-pink, respectively, obvi-
ously wore their finery at the society-paragra-
phistes. Little Mrs. Savage tripped prettily in
pale blue. Miss Church had a neat, pink frock
and a highly successful hat. A lady with an
arsenic green ruff looked as if she had trimmed
her throat with curly cabbage.
As a display of gilty splendor and gorgeous
gowning the Royal Panto., “Jack the Giant Kil-
ler,’’takes high rank among seasonable infestivities.
The purple and fine linen is not merely confined,
as it often is in Pantos., to the jokes of the come-
dian and the petticoats of the ballet; but it
is lavished broad-cast on every performer down
to the minutest “ super ” whose small body
is encased in glossy satin and the most lustrous of
silken tights. In the midst of so much sartorial
splendor, even Lauri, as the inevitable burlesque
female, hadn’t the heart to forego a chance of
sporting a swell ng-out, and looked a positively
pretty woman in powdered hair and a multi-colored
Watteau gown. The four new ballet-maidens
settled down into the affections of the male portion
of the audience with a loud clank—especially
the dark - eyed houri who distributed smiles
and winks round the house with devastating
effect. The first three dances of this fascinating
quartet, though performed in garments of much
loveliness, were eclipsed by the delirious whirl in
the Court scene, where the beauties, gowned in
indescribable masses of pink brocade, scarlet satin,
white lace poppies and ribbons, finished up their
can-can by being borne aloft, shoulder-high, each
with" a fascinating black satin shoe pointing to the
gas-jet. Lottie Moore, more winsome than ever,
but wearing a frock of uncompromising blueness,
which clashed with its surroundings every time,
made Pansy quite an intelligent panto, girl. In the
final scene she comes on inside an eye-dazzler of a
brocade frock with pale-blue trimmings, and spoils
the effect thereof by exhibiting doubtfully white
hosen which suggest the harrowing thought that
Lottie’s washing hadn’t come home in time. Tom
Woottwell’s indigo circus-tent pants looked well
when he entered on his patent motor-car, pro-
pelled by a little portable fireworks-factory con-
cealed in a kerosene-tin. A wild craving of the
audience for encores gave opportunity to a robust,
red girl to tack on several additional verses to a
song which was dreary and wholly unintelligible.
The only words of it which were understandable to
an English-speaking audience w ere “Themayden
was so sorsee,” and they didn't seem worth re-
iteration.
Contradictory reports are given about this year’s
Xmas trade. Some leading firms declare they
have had better biz. this year than they have since
the great gone-bung time ; other traders depres-
singly state that money is tight. In that case,
money must be like every second man in the
streets towards the wee sma’ ’oors on Xmas Eve.
To the theatre-goers leaving the Royal near mid-
night, where “Jack the Giant-Killer” was still
going strong, the prowlers looked very staggery.
A roaring trade is doing in gloves ; they are the
“ toney ” present at this festive season. One
lady, whose name wold bullocks would not tear
from me, was taken into a glove-shop by a man
friend, who asked her advice about getting some
pairs as a present for a lady. The sagacious
woman thought she knew whom the gloves were
for, and chose assorted colors in three-and-nine-
penny misfits, inwardly rejoicing at the thought
of the other woman's disgust at the badly-chosen
gift. The donor meekly suggested a higher price,
ut his adviser assured him he was doing the right
thing at 3s. 9d., so he bought four pairs and handed
them to her with a polite bow as his little Xmas
tribute. And the glove-expert had to look full of
wild rapture, and to accept, with a twisted smile,
her cheap kids.
For want of style and dowdy frumpishness the
first-night d.c. of “ The Babes in the Wood ” was
below low-water mark. Every woman flopped
and fanned herself, and her hair came out of curl,
and few men were in regulation rig, while the
gowns were all above high-water mark and a low-
cut bodice the exception. One frock, strapped up
over the shoulders with beady bands, was smart,
and a black-and-white dress in the front row
looked fashionable. Ada Reeve, like a green-and-
gold lizard in tights, annexed many flower
bunches and one ingenious device of a vegetable
bow and arrow. Alice Leamar’s wedding-dress
is looped about with pearly chains, and dainty
cupids hold up her white train, pink as an em-
bodied bridal blush. The glitter of the wedding-
ballet culminates in the lobster-salad girls with
silver bowls for hats and lettuce garlands on their
skirts, and in the walking Neapolitan ices in
correct stripes. Robin Hood’s merry men are
bandbox foresters, correctly clad in cloth by
an accurate costume designer. The make-up
of the two desperadoes, Coleman and Burke,
is a feature of the show. In their heavy-
villain boots, drooping wool-feathers and gaudy
cloaks, they prowl about melodramatically and
carry in their belts a whole marine-store of rusty
ironmongery. On the proceeds of their crime
they emerge as cheap swells at a seaside resort,
where Burke is annexed by a large rope to the
largest hat on record.
A cruel disappointment befell an up-country
township recently through the non-arrival of any
dignitaries to bless the local flower-show. Capt.
Ferguson, the nearest approach to vice-royalty ob-
tainable, was expected, as he was visiting those
parts, to throw a Govt. House halo around the
blossoms. To meet the train he was supposed to
arrive by, a squadron of the district soldiery was
drawn up, and an officer pranced up and down
before the regiment in martial style. But the
looked-for visitor didn’t arrive as per agreement,
and the Mayor had to be hastily turned on to
open the show before the exhibits wilted away,
which was a very flat proceeding. And the dis-
gusted soldiery drowned their grief in beer.
It is “the thing ” among the Women’s College
students to wear sun - bonnets in the college-
grounds. The flopping head-gear is more com-
fortable than the hard, unyielding trencher, and
surrounding an innocent baby-face with fluffy
hair it looks fetching; but the soft muslin frills
standing off from a bald, knobby forehead and
glistening specs, give the wearer the appearance
of a she-wolf in lamb’s clothing. By the way, a
back view of a girl-undergraduate in a roomy
gown, topped off with a flapping cotton bonnet,
doesn’t suggest much grace of outline.
Nicholson and co. issue their Xmas musical
magazine with a bold challenge to the whole
world to show any other shilling publication of
similar quality. Twenty-six new and popular
ballads for a shilling come to less than a half-
penny per ballad, which certainly is cheap. The
principle soul-cries of the production are a
specially-composed song “This is Love,” by A.
Plumpton, Florence Schmidt’s favorite encore
warble, “ The Day when You’ll Forget Me,” and
a tuneful “Japanese Lullaby” sung by Fannie
Liddiard, at the Lyceum Theatre. Besides these
there are a good number of sad comics and some
good standard songs, the whole enclosed in a gay
and artistic cover showing a draping of national
bunting over a glimpse of the ’Arbor. This
locally-produced music-book has no “ Made in
Germany ” about it; it is all got up right here on
the spot.
Divorce is a queer stepping-stone by which to
clamber up among the enthroned gods on society’s
gilded seats, but some ambitious women are using
their rumored domestic cataclysms in that way.
A handsome woman, whose name has been in
everybody’s mouth lately as a future figurer before
Justice Simpson, took the opportunity recently to
go forth in public with her hubby and intro-
duce him to an acquaintance, observing ironically,
“ I suppose you’ve heard that my husband is
going to divorce me, and that Mr. So-and-So and
Mr. Such-and-Such are to be made co-respon-
dents ? ” The acquaintance hadn’t heard of it till
then, and was properly sympathetic at the
unfounded rumor, and also at the social status of
the alleged co-respondents—all thoroughly Naice
men. “The Blanks are not going into the
Divorce-court,” “ Mr. So-and-So is not a co-
respondent,” is as good an advt. as putting it the
other way round.
Many an unwritten romance could be disin-
terred from the old-clothes shops. The chief
dealers have their regular connection, and at the
season’s end triumphs of toggery are disposed
of cheap by leaders of fashion to the reach-me-
down vendors. It is an understood thing that the
garments must be altered so that their identity is
destroyed. And, as a rule, the Sydney smart
woman’s clotheß become old friends to her friends
before she discards them, and are easily recognis-
able. A lady had an imported skirt when the
black skirt and colored blouse boom was raging,
like unto whioh there was no other robe in the
city ; she wore it until its decadence, and even
beyond, and then sold it. And. one day a spiteful
cat, paying an afternoon visit, gave her »'vay
before a whole roomful of callers by saying : yh>
my dear, I’ve just seen your black skirt walking
down the street with a cream blouse, and it looks
as good as new.”
The stamp-collecting craze is not so crazy as
some people imagine. Postage-stamps are quite a
modern idea, even now, and hundreds of ‘ first
•issues,” still obtainable for a few pence, or shil-
lings, are likely to become valuable relics before
the youth of to-day becomes bald and toothless.
This is the golden age of philately. The rarity of
early Australian stamps, for instance, is bound to
keep pace with the advance in population and
education and consequent increase, in letter-writ-
ing, apart from the gradual disappearance of
present collections through accidental destruction.
Australian stamps, on a rough reckoning, were
about forty times more scarce at the outset than
English issues. In all countries the tendency is
for stamps to grow commoner, and, therefore, less
worth collecting as time goes along, until, at last
the philatelic demand will be for “ first century ’
issues only. This is the first century. Most of
the stamps we give away to acquisitive small boys
are destined to crumble into oblivion under a glass
case.
Lydies aren’t popular lodgers with landladies
anywhere. ’ Even in the sparsely-womaned West,
girls travelling “ on their own ” get the cold shoul-
der from hostesses. Two girls from this side, who
lately went to Perth to find work at the inevitable
typewriter, on landing, wearily trudged about
from house to house, only to be met with the curt
refusal, “We don’t take lydies.” The kind of
woman who gets on in those parts is the one who
is never without an escort. But civilisation is
spreading, and so a mine-manager found when he
arrived at a Westrahan town, bringing with him
a lady whom, to appease the proprieties, he called
his “sister.” But (I am told the “sister” was
fascinating) the shift-boss, the engineer, and one
or two others promptly proposed to her, so the
manager (who evidently was a Bible student, and
knew the game had been played before) put a full
stop—or, at all events, a semi-colon —to all such
philandering by marrying the lady himself.
Some of the ’buses with the new stair attach-
ment have been entirely deserted on top lately,
women preferring to crowd inside on sunny days
to sitting aloft on the shiny oilcloth. A ’bus-boy
explained the reason to a curious “ inside ” on a
day of lofty temperature : “ Carn’t sit outside —
jist feel them cushions.’
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Married lady-doetors, apparently, follow the
example of married stage-ladies in being profes-
sionally known as ‘Miss.’ The ex-Melbourne
Hospital surgeoness, though now Mrs. Buchanan,
practises as ‘Dr. Ereda Gamble,’ and has a huge
brass-plate so inscribed on her Collins-street front-
gate. Similarly, Melbourne’s best-known woman-
medico, Dr. Constance Stone, is, privately, plain
Mrs. Jones.
“ Mt. Morganaire D’Arcy, now of Stanmore
Hall, Middlesex, has just lost his wife. News of
Mrs. D’Arcy’s death reached Australia on Christ-
mas Eve. She was a dark, handsome lady, for-
merly Miss Birkbeck, of Rockhampton. Some of
her picturesque clan still bask in Bananaland
sunshine, only pausing in their enjoyment of life
when some tactless outsider describes them as
‘Mexicans’ instead of ‘South American Spanish.’
“ Somehow, Mrs. D’Arcy seldom appeared in
public, and with increasing riches and importance
she grew more and more retiring. To the last,
however, she was well worth looking at, and she
understood, moreover, the secret of setting-off to
the best advantage her raven hair, stately figure,
and clear-cut features. Old Rockhamptonites still
admiringly chronicle remembered glimpses of her
as a young wife, garbed effectively in colors—
daring for those times—as she drove about the
township in her husband’s one-horse buggy.

was a translation of Hugo’s ‘Fishers.’ The

‘ the ominous old ocean sobs without ’ as ‘ the
amorous old ocean,’ etc.
“But, really and truly, love seems to come
almost as naturally on the ocean-wave as sea-sick-
ness. A fond girl travelled all the way from Eng-
land in the steerage, just the other day, to marry
her true love in Australia. The faithful one had
hardly stepped aboard to welcome his bride-elect
when she asked, in a snip-snap tone : ‘ Can you
marry me, and give me a home, within half-an-
hour ? If not, here’s a chap as will ! ’ turning to
a fellow-passenger standing sheepishly at her
side. The sight of a rival in the field speedily
heated up Number One’s affections, which, perhaps,
had grown a bit lukewarm, and he talked so
eloquently of a double-frontage villa and a lady-
help, that he haled his matrimonial consignment
and her worldly goods ashore without further
bother.
“ A neat thing in semi-official Xmas cards was
issued ‘with the best wishes of the Mayor of
Melbourne and Mrs. M'Eaoharn.’ The four sides
of this festive souvenir present views of the Town
Hall, Prince’s Bridge, Queen’s Wharf, and other
places as they were in 1857 and are to-day ; also, a
picture of Melbourne’s first wild, untutored
settlement (1837) on the bushy Yarra bank
where the modern wharf-lumper now earns
colonial beer by the Sweat of his brow.
Eawkner and Batman, the early fathers of Mar-
vellous M., are suggested by two very small figures
crossing the Yarra—which is believed to have been
quite suitable for bathing, at that period—in a
small boat. Presumably, they have just corn-
pleted the purchase of the future Victorian capital
for a few yellow glass beads and a bottle of rum.
A black dot on the opposite bank of the river may
be taken to depict the royal aboriginal vendor of
the property engaged in emptying the bottle.
“Aprons and embonpoint go abominably to-
gether. At a tony charity function of modern
times, more than one beefy dowager blotted an
otherwise agreeable landscape through adopting a
‘pinny.’ It’s like going to a ball with palpable
powder on your nose, as if to accentuate your mis-
fortune in burning the offending feature beet-red
at tennis that afternoon. .
“About a year back, the bride of a recent
fashionable wedding had pointed out to her the
girl whom a,n acquaintance was about to make his
wife. Gazing in true feminine fashion at the
other, she turned to her friend. ‘What special
qualifications has she to be Asterisk Dash’s girl ? ’
she demanded, with a snort. The question, in
time, returned to the principals, and the other day,
when her own much-advertised wedding took
place, one of the first telegrams to arrive was from
Mrs. Dash, ‘ What special qualifications have you
to be Blank Thingumbob’s wife ? ’
“ It is related that during Labor-member John
Hancock’s last election for Footscray, two fine
carriages with spanking horses drove up to his
committee-rooms. The drivers asked for placards,
and labelled their vehicles ‘Yote for Hancock.’
The members of Hancock’s committee were
puzzled about these carriages; they couldn’t
understand where they came from. But they
were employed all the day in Hancock’s service.
It now transpires that they were sent up by
squatter Pursey Chirnside from Werribee. One
of the few commendable features in Pursey’s
political character is his liking for Hancock.
“Brassey, Madden, and M‘lntyre sat together
at the Melbourne Conservatoire of Music concert,
and pretended not to be bored. These three wise
men apparently know little about music. One of
the numbers performed was Schubert’s Unfinished
Symphony—two movements, allegro and andante.
It was played in the usual way, with an interval
between the movements. But when the allegro
movement was completed, Brassey, Madden, and
MTntyre joined in the applause, and then turned
to their programmes to see what was to come next.
The next number down was a song from Weber’s
‘Der Freischiitz.’ But when the orchestra began
again on the andante of the symphony, the mix-
ture of bored anguish and amazement on the faces
of the illustrious three was comical. They looked
at their programmes and at each other, and then
had a whispered consultation, pointed to the next
item, elevated their eyebrows, and at last gave up
the puzzle.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Appropiate scriptural welcome to N.S.W.
politicians visiting Japan : “To see the nakedness
of the land are ye come.”
In reply to a demand that the Yic. Govern-
ment should raise a loan and start a policy of
“reproductivepublic words,” Premier Turner re-
plied flatly that he didn’t know where to find any.
The Age falls on Turner very heavily over this
statement, yet in the sense the Yic. Premier pro-
bably intended the statement was logical enough.
There is hardly such a thing to be found now as a
new public work that will pay, for years to come,
the full interest on the money borrowed, even at
3 per cent., for its construction. There are any
number of new railways and water conservation
works wanted which would pay perhaps 1\ per
cent, immediately and more afterwards, and these
provinces will have to tackle them seriously before
long, or else petrify. But if they are built with
borrowed money the debt and the interest
deficiency are such a huge handicap that they will
probably not get anywhere near the paying point
for 200 years. The N.S.W. railways, for instance,
are doing better than some other systems, yet
they have nearly =68,000,000 of accumulated defi-
ciency to work off before they begin to be a profit-
able property.
An “ Arabian Nights ” story relates how
a certain fisherman found a very small pot sealed
with a cabalistic seal—and opened it —whereupon
out came a tremendous genie,, who spread over
heaven and earth before the fisherman could say
that the prawns in some Sydney shops go uncom-
monly high this weather. Something of the same
kind seems to be happening in the Carruthers-
Norton libel-case. This case started in a small,
mild way over a piece of land which, it was
alleged in Norton’s paper Truth, had been im-
properly or foolishly dealt with by Carruthers
in his capacity as Lands Minister; but the
original, dry, technical little case has spread
so fast and so furiously into alleged de-
tails of the private life of all manner of
people that the early aspect of it threatens
to disappear altogether. At the present rate of
progress the little town where the case is being
heard will soon not be near large enough to hold
the witnesses who have come to swear to sensa-
tional things of one kind and another.
There are lots of escapes from collision at
Sydney Circular Quay which cause no fuss and
never get into the papers. As long as there is no
actual dull thud, and no bow driven five feet into
an opposing sponson, the reporter-man doesn’t
consider it his duty to make remarks ; for which
reason the public continues to plank down its
money in a joyous spirit for a passage which
may end in time, but has a good many chances
per thousand of ending in eternity. On Box-
ing Day, however, there was a very consider-
able thud, and the North Shore steamer Cammeray
sunk the Princess with such velocity that the last
of the passengers was barely dragged off in time
to save that person’s bacon. One moral stands
out pretty clearly from this and similar collisions:
no single-ended ferry-boat, which has to back out
and swivel round through crowded traffic, should
be allowed to ply from Circular Quay, The local
Marine Board is popularly understood to consist
of seven very old mummies, in a very bad state of
preservation, so nothing great in the way of reform
can be expected from it. But this very small
reform is hereby thrust under the Board’s collec-
tive nose, in the confident hope that the youngest
and least decayed mummy may be induced to
sneeze violently and wake up.

Personal Items.
A German at South Melbourne, after
solemnly declaring, the other day, that he was
tired of life, went apart with a razor and cut his
nose off. If he had been a Jew, now.
Kingston has wonderful luck in advertise-
ment. Even the man with two wooden legs
thoughtfully assaulted him at 10 a.m., in ample
time for the first editions of the evenin’ papers.
Daniel Ferron, who died lately in Castle-
main*- (Vic.), was the original discoverer of the rich
Fryerstown reef. Old Daniel, like most of the
ancient pioneers, pegged out in the Benevolent
Asylum.
Even Westralian justice wakes up now and
then and clears its character by gaoling the t’other-
sider with energy and despatch. The two Vic-
torian brothers Anderson, who lately flogged three
aboriginals to death, have both been disposed of.
One died of typhoid in Fremantle gaol while
awaiting trial, and the other gets penal servitude
for life.
H. : “I suppose the rumor that Dan Barry
wanted Graham Berry to do King Lear, on shar-
ing terms, is a funniment. In a book called
‘Pillars of the Empire,’ poor Berry is elaborately
described as one of them. What a grand thing it
is to be a pillar. Reminds you of Falstaff’s
soliloquy on honor. Who hath it ? He that was
pensioned on Wednesday.”
It is likely that poor old M‘llwraith,
now that his day is done, thinks that if he had
his time to live over again he would do many
things differently. Among others, he probably
wouldn’t boot even the humble variety of press-
mam quite so hard. M‘llwraith, in the era of
his greatness and his stupendous overdraft, never
disguised his low opinion of the ordinary news-
paper hack. And now a good many very small
hacks who, among them, might have done a good
deal to ease his downfall, are getting square with
their ancient contemner.
Henry George delivered six orations on
the day of his death. He went to bed tired and
worn out —woke up a few hours later complaining
of illness, and died of apoplexy 30 minutes after-
wards. They placed the body in state at the
Grand Central Palace, and 90,000 people came to
take a last look. There was an enormous funeral,
and then a parade at night, to which every labor
organisation in N.Y. sent a delegation, and the
police had to stop the thousands of non-official
workmen who wanted to join in. All of which
goes to show that the great idealist was not with-
out honor even in his own country, and his own
generation.
“In ’62,” writes “Three Up,” “Bill
Price faced the sand-fly and mosquito and went
Out Back to do battle with droughts, heat-waves,
sandy-blights and sundries. In camp Bill drank
strong tea and plenty of it, and at the
local shanties he never drank beer if he
could get anything stronger. Some-
times he chewed tobacco, sometimes
smoked a pipe ; but he mostly had a
pipe in one side his mouth and a
“chew” in the other. As might be
expected, it sent him under (’twas the
drink, J think), and he died near
Louth, N.S.W., the other day, aged
98 years.”
As a councillor of the munici-
pality of St. Kilda (Vic.), Premier
Turner is still the commonplace,
tinpot, suburban magnate he was
prior to entering Parliament, 10 years
ago, unaltered but for the fact that
the “ dignity ” peculiar to the munici-
pal councillor has become inflated in
proportion to his advance. The other
day, at the St. Kilda Council, he
raised a royal row about the playing
of a band on the local Esplanade on
Sundays, not from pious motives, but
because such a crowd collected that
the premyah and his “ sweet ”
couldn’t conveniently get past to
church.
Ex - bank manager Urquhart,
M.H.A. (Tas.), when in M.L. years
ago defended himself in an important law-suit
and won it. The Judge was so impressed by his
able address that he told him he ought to study
for the law. Urquhart took his advice and is
now Attorney-General of Tasmania.
G. A. Lloyd, who died in Sydney last
Saturday aged 83, was once something of a power
in cornstalk politics. He entered the Assembly
in ’69, and was Treasurer in the first Parkes
Ministry, and Minister for Mines in the second.
He was also one of the founders of the A.M.P.
Society, and in his earlier days he was a dis-
tinctly Liberal politician. Then, when he
became too old to be much use, the miser-
able nominee system shunted him into the
Council, and all that this generation remembers of
him is that he was a very feeble and conservative
and ordinary old man of no special account. The
system of appointing Councillors for life seems
specially devised to enable politicians to outlive
their reputations.
G. W. Steevens, in a London Daily Matt
article, entitled “ Under the Iron Heel,” thus
describes Kaiser Wilhelm as seen recently in
Berlin
A man of middle size, sitting constrainedly and bolt up-
right, a dead yellow skin, hard-pencilled brows, a straight,
masterful nose, lips jammed together under a dark
moustache, pointing straight upward to the whites of his
eyes ; a face at once repulsive and pathetic, so harsh and
stony was it, so grimly solemn ; a face in which no indi-
vidual feature was very dark, but which altogether was
black as thunder. He raised his gloved hand in a stiff,
mechanical salute, and turned his head impassively from
left to right, but there was no courtesy in the salute, no
light in the eye, no smile on the tight mouth for his loyal
subjects. He looked like a man without joy, without
love, without pity, without hope. He looked like a man
who had never laughed, like a man who could never
sleep. A man might wear such a face who felt himßelf
turning slowly into ice.
The eternal Chief Justice Madden has
been elected as Chancellor of Melbourne Univer-
sity in succession to Brownless. There isn’t an
honorary billet in Melbourne now, with any
dignity and any chance of wearing long petticoats
in it, that Madden doesn’t get elected to.
Hassel, manager of Olive Downs station
(N.S.W.), is considered one of the smartest and
most daring men amongst cattle in the backblocks.
He is a novelty, besides. He has never been
known to swear, and no cuss-words are allowed to
circulate on his station. Consequently his men
near choke ” at times for want of ventilation in
that respect. One old whaler, who couldn’t exist
without an occasional explosion, rode across to
Whittahranah run one Sunday, and spent half
the day on Back Creek pouring out all the im-
prisoned accumulated blanky of months. How-
ever that may be. Back Creek never holds water
now. There’s a fiery drought there.
It is announced that Dr. Cockburn is to
succeed Play ford as S.A. Agent-General. This
will leave Kingston and Holder as the two survi-
vors of the original Ministry which took office in
June, ’93. Cockburn is Scotch, 47 years of age,
and, during his 13 years of office, he has been
Premier once, Chief-Secretary twice, and twice
Minister of education. His departure from
S.A., if it really happens, will be another result
of the Adelaide Hospital row, which has been
the cause of nearly everything that has happened
m S.A. for two years past. As a member of the
Kingston Government, Cockburn is practically
ostracised by the profession which takes off your
leg for a consideration, and, as a consequence, his
practice is probably hardly worth keeping now.
An highly-ornamented tatooed relic of last-
century Maoriland went to the Happy Greenstone
Land lately at an unspeakable age from Kahia-
pohia (Kaiapoi). Paari Taki (Paul Stack as he
was christened when he ceased to be a stout pagan
and became a dubious Christian) was a great man
down South, and to show his gratitude to a Kapai
Government which made him a native assessor he
wore a bell-topper—the rest of his clothing was a
flax mat. Paari was a fighting man in the expedi-
tion oi 200 warriors which was sent up to Cook’s
Strait in days when the century was young to deal
out utu (stoush) upon Te Rauparaha after that
gory savage’s descent on Kaiapoi. There were
giants in M.L. in those days, and of these Thaia
Taihewa, Pita Te Hori, Hakopa Te Ataotu,
INatanahira Warawarautu, Horomona Pohia,
Thaia Tainui (you will find their illustrious but
discordant names graven on Kemp’s deed of June,
’4B) have “gone over” to Shad'owland. Paari,
like all of his race, dealt largely in numbers, and
was wont to tell how he was personally cognisant
of the eating of 70,000 Maoris in his early fighting
dayß.

A coincidence : In 1885 Premier King-
ston’s brother, who had been sent to gaol for
taking a hilarious pot-shot with a pistol at a pass-
ing cabman, was released “ in order that he might
enjoy his Christmas dinner at liberty.” On
Christmas-eve just past Kingston let out Jewell
for the same purpose, after seven days of hi? six
weeks’ sentence had been served. Jewell was the
man with two stumps who pounded Kingston
cheerfully on the head with a walking-stick.
S.A. law has just sent a broken-down
old miner of 60 to gaol for six months for not sup-
porting his grandchild. The said child’s father is
well-off at Broken Hill, but, as he is out of tlie
jurisdiction of the S.A. Court, and the S.A. law
is too tired to take proceedings in N.S.W., it gets
square by jugging the grandfather. Which seems
a little rough on the latter somehow.

Queer old “ Hoppy ” Hopkins, Vic.
Western District squatter, ex-Geelong Mayor
and M.L.A., has gone under, and Turner will
gather in another handsome cheque for probate
duty, unless there is truth in the vague rumor
that Hopkins suffered many financial disasters of
late years. “Hoppy” was the strange old man of
Vic. politics. _ He was eccentric and slouchy in
dress, and his clothes always looked as if they
had fallen on him from a great height. His suits
excited painful speculation in gilded ball-rooms
by reason of the fact that two-thirds of the time
they seemed to be on the point of falling off, but
Hopkins always hitched them up at the critical
moment, with a weird spasm of his long body,
and saved the situation. Hopkins had a kindly
heart, like many other unusual-lookingmen, and did
many kind actions in a certain convulsive fashion
of his own. He leaves a young widow behind
him, and his death is said to have cast a gloom
over Geelong. But ali ttle additional gloom would
not be noticeable in Geelong, anyhow.
Dear Bulletin, —Some people will per-
sistently keep up the legend that Margaret Catch-
pole is identical with the late Mrs. Reibey. If
your correspondent “G. 8.” will take the trouble
to turn to last edition of “Margaret Catch pole ”
he will find an ample apology by the author of
that book, Rev. Cobbold, for his mistake. Here
are a few comparative dates. Margaret Catch-
pole was born in Suffolk, 1773 ; arrived in Aus-
tralia, 1801 ; married , 1812. Mrs. Reibey (nee
Mary Haydock) was born in Lancashire, 12th
May, 1777 ; arrived in Australia, 1790 ; married,
1792 (at the age of 15). Her husband, Thomas
Reibey, died 1810, two years before Margaret
Catchpole was married. Of her seven children
none are living, but these are the birth dates of
her grandchildren, viz., Thomas Reibey, of En-
tally (Tas.), born 1820, and James Thomson, of
Burrier, Shoalhaven, born 24th Aug. 1824, re-
spectively 8 and 12 years after Margaret Catch-
pole’s marriage. If it is maintained that these
people are Margaret Catchpole’s grandchildren
that lady’s immediate descendants must have
married very young indeed.—T.M.T.
Captain Curling was Police Magistrate at
Napier, M.L., in 1859. Mrs. Curling “led
sassiety ” there. A strong detachment of the 65th
Regt. was quartered at Napier then, and Curling,
in the course of his duties, had to issue warrants
of arrest against two of the officers for debt. So
one morning, when Mrs. Curling read over the
list of invitations for a ball, Curling told her that
the two best dancers couldn’t come because they
were under lock and key. Then one of those
things that Wragge describes came along. When
it was over, Curling went down to his office
pledged to release the pair, and, as the only way
out of the difficulty, a document was drawn up
and signed, by which they agreed, upon their
honor, that if they were released to attend the
ball they would return to gaol immediately
afterwards. They went to the ball, where they
met the man who had jugged them; and they
kicked him out of the house and pitched him into
the lagoon. Then they went on dancing—with
Mrs. Curling chiefly, and when the ball was over
they struggled up the big hill at the back of the
town and went to their quarters in the barracks
very drunk. They were so drunk that they
forgot all about their honor. And when they got
sober they thought it was too late to remember
what they had forgotten. In the end, Curling
had to mortgage his screw for six months to pay
the debt and placate the creditor’s feelings and
soothe his bruises. Ani when, afterwards, those
two young gentlemen came to grief in the Maori
war, and one was shot dead, and the other was so
badly wounded that he had to leave the army,
Curling got drunk—with joy principally.
. Samuel Davenport, aged 79, the lone sur-
vivor of the first S.A. Ministry under Responsible
Government, still does the Adelaide block. He
was appointed Commissioner of Public Works in
March, 1857, though his Parliamentary career
started as early as ’46. Finniss, the first S A
Premier, died a few years ago.
About £2OOO has been subscribed for
the William Clarke statue, and the bronze image
of that ancient, ordinary millionaire will soon be
under way. If they want to make it a life-like
statue, however, there should be another man on
the pedestal besides Clarke, and the latter should
be lending, the other man something. No statu6
of the good-natured old man could be lifelike
unless it represented him in the act of having
something borrowed from him.

Dear Bulletin, —The visit of Lord
Ardilaun reminds me of a general election in
Dublin in the pre-ballot days. Lord A. was at
that time Sir Arthur Guinness and head of the
great brewery; he was also a candidate for the
city. A peculiarly Irish placard was posted on
every wall, reading : “ He that is not for us is
agin us (A. Guinness). —Hebrews xx.” During
same election the whiskyfied Jehu who drove me
across town (he was a freeman and entitled to a
vote) told me en passant that he had received £5
each from agents of two candidates for his vote,
and the committee for the third had paid him £7
for hire of car to block him from voting at all.
Being a trifle confused as to the morality of all
this, I asked him whom he intended voting for.
“Be jabers, yer ’anner, I axed the praste, an’ he
towld me they were all heretics, and a bad lot, and
not to vote at all, and nayther I will, blasht
them.”— Harry Monck.
Brisbane Street, a publication with a
splendid bump of language, thus discourses on a
local M.L.A.
He has the intelligence of a ’bus proprietor ;
and, when he chooses, the manners of a bear. The Street
is open to bet that he does not read six decent books in
any average six months and could not speak intelligently
for six minutes on any principle of civic management.
He is the very type of your small-minded, hide-bound,
pig-headed, out-of-date Fat Man.
Then the writer hurries on to another man and
remarks
The Street understands that Mr. Blank is shortly com-
ing to Brisbane. Mr. Blank is an unparalleled scoundrel;
and if he does come, this gentle paper will write him such
a puff biographical as shall move him to the depths. The
Street loves unutterable cads who go round the world
under a score of aliases and live on the trifles they feloni-
ously pilfer from decent people.
There’s nothing weakly circuitous about that
streak of eloquence, anyhow.
“The accused,” insinuated Judge Win-
deyer in his summing up of the Suffield case, “is
stated to be a man of education. Colonel Baker
was an educated man, which didn’t prevent him
from committing an offence similar to that with
which the accused is charged.” Which was, as
his Honor very well knew, absolutely untrue.
Col. Baker was never charged with rape’; but with
an indecent assault, about which there was not
even a suggestion of the brutality alleged against
Suffield.
The case in which J. H. Canuthers,
N.S.W. Lands Minister, sues John Norton for
criminal libel, developed an unexpected patch of
interest the other day, when Norton put into the
box a female witness who said such remarkable
things that Carruthers’s counsel asked the pre-
siding Judge not to allow publication until the
other side had been heard. The presiding Judge
(Backhouse) made the order as requested, and
established what seems to be a precedent in
criminal cases.
The publicans in a distant Westralian
mining-camp have started a bottle-breaking club
to keep the hop-beer men from using the empties
and thereby injuring their business. The rules
are very simple; so much a shot (the amount
varies according to the drunkenness of the com-
petitor), and as little as the publican can get the
winner to take for each broken bottle—the money
to be spent in liquor. There is one lady in the
club; she wildly swipes her half-brick at the
bottle as representative of her husband, who can’t
aim straight because he is blind in one eve and
cross-eyed in the other.
Ed. Bulletin, — Your par. re horse found
dead with bell-chain cut into his neck reminds
me that, back in the sixties, a bullock yarded in
Namoi district, N.S.W., had a bow and half a
yoke on, and the bow, buried slantwise in his
neck, had cut its way into the bone, the attached
half-yoke hanging down. The flesh had healed
perfectly sound, leaving only a little sore on each
side of neck where the bow went through. The
beast had been so often chased in that dense
brigalow scrub that he could gallop as safely as
any old warrior with 4ft. horns, and never strike
a tree—with the bow sticking out on one side and
the half-yoke swinging on the other !—R.F.

Aboriginalities.
Two mates left Barcaldine (Q.) for Delta,
40 miles distant, with two bottles of whisky. One,
unknown to the other, swallowed the whisky neat,
and fell off his horse 10 miles from Delta. The
more sober one made on for the station, and re-
turned next morning with a stockman to pick up
the derelict’s tracks. They found him naked,
wallowing with wild pigs in the shallow, muddy
water hole.” Had there been delay in picking up
his tracks, he would now have been pork.

Dear Bulletin, —A fortune awaits the
man who can discover a dead-sure preventive or
cure for fly-bunged eyes. Every third man you
meet m the bush, just now, has to do his seeing*
with one blinker—a fly having closed the other
for repairs. To have both lamps bunged up at
once means inexpressible suffering and total
blindness for at least three days. Although every
summer my eyes close up alternately once a week
or so, I ve never yet been able to catch the fly in
flagrante. You never feel the actual bite or sting.
An itchy sensation sets in suddenly, and imme-
diately after the eye starts swelling, and continues
swelling for a day and a half, then slowly goes
“°'Y n again to normal for the next day and a
Once the eye has begun to swell, nothing on
earth I know of can stop it. Little pain, but the
itching is almost unbearable. The general help-
°f babies and drunks makes them a verit-
able gift to the flies. Opinions differ as to the
particular kind of fly that does the mischief. Some
hold its a small, black breed, whose special
mission on earth is to bung-up eyes and die.
Others think it’s the ordinary house-fly, which,
after feasting on putrid matter, poisons the eye’
up or nose when touching it. Anyhow, to my
knowledge no one has ever yet caught or even seen
the mysterious insect. Can any of your snake or
fish authorities enlighten me?—R.H.
* * • •
Smithson is coach-driver in a West Queens-
land township. Eor many years he got tight
every journey, and never, until last week, had a
mishap. A month ago the Salvation Army got
hold of him and induced him to sign the pledge.
First fruits of sobriety—passenger with a broken
arm, two crippled horses, and a coach in ruins.
Also, writer had a friend who always imbibed
freely while travelling. Last journey per steamer
to Townsville be never touched a drop. Just as
we were stepping off the boat on to a tender,
steward rushed up to iny friend and handed him
a bundle of notes, which had been found by that
official in my friend’s bunk under the pillow.
Good God ! ’ said the astounded man, after
making the steward a handsome present, “this
is the first time I ever travelled sober, and it’s the
first time I ever made a blunder in money mat-
ters.” Moral? There isn’t any.

Dear Bulletin, I, Bartholomew Jenkins,
do vouch for the following. Years ago I was in
r-harge of a Govt, office up-country. Our water-
supply was contained in an underground tank,
nnd it began suddenly to taste horribly bad and
to smell like fury. Reported the matter, and re-
ceived orders to get tank cleaned out also to
report upon cause of aforesaid fragrance. Found
a dead cat in the reservoir in a most advanced
state of decomposition. Reported as per mandate
and received back a document from head-quarters
demanding to know categorically, “ How did the
cat get into the tank ? ”
• • • •
At Drouin (Vic.), the other day, a young
man was bitten on the wrist by a venomous snake.
The bite was cut open and a young calf was be-
guiled to suck the wound, and nearly sucked the
patient dry before he was successfully hauled off
by the tail. At latest advices the man was re-
covering, and the calf was in perfect health.
* * * *
Travelling by coach, some time ago, in
company with a stock-and-station-agent, in tha
Darling backbloeks, we were persuaded to play
“ bottle-100 ” with the driver, for a shilling a time.
The one who sees a bottle first calls “ bottle,” and,
if it is really a bottle and unbroken, the other
players pay him a shilling each. Should it be a
broken bottle or a jam-tin, or something of that
sort, the one who calls pays a shilling all round.
We had played this for some time, the driver
“getting a bit ahead of it” every mile, when I
noticed a crafty smile on his face, and decided to
be careful. I saw a square gin-bottle lying on the
roadside, but determined to pay one shilling rather
than tvyo, and kept quiet. The agent, however,
yelled “Bottle!” when the driver remarked,
“That’s a bob all round ; it’s broken.” When we
came up to it, it was apparently whole, but the
driver pulled up and asked me to turn it over. I
did so, and found a big hole in it. “ How did you
know it was broken ? ” “ Because I put it there 1 ,
broken side down, on my last trip.” We thought
we would play a little longer, although by this
time not sweet on the game. After losing about
30s. between us to the driver, we were surprised
to hear him say “ Bottle ” in a matter-of-fact
style, without looking up from his horses.
“ Where ?” we asked. “ See that bush ? There’s
a small medicine-bottle in it.” When we came
alongside, we could just see it stuck on a twig.
Said the driver, with a reminiscent look on his
face, “ I put that bottle there about eight years
ago, and I’ve done pretty well out of it, for such
a little one.” I decided I was a mug. I don’t'
play bottle-100 with coach-drivers any more.—
Balmalcolm.
* * * *
A German workman has been lying for
weeks in Perth Hospital with a wrecked spine.
He and others were working at an old brick wall,
when an alarm was given that the wall was
falling. The others ran, but the German, a man
of phenomenal strength, acting on an impulse,
set his back against the wall. “ I vos a tain fool,”
he said to his mate, when awaiting death in the
hospital, “ I thought I could prop up a brick
wall.” The writer saw a manifestation of the
same impulse once in Gippsland. A settler
falling a tree noticed as it began to go that his
brother was walking within range, and ran and put
his hands to the falling butt. Sense came to him
in time, and he jumped clear. The brother was
killed by a somersaulting limb. This same un-
reason is probably responsible for many so-called
heroic actions.

paddock. Being unable to brush the flies off the other horses, no one brushes then ff him. And,
besides being driven mad by the dying pest, he gets kicked and bitten whenever he comes near the
rest of the horses. —6 & 8.

* * * *
The aboriginal too is wonderfully prehen-
sile. Saw a charming illustration thereof in
Western Q. Nigger came up and offered to show
crowd a jumping trick. Induced bystander to
put a florin on the ground just at the aboriginal’s
foot and to place a half-crown about 4ft. in front.
Nigger jumped, landed (by mishap, of course) on
the half-crown—which couldn’t be found when
people searched. “ Buried in the sand,” said the
crowd. No —Warrigal had gripped the coin with
his big-toe joint, and was beautifully drunk an
hour afterwards.
« » » *
Dear Bulletin, —You have grievously
man-handled Young Australia for his supineness
in allowing his politics to be made in Germany,
London, and other foreign parts. I think the
reason why the imported politician flourishes like
a green bay-tree in Australia whilst the local pro-
duct is a comparative failure lies in the uncon-
vincing nature of Australian stump-oratory. And
Australian stump-oratory is dismal and lacking in
grip and go because of the great Australian
adjective. In ordinary life the Australian who
wishes to be incisive and carry conviction illumin-
ates his speech with innumerable blankies, and
his conversation goes like a cavalry charge ; but
when he arises in public to state his case and air
his opinions before a mixed audience, he has to
drop his crimson adjective, and is presently a
terrified and bewildered wretch, bushed in the
tangled ruins of ten-thousand unfinished sen-
tences. He starts out well, and gets along all
right until he strikes the place where the first gory
expletive should come in, then he suddenly recol-
lects himself, baulks badly, and the sentence goes
to pieces on his lips. This happens 25 times in 25
minutes till the audience gets tired and derisive,
and the orator’s speech, when looked back upon,
seems about as coherent as a dictionary that
has been run through a chaff-cutter. There
are only two remedies. We must sanctify blanky
and make it permissible in polite society, or else
repress it with the utmost rigor of the law, so that
the new generation may accustom itself to think-
ing without the assistance of the present indis-
pensable bloodshed.— Silas Snell.
* * * *
Squid : “ The scarcity of firewood in Maori-
land is a drawback to rural settlement in that
country. The only w.ay you can boil your billy is
to fire the grass and -follow the flame with the
billy held over it. On windy days, you have to
travel like a locomotive across miles of treacherous
ground before the requisite temperature is ob-
tained, Some of the Maoris, who are smart
runners, command a high premium as fire-chasers,
but frequently have to run so far that the water is
cold again by the time they get back.” Next !

NOTICE.— White Stab Line of Coaches.— The Owner
desires to notify that after battling through the winter
he has received, on the first day of summer, one
week’s notice to leave, the stables, and consequently
the “ White Star Coach ” will make her last trip under
the present Management on Monday night. The Pro-
prietor wishes to thank those residents who have
so loyally supported him during the eight months
the Opposition were trying to crawl into his
stables ; and leaves the Public to decide whether their
interests are best served by having “ only one ” coach
running to “only one house.”
In justice to Mr. , the Proprietor of the Line
desires to state that Mr. is also entirely exonerated
from the serious charge of having stolen a Grey
Cat from the Hotel, as the valuable feline turned up
about two hours after Mr. had started for
Kaikoura. Neither Mr. or his boss bear the Grey
Cat any ill-will, and they both trust that she may long be*
permitted to shriek at the midnight moon undisturbed by
the actions of any unprincipled adventurer.
Ohingaiti, November 9, 1897.

The ways of the country lock-up keeper
would do credit to the Heathen Chinee. When
cut firewood runs short the keeper simply looks
out for a guileless blackfellow, gets a couple of
nobblers of rum into him, and then runs him in.
Fined 10s. or seven days’ look-up. As if any
blackfellow had 10s. about him! The wood-
splitting commences at once, and lasts, with an
occasional turn at white-washing, the whole seven
days. Fact.

The twin-record. A St. Louis (U.S.A.)
woman has just given birth to her fourth consecu-
tive pair of twins. She is but 18 years of age.
Boston Medical and Surgical Journal goes
bail for the facts.
At the recent Fremantle (W. A.) municipal
elections, a one-legged candidate put up. Old
lady with one arm enters polling-booth : “I don’t
know much about electioning, but I wants to
vote for the chap what’s got one leg. ”

Holdar M. : “ Was one of a recent audience In a city
theatre, following the march of a realistic drama. I
studied surrounding faces, and marked how the mass
palpitated with pleasure upon the hero’s release from
prison, where he had been interned upon stage suspicion
compared to which that against Suffield was of heavy cir-
cumstantial calibre. Why do people throb with vitality at
the consummation of an understood effect, yet remain
deaf to the cry of real flesh and blood, praying not for
sensational release but merely for unbiased attention ?
Haresfoot : “ When Mr. Wilson Barrett’s ‘ Sign of the
Cross ’ co. was in Norwich, the representative of Nero
read the prayers in Church one Sunday morning. The
time will come when theatrical companies will be com-
pelled to attend church for advertising purposes, just as
tire coachman had to come in to prayers, which, he
hoped, would be remembered in the wages.”
Haresfoot : “ Lawrence Barrett, U.S. tragedian, re-
cently defunct, was engaged by Williamson for Australia,
ten years ago, but broke his engagement, to go on the
road with Edw’in Booth. A more legitimate type of actor
than his no-ways relative Wilson Barrett, he was yet
something like the Britisher on a lighter scale.”
N. : “The lovely' grace of dancing is carried to such
an excess as to be demoralising. It is poisonous to the
children. A horrible type of thin girl-child is created,
with pasty face and clubby hair.”
Viator: “In English provincial travelling theatrical
companies now, the agreements require actors and ac-
tresses to be well-dressed ‘both off and on the stage.’
Consequently, it is getting to be a point of honor with
stars to dress shabbily, just to show they are not common
cattle, amenable to the manager’s threats. A very similar
distinction between the china and earthenware of the
profesh is that which requires the stock actor to walk
across the stage in taking a call before the curtain,
whereas the leading people only come out and bow.”
When Dampier was touring Victoria not very long
ago the co. did a one-night season at a small
town. Next morning Alfred Rolfe was on deck
when a cordial inhabitant called in to congratu-
late him. “ You did that very well,” he said,
shaking Rolfe by the hand; “I knew when I saw you
doing it that Bill Stone must have called in and given
you a few hints.” Rolfe intimated that he was glad the
inhabitant had enjoyed the piece, but that unfortunately'
Bill Stone hadn’t called. The inhabitant was not only
surprised ; he was pained. “ Bill must be unwell,” he
decided ; “ when a new company comes here Bill always
calls on them and gives them a few hints. I don’t know
how some of them would get on without. It’s a wonder
ful advantage to a travelling company to get a few hints
from a man like Bill Stone.” “Well, who is Bill Stone ? ”
demanded Rolfe, a trifle irritably. “What? Don’t you
know him? Why, Bill’s one of the best actors in this
country. He lives just down the street. You oughter
see him act. Why', I’ve seen him go across that stage in
two jumps.” Then Rolfe, who couldn’t go.across the
stage in less than three jumps himself, apologised for his
ignorance and retired discomfited.
An envelope from Bland Holt can be picked out of a
thousand, with his back hand and the thick strokes of his
quill. The postman in Melb., Sydney, Brisbane, or all
over Maoriland, will say, “ Bland again.”

Some or the girls at the Melb. theatre-bars, in their
eagerness to push business for the show, have a painful
habit of asking, “ Are you going inside?” From a medi-
cal point of view, the question is superflous. Every man
who takes frequent doses of theatre-bar whisky' is pretty
certain to be going inside, if not already gone.
Cockaigne : “ Goddard’s ‘ Players of the Period j states'
that Mr. Wilson Barrett first appeared at Halifax, in 1864,
aged 18, so that he would now be 51. He used to live at
the Priory, St. John’s Wood, the house once occupied
by George Eliot. There is a pretty little yarn bandied in
his biogs. One winter’s night, in the snow, he reached
h >me, and found a boy shivering outside. The boy only
wanted to look at him. Barrett took him in and gave him
supper, and sent him home with all that remained for his
mother, and a bottle of wine, too. The boy was to call
at the theatre next morning for a billet as messenger.
However, Mr. Cobbe, the acting-manager, recognised
this Arab as a girl, who, in her own clothes, had asked
him for work. Presumably the pathetic maid was
mashed on Barrett. Some time afterwards the girl was
sent to prison by the oity magistrates for personating a
boy."
Mahlstick : “The Students’ Exhibition, just open at
Melb. National Art Gallery, brings out the ideal Trilby
of the trustees, the Trilby whom they probably regard
as specially adapted for the Altogezzare, as Svengarly
says. lam sorry to write disparagingly of a person who
may combine the mind of a Florence Nightingale with a
frame which I must term stringy, cow -like, and absolutely
unpicturesque. Far better to draw only from the Venuses
of Medici, Milo, the Anadyomene, and Canova.”
First nights at Melb Princess’s are religiously attended
by a man with a famous, infectious laugh. It is a valu-
able laugh to the management. On the first night of
“ The French Maid,” the maid was singing about the bad
bold lodger who called her into the bedroom and chucked
her under the chin, and when she got to the refrain —
“ What did he take me for? ” the audience was just for a
moment inclined to feel shocked. Then the laugh came
and broke up the awful silence.
Paper Cap: “Maybe the scribe who enthused over
Naphtha Joseph Dias, as the star low comedian of
eighteen hundred and fast-asleep, got it from little Joe
hisself. This was the case. Joe obtained his chance—the
only one—at Bendigo, or somewhere. It was as Toby
Twinkle, in ‘ All that Glitters is Not Gold.’ He knocked
that Bendigo audience; but, would you believe it, he
never got another show. No one now living can tell the
reason why. You need to live behind the scenes, and get
to know the hanky-panky.”
H. : “‘Olaudian’ is based on Godwin’s novel, ‘St.
Leon.’ Nobody can help observing how inferior the three
last acts are to the very strong first one, in which Willard,
as Father Clement, divided the honors with Barrett.
When the original production was a bit of a rage, Toole
appeared in a burlesque by Burnand, ‘ Paw Claude,’ in
which very subtle observers were supposed to discern
‘ Pork Lard.’ ” The exact value of “ Pork Lard ” when it
was discerned is uncertain.
Daniel Emmett, born 1815, father of negro-minstrelsy
and composer of “ Dixie,” still lives at Mount Vernon,
U.S.A. He has seen all the others pass—Foster, Bryant,
Birch, Christie. Even the original minstrel has passed
away. What we have to-day is but a variety of the
variety-show.
A London paper remarks that Boucicault — our Bouci-
cault —is no stranger at the Court Theatre, where he now
occupies the managerial chair. Some 17 years ago, when
his brother-in-law, John Clayton, was bossing the show,
Boucicault played at the Court in his ow’n little comedy
of “ My Little Girl,” taken from Besant’s novel.
Cockaigne : “ Most of Irving’s engrained stage eccen-
tricities are through defective sight. Buckstone was so
deaf that he had to watch the mouths of the other per-
formers.”
Haresfoot : “Talking of Sunday Thespic Obsequies,
never will I forget the fair Ophelia’s. Nothing impresses
the clergy so much with the deep-hid theological bent of
the theatrical profession as meeting its members at a
funeral, or discussing Scripture with an actor on a steam-
boat deck by night. If you can only get an actor or actress
to church, they are the churchiest people there. If a
civilian, so to speak, says prayers into his belltopper half-
a-minute, a Wilson Barrett will take a whole one. Well,
when Ophelia was buried, Sir Peter Teazle, the Panto-
mime Monarch, the Fairy Muslina, and the rest, quite
made me cry, too.”
The w'ife of a very toney professional man has a sister
married to a most disreputable bummer. The toney lady
excuses her husband’s relationship by explaining that the
bummer “is not my husband’s brother-in-law, he (the
bummer) is only married to my sister.”

the tending of these now’ neglected graves.—Actor.

Every man in Australia should read the
record of the Suffield case just published by The
Bulletin in a 64 pp. pamphlet, post-free 7d. in
stamps. The proceeds, after payment of ex-
penses, go to the fund for assisting Suffield to have
his case heard at ah open public enquiry.
Alb. : “Clerical celibacy is a Papal ordin-
ance, about 900 years old, and could be abrogated,
though it won't. What strikes me is the absurdity
of the Protestant opposition. The youngest and
most stalwart clergy of the Protestant sects are
kept in celibacy. The one with the best chance of
getting among the females of the flock is the
learned, spectacled, sapless little tadpole of the
craft —a pale, anaemic atomy, with feeble body and
bulbous head. The Protestant theory is, that if a
clergyman can afford it he has irresistible carnal
and sinful impulses, but if he can’t, he hasn’t.
Let sauce for the goose be sauce for the gander.”
“The Suffield Case,” a 64 pp. pamphlet
containing the evidence at the police-court and at
the trial in this notorious case, with a commentary
and plan, is now published. Post-free from
Bulletin Office, 7d. in stamps. Proceeds, after
expenses paid, go to help Suffield.

A stylishly-dressed woman called at a
certain Melbourne hospital to be treated as an
out-door patient. In the middle of the examina-
tion the doctor stopped and said: “Look here,
do you ever wash yourself ? ” Gasped the woman:
“ Yes, of course I do—on the parts that require it.”
Dr.: “ Well, go home and get a bar of soap and
wash yourself on the parts .‘•hat don’t require it
for a change. ”
Pathetic hunk from recent discourse of
Bishop Stone-Wigg, just down from New Guinea.
“We lately sent two Kanaka teachers to serve
four villages. On the day of their arrival great
festivities were proceeding, in which they joined
heartily. They discovered next day they had
unwittingly participated in a cannibal feast.' There
had been a battle with a mountain tribe, and one
of the enemy had been taken captive and used as
material for a banquet.” By the way, oughtn’t a
teacher or missionary who unwittingly participates
in such a spread to be suspended a while from his
ghostly functions—long enough, at least, to let
him get the taste of the lamented fried brother
out of his mouth?

IN AND ABOUND MELBOURNE.


A Story of Dread and Gratitude.
A tale of surpassing interest to the afflicted,
who unhappily constitute a large percentage of
humanity, could be evolved from the many and
curious expressions of gratitude which have
emanated during the past few years from sufferers
in all parts of the world, who have been made
whole through the agency of one simple dis-
covery.
What an insight do these records give us into
the miseries and agonies borne by the human
race ! Wbat splendid examples of endurance,
gratitude, and even heroism under cruel tortures ’l
What thrilling incidents of courageous struggles
with the demons of destruction, and of simple
faith in the honesty and ability of the rescuer to
save, do they afford !
h rom every quarter of the globe these personal
histories ha e been gathered, from every nation,
and from all peoples. From ice-bound wanderers
in Arctic snows, and from dwellers under Southern
palms. From the stately cities of the Old World
and the far-off corners of the great American con-
tinent ; from the vast plains of Australia, the
lonely veldts of Africa, and the coral islets of
Southern seas, Christian, Turk, and Pagan unite
in swelling the volume of gratitude which has
awakened the world to the supreme merits of ST.
JACOBS OIL. Every wind that blows carries
with it many-tongued acknowledgments of salva-
tion from physical wreck by the world’s Great
Pain Conqueror, and the experiences of the vic-
tims go to make up a story of dread, the harrow-
ing nature of which is only alleviated by the
revelations of their escapes' from the King of
Terrors through its instrumentality. The few
extracts given herewith are taken from a volume
which tells in detail the history of their sufferings
and the hopelessness of their prospects under
every other description of treatment.
Names and addresses are given as guarantees of
accuracy, and the reward of .£100, offered many
years ago by the proprietors of St. Jacobs Oil, is
still payable to any person who will prove satis-
factorily that any of the testimonials published in
the interests of this marvellous curative agent are
other than genuine. The book is opened hap-
hazard, and the record made without any attempt
at classification :
Mr. .T. Marlow, merchant tailor, of 11 Flinders-
street East, Melbourne, tried every known remedy
suggested for rheumatism, which had troubled
him for five-and-twenty years, and cost him large
sums of money. St. Jacobs Oil gave him relief on
its first application, and eventually drove the
rheumatism from his system.
Mrs. Mason, of Young-street, Redfern, cured a
severe attack of neuralgia by the application of St.
Jacobs Oil, after all other so-called remedies had
failed.
Mr. John Taylor, of Messrs. M‘Ewan and Co.,
of Melbourne, asserts, out of the depths of his own
experience, that St. Jacobs Oil is a certain cure
for lumbago and toothache. He had both badly,
and claims to know.
Mr. Maurice D. Benjamin, of Brisbane, had
grave doubts about the power of St. Jacobs Oil to
conquer rhejjmatism, but, when he had an oppor-
tunity of putting it to a personal test, he cheer-
fully acknowledged its wonderful capacity for kill-
ing pain and giving rest.
Mr. William Beach, the Australian oarsman
who lowered Hanlan’s colors, found St. Jacobs
Oil of the greatest service to him when training,
because he could always use it successfully for the
relief and cure of stiffness, cramps, and all muscu-
lar pains.
Mr. Frank Stephens, of Auckland, was lame
from an injured knee for many months. All
remedies recommended and tried were unsuccess-
ful, but St. Jacobs Oil removed every trace of pain
on a second application.
Mr. Edward Hanlan, the great American
sculler, and, for a long time, champion oarsman
of the world, strongly recommended St. Jacobs
Oil when he was in Australia, more particularly
for muscular pains in the limbs.
Mr. G. Brett, the champion amateur oarsman
of Australia, looked upon St. Jacobs Oil as
having been specially prepared to meet his suffer-
ings, and expressed the opinion that no boat-house
or training-quarters should be without it.
Mr. A. H. Jarvis, of the Hindmarsh Cricket
Club, Adelaide, constantly used it for accidents
in the cricket-field, and bears hearty testimony to
the yirtues attending its use as a remedy for all
sprains and bruises.
Mr. G. Alexander, a prominent member of an
Australian Eleven expresses his gratitude for the
services rendered by St. Jacobs Oil to the mem-
bers of the team when in England, where it was
for all accidents happening in the field.
Mr. Harry Rickards, the popular comedian and
theatrical manager, whom everybody knows,
emphasises his opinion as to the valuable proper-
ties of St. Jacobs Oil by referring to an occassion
when he was so ill with cold and pains in the
chest that he despaired of being able to appear on
the stage. He, however, used St. Jacobs Oil
freely and was in front of the footlights at the
appointed time free from all pain.
Mr. Harry Hoyte, the popular comedian, was
confined to his bed by an acute attack of rheuma-
tism, and. vainly experimented with many alleged
remedies ineffectually. Having no stage substi-
tute he was in despair, until St. Jacobs Oil was
tried, when he obtained instant relief and was
enabled to resume his professional duties.
Tiie above are but a very few of the opinions
expressed in Australia out of the enormous num-
ber that have been tendered. It would be im-
possible to reproduce them in a newspaper on
account of the space they would occupy and the
cost of insertion, but they are at all times open to
inspection.
Representative pharmacists throughout the
colonies have also cheerfully acknowledged, not
•nly the large sales effected by them, but the flat-
tering reception given to it in every instance by
their customers, whilst the leading wholesale
houses unanimously testify to its popularity and
success, following on the huge distribution of it
through their hands. The latest of these docu-
ments reads as follows :—
“Sydney, 7th October, 1897.
“ The Charles A. Yogeler Co.,
“Baltimore, U.S.A.
“ Gentlemen,
“We find by reference to our books that we
have sold, between November 30th, 1881, and
April 14th, 1897,
1,109,808 bottles
of St. Jacobs Oil.
“ It might be gratifying to you to know that no
other medical preparation that we have handled
during this period has anything like approached
the sale of St. Jacobs Oil.
“ We are, Yours faithfully,
“Elliott Bros. Ltd.”
Comment on such testimony as the above is ob-
viously unnecessary. There is no reading between
the lines ; there are no puppets to dance when the
strings are pulled. The one patent fact is that
St. Jacobs Oil is the one all-powerful conqueror of
every sort of pain, and that the public know it.*
