POINTS
Q. Labor-member Sim (to Minister of
Education Dalrymple): “ Neither you nor I have
a prepossessing appearance.”
The S.A. Govt, has practically promised to
take up next session O’Malley’s bill to legitimatise
children born out of wedlock, but whose parents
subsequently marry.
The slogan of the victorious Tammany
J»rty at New York municipal elections (among
beer saloons of the Bowery, anyhow) was
lo Hell with reform.” No beastly hypocrisy
about that.
Brisbane (Q.) Worker editor Higgs is
mvited to run against M‘Master, M.P. for
Fortitude Valley, at next Parliamentary election.
Higgs, who has energy enough to run against the
archangel Gabriel himself if necessary, has
accepted.
c< In Q. Assembly. Labor-member Stewart :
“The Legislative Council is governing the
country ; the people’s representatives in this
House are only spectres.” Labor-member Cross
(sft. high and 14st. in weight): “Do you call me
a spectre ?”
The Westralian Parliament grovelled
greatly at the “ generous action ” of Premier For-
rest m proposing a £l5O annuity for the widow of
his late adversary, Gov. Broome. Blessed is the
man who can afford to pay his respects to a lady
at the public expense.
In the last days of the Maoriland session
Premier Seddon managed to pass a common-sense
resolution to the effect that the unfinished busi-
ness of this year shall be taken up again next year
at the point where it left off. Hitherto, M.L. has
gone on the old British fool system by which
everything has to be started afresh with a new
Bession, and all the oceans of conversation gone
over again from the beginning.
Somers Vine has
spoken hopefully and
patronisingly of the de-
bilitated Federal Council.
If that institution stands
this last shock people
will begin to believe in
its enduring vitality. The
approval of Somers Vine
is generally fatal.
Official estimate of
colored population (other
than aborigines) in Q. :
Chinese, 7888; Kanakas,
8248 ; Japs, 2632 ; Jav
nese, 468; other Asiatics,
948. Does the last num-
ber include all the In-
dians, Syrians, Persians,
&c. ? It looks mighty
small. However, the
total estimate reaches
20,184.
Again stated that Q.
Premier Nelson intends
to resign his seat next
year, be appointed to
the Upper House, and
take the Presidency of the
Legislative Council after
Palmer is pensioned off.
In Q. the President of
the 1 Council is ap-
pointed, and may be re-
moved, by the Governor.
It is commonly supposed
that the President of the
Q. Leg. Council is, neces-
sarily, Acting-Governor,
but in March, 1893, a
commission was issued,
appointing Palmer Lieu-
tenant-Governor. So the
bullocky old man would
continue to reign atGovt.
House in Lamington’s
absence, even though he
vacated the Council’s
chair. But Palmer is a
poor man now, and the
presidential salary of
£lOOO a year is a very
important matter to him.
The Victorians are a good deal worried
about the insane arrangement of the railway which
has its terminus on the Murray at Cobram. Some
intelligent Government made the railway terminus
at Cobram where there is no bridge, and then it
built a bridge at Tocumwal, nine miles away,
where there is no railway. And it didn’t even
make a road between the two places, freight
going per tired bullock across a deep black-soil
plain covered with crab-holes. For which reasons
much of the language in these parts is quite un-
publishable.
In a Cue (Westralia) Sessions case lately
one of the jurors was actually also a witness.
And, after he had given his evidence, he got back
into the jury-box to well and truly hold the scales
of justice between the side he was swearing fop
and the side that was swearing against him.
The Brisbane Defence Force was asked
recently for the loan of a gun-carriage, as an
ornament to a military funeral. Reply : “ Horses
turned out to grass, and can’t possibly be avail-
able in time ” —then two days ahead. If these
horses are out at grass when the invasion
comes
The Victorian Old Age Pension Commis-
sion strongly recommends, among other things,
the establishment of a State Life Assurance
system. Even the hardest lying on the part of
one or two Tory papers can’t disguise the fact that
State Life Assurance has been a great success in
Maoriland, and the idea keeps spreading.
Now that Graham Berry has been
“ honored ” with a shabby little Government an-
nuity, the Liberals of Victoria are invited to raise
a Longmore Memorial Fund for the benefit of an-
other waning warrior. Longmore is 71 now, and
not too affluent. He and Berry are the two lone
survivors of the MacPherson Cabinet of ’69.
Russell, M.H.R., on the Maoriland Old
Age Pensions Bill : “Theimprovident poor should
not be encouraged. I knew one man who spent
.£63 in one drunken bout. Ward, M.H.R. :
“ What greater argument could you have in favor
of giving pensions, to the improvident ? That man
contributed about £45 to the revenue in one act.”J
A big fight is impending between the Ass.
crowd and Labor-party for the seat in S. Adelaide
rendered vacant by “ M'Pherson’s death. The
Labor nominee will be either Kirkpatrick, ex-
M.L.C., or Hutchison, both able men. The
latter was a fellow-apprentice of M‘Pherson’s in
England 20 years ago. The Tory nominee will
probably be ex-Mayor Shaw, of Adelaide, part-
proprietor of the famous Londonderry mine.
B. R. Wise is telling all over again in the
papers how Reid was elected leader of the Foreign-
shoddy party mainly because M’Millan, Bruce
Smith, and Sunday Brunker refused the position,
and only after it had been offered to Wise himself
and declined. Which mainly goes to show that
some men don’t know how to grab at the skirts of
opportunity when it comes along. No one would
dream of offering Sunday Brunker the leadership
now, and Ashton has succeeded to Wise’s place as
the rising hope of the Free-raid party, and Wise
himself is fast becoming only a soured elderly
young man with a grievance which he ventilates
in the papers.
“TO THE PUREETC.
PARSON (entering library): “Have you ‘ Moths’—by Ouida, I think it
is? My daughters are earnest students of entomology and
would dearly prize such a work."
Zeal (Meib.), M.L.C., on the referendum:
“Itis a most pernicious measure. It is calculated
to sweep away the Legislative Council.” The
metropolitan sewage system is also pernicious—it
is calculated to sweep away typhoid. Somehow
the writer is reminded by Zeal’s lucid remark of
an old Smeaton miner he once knew. The miner
had a large, purple wen on the summit of his
head, and was advised to go to Creswick hospital
and have it yanked off. He demurred ;he didn’t
want that wen off. Asked wh,y, he replied
surlily, “ Well, ye see, I’ve had it so long !”
A HINT TO TOBACCONISTS.
Queensland Assembly wound up the 1897
session without any passing compliments. Last
year, Keogh, as the youngest member, delivered
the usual valedictory oration about the beauty
and honesty and noble character of everybody,
and the deep debt of gratitude which the country
owed them, and so on. This year there were only
grunts.
According to G. ex-Republican Dibbs :
“ We can no more do without English capital than
we can do without air to breathe or water to
drink.” Well. England waded in and made her
own capital, and if this country can’t do likewise,
and is going to lie down helplessly all its days
calling on Bull for assistance, it had better be
obliterated.
Q. Home Secretary Tozer :
A Minister of the Crown has no time to wade through
the Gazette. I have not read it for seven years, and I
do not want to read it for seven more.
Query : If Ministers don’t read and don’t want to
read the Gazette, and Horace Tozer, as in this
case, doesn’t even know the things that appear
in it over his own signature, how does he expect
the public to be enlightened by it ?
Postmaster-General Duffy (Vic.) the other
day asked Parliament for power to stop letters
addressed to sweep-promoters outside the pro-
vince, and for a general authority to open letters
—anybody’s letters —when they were supposed, or
were said to be supposed, to be connected with
gambling business, or other illegal business, or
with practically any business at all. The idea
didn’t come off, but the mere proposal shows that
the Sabbath snuffle crowd is still a power in Vic-
toria.
Pinnock, Brisbane police-magistrate, is re-
ported as having recently told a vagrant that “ no
man need be out of employment in Brisbane. If
you don’t get work within a week I’ll give you
six months.” That callous remark, if Pinnock
was correctly reported, helps to justify any decent
Government in shunting the awful old man off the
bench. If Pinnock would disguise himself and
walk round under the name of Smith looking for
work for one week, and getting nothing but abuse
and dog-bites, he would perhaps go a little slower.
Slab of jubilee gush by Colonel Building-
Society Templeton, at the banquet given to the
Bisley rifle-team in Melbourne—the howl of a
strong liquidator in his poetical agony !
And when we gathered in St. Paul’s Churchyard and
heard the Old Hundredth rise in the air it seemed to be
the culminating point—the grand fortissimo of a cres-
cendo that had started in the far east, gathered in
strength and volume in our own Australia, and swept
over India, and up to the very feet of our Sovereign
Queen. And as we turned to sleep that night we knew
the last echoes of that hymn were dying away on the
prairies in the Far West of Canada, leaving an atmo-
sphere of j'oy and sweetness encircling the whole world
round.
Ricardo: “ Graham Berry’s jingoistic ac-
tion in regard to the New Hebrides, when Agent-
General, endeared him for ever to the hearts of
two of the most strenuous advocates of his pension
—Service, inside Parliament, and Harper, now
outside. It is understood, in such diplomatic
circles as we have, that Berry saved the New
Hebrides when Salisbury wanted to hand them
over to the French. And the New Hebrides are
as Scotch as the old ones. Service and Harper,
with the help of Rentoul, are running them
financially on strict Calvin and whisky lines, in
opposition to the blighted school of the renegade
Higginson.
Apparently Drake, of Queensland, was
only babbling through his hat when he tabled his
famous motion for a State grant to the allegedly
penniless and bedridden MCI wraith. On the face
of things, it was a challenge to Nelson to prove
that his old colleague was really in distress, but
Drake, as usual, hadn’t the courage of his opinions.
Premier Nelson offered to put the motion at the
head of the notice-paper “if the hon. member
asks me to do it,” and thus give Drake every
chance of denouncing MClwraith as a fraud and
a malingerer. But “the hon. member” didn’t
ask him to do it, and the matter dropped. Every-
thing drops more or less in Queensland.
I
Q- Govt ' has im P oBed a border-tax of
~ 2 a ton on wool in order to prevent Q. wool
from finding its ocean outlet at Sydney. But the
tax prevents very little. The south-western wool-
growers take their wool to Mungindi, pay the
border-tax there, and cart it to Moree (N.S W )
rather than attempt to carry it to Yeulba, on
the Q. rad way. The road to the latter place is so
awful that a round trip from any of the stations
concerned to Yeulba and back would take two
months at the best of times (and can’t be done for
any money just now ; teams would perish), while
the journey to Moree and back can be done
within 14 days.
Murphy, M.L.A. for Warrenheip (Vic.)
has long craved for a railway from Bungaree
Junction (in his own electorate, of course) to the
local racecourse. The Government refused on the
ground that the line would cost £3500 ; it would
only be used two days in the year, and the esti-
mated revenue was just £75 per annum. But, all
the same in the early morning, after a late
sitting, when there were 37 people in the House—
a moderately full attendance—the second reading
ot the Bill authorising Bungaree railway—where
the tram is to run twice a year—was carried by a
majority of seven. The fine old boomers, Gillies,
M In tyre, O Loghlen and co., who always vote
tor borrowing the money and building every rail-
way everywhere immediately, were with the
majority, while the Ministry cursed vainly in the
rear.
In Maoriland just now the Tory organs
that Robert Stout shall throw up the last
remnants of his old Liberalism and take Russell’s
place as Opposition leader, while at least one
Liberal organ (the Canterbury Times) suggests
that Stout shall go back to his old faith and
succeed Seddon as Liberal Premier. Why Stout
should be in such urgent demand is difficult to
understand. A.s Grey’s Attorney-General he did
nothing in particular, and as Premier he was the
head of a slipshod Government which was chiefly
notable for its ruinous deficiencies. Stout, like
Drake in Queensland and Deakin in Victoria,
acquired his reputation for ability mainly through
his oratory, and like them he is one of the etern-
ally coming men who never come. And the
coming man who has been coming ever since he
was 30 and hasn’t arrived at 53 is none too pro-
mising.
“A HIGHLAND FLING."
The Canterbury Times (Christchurch,
M.L.), a paper which has hitherto been a consis-
tent supporter of the Seddon Government, sug-
gests that Premier Seddon should go to England,
or, perhaps, be honorably kicked there, as Agent-
General ; and that Robert Stout, the hairless
teetotaller of the awful Midland Railway scandal
days, shall turn democrat again, and abjure his
new Tory friends, and become a respectable mem-
ber of political society, and the country shall for-
get what a ghastly failure he used to be as Premier
and give him another trial in that capacity. The
same paper proposes also, that Agent-General
Reeves shall come back from England and become
a member of the Stout Democratic Cabinet; and
that ex-Treasurer .Joseph Ward shall be white-
washed and made Treasurer again ; and that the
Left Wing of the Liberal party —the section which
has partially quitted the Ministry by reason of
Seddon’s autocratic methods, and which partially
straggles after Stout—shall come back to its
allegiance, and that the re-united party shall
follow Stout as it used to do Ballance. Which is
much the same as suggesting that Stout shall be
ground up in the mills of the gods and made young
again, that the old pot which was broken
shall bef "he whole once more and not be at all
weak st* '~He joint.

name of the Sarah Sands Hotel, in the Melb.
suburb of Brunswick. It was called so after a
shipwrecked vessel, long ago.”

a charge of being a vagabond, with no known fixed
or permanent residence.” Rig AULT, Prefect of
Police to the Paris Commune, 1871.

* * * *
„ Harry has retired from first-class cricket in
Melbourne, and has taken a billet at Brighton.
* * * »
Ages of prominent cricketers Briggs, 35;
Darling, 27; G. Giffen, 38; Grace, 49; S. Gregory, 27;
T. Hayward, 26 ; J. T. Hearne, 30 ; Clem. Hill, 20 ; Hirst,
20 ; Iredale, 30; E. Jones, 28; Kelly, 30; Lyons, 34;
to uf ren ’ 26 > Mason - 23 ; M‘Kibbin, 26 ; C. M‘Leod, 28 ;

m * * m
Adelaide cricketers don’t enthuse about Giffen
as much as those who are farther away. He is in wretched
batting form, and only bowling fairly. The writer would
prefer to see Noble in the team in Melb. on present form,
but Giffen might possibly be valuable on his own Adelaide
wicket in the third test-match.
* * * *
After taking Billowell over to Melbourne, ’twas
hard lines that he was not played v. Victoria, as now his
chance of being included in the test, which begins to-day,
is ml. Bill is in first-rafe form, and his bowling is much
respected and feared by a majority of the English players.
\Vnen the third test has been played and lost, it will sud-
denly be discovered that Billowell can still bowl. What
a farce cricket has become, when kissing goes by favor.
No pals—no place !
* * * *
The papers are now working up an agony over
an operation to be performed upon Ranjitsinhji’s tonsils.
If it is the removal of them, it is scarcely worth calling an
operation. The writer’s boy, 13 years of age, had his re-
moved a few weeks ago. He was promised half-a-crown
for the trick, but, upon sitting in the operating-chair,
said it was worth half-a-caser a tonsil, which was duly
promised. The doctor, after injecting cocaine, caught
hold of a tonsil with,a pair of pincers and snipped it off,
ditto the other ; the youngster received his five bob, and
there the matter ended.
* » * «
Writes X. : “ George Giffen now declines to
play on any terms in the second Test-match, as he has
not had sufficient time to practise, and is submitting an
offer for the third match. Giffen has taken a correct
stand, for it really looks as if the Australian cricketing
authorities, so long as they can collect a colossal revenue
with a Ranjifsinjhi, care mighty little as to how the
matches go. Sydney ground has been spoilt as a cricket-
field, both for players and onlookers, bj y the revenue-pro-
ducing cycle-track. Every effort should be made to win
these test-matches, even if the whole revenue derived be
expended in the attempt. Sydney' ground is now more
than half taken up with grand-stands, and a rest must
now be taken in that direction, or the crowd at a shilling
a head will have to take up its position outside the
fence.”
* * * »
One of many inane yarns re Ranji credits that
unthroned alleged highness with having undertaken to
give £5O to the “ Australian charities” the first time he
was clean-bowled in this country. Ranji has no £5O
notes to fling anyway. He’s not even a prince
when he gets to India, and lie has no revenue whatever
“on his own.” Ranji now is only a brown Pretender in
very modest circumstances, and he doesn’t even pretend
to the throne in a very active way. Also, it is hardly
large enough to be called a throne.
* • * «
Cricket runs in families. Witness the Trotts,
Trumbles, Graces, and Giffens. In Adelaide four Hills
play for one electorate club. Recently A. Hill made
149, F. Hill 68, and H. Hill 22 (not out). They are all
brothers of the great Clem, and their father was a famous
old-time Adelaide cricketer.
* * * *
Mentioned in cricket circles that a very aggres-
sive animosity exists between a well-known Australian
bowler and a member of Stoddart’s team. Both parties
are alleged to have told friends in strict confidence that
something sudden will happen to the other fellow w hen
they meet. The question is now, supposing they do meet
under favorable conditions, which fellow will the sud-
denest thing happen to ?
* * * *
The sweet girl scholars at the Presbyterian
Ladies’ College, Melbourne, have a cricket-club and prac-
tise regularly in shortened skirts and with a great excess
of pads, gloves, and other safety appliances. The vulgar
multitude'is rigorously denied a glimpse of the girls at
play on account of the superfluity of ankle in the busi-
ness, but a few pawky elders who know as much about
cricket as a mole knows of the Copernican theory stand
round giving advice. The girls are being taught cricket
because it is so English, you know, and no people are so
beastly English as the Scotch nowadays.
* • * •
The V.R.C., being about .£50,000 better off than
it was five years ago, will probably make a widespread
increase in its racing stakes for the coming year, rather
than add a huge lump of surplus to the Melbourne Cup
attraction and overlook all the minor handicaps. Pro-
bably any improvement of big events will be in the
way of swelling the prizes for second and third place
runners, a reform that would naturally make for honest
sport. And honest sport is what the Y.R.C.—like every
other Australian racing club—wants badly, very badly.
Adelaide racecourse presented an unusual sight
on Dec. 18, the first race-day after the passing of King-
ston’s Gaming Act. Bookies and tote-men fought shy of
the meeting, saving Carr, the leading Adelaide penoillor,
who attempted to dodge the new law by receiving invest-
ments and paying full dividends without commission.
Apparently the Act doesn’t reach Carr, but where the
latter’s profits come in is a mystery at present, as the
fractions over and above divs. are very small. The
absence of bookies and commission-agents increased the
tote-receipts of the club by £2OOO as compared with last
year. A ss. machine was worked for the first time at
this meeting. The 6s. tote is the last straw for Cohen;
it really looks as if the pelican-beaked race will have to
start in the General Debility business, or get into Parlia-
ment, or do something for a crust.
* • * *
Randwick brought together a proper Sydney
holiday attendance on Monday last for the A.J.C. Summer
meeting. Sammy Brass was the first fancied for the
hurdle race, but ere the flag fell the crowd discovered
Hereford, who was making his maiden efforts over
hurdles, and Mr. M‘Grath’s horse was backed down to
6to 2. Sammy Brass fell at the very first hurdle, and
Hereford won as he liked from Crysalite. For the Villiers
Stakes, Loch Marie was favorite at the short price of
3 to 1, but the public, recollecting Whakawatea’s
recent return to brilliance, backed him heavily, not-
withstanding the old horse’s 71b. penalty. Loch
Marie won in a canter, and the unmentioned Mecca
behind her. Half-a-dozen two-year-olds turned out for
the December Stakes, Essex and Lucky Boy being most
fancied. The winner turned up in Picture, who started
at 10 to 1, the two favorites finishing last. Phillip was the
first horse rushed by the public for the Summer Cup,
but so soon as it was seen Kelso meant backing his horse
in earnest, a rush was made for Tornado, and he had first
call at 6to 1. Tornado won by a bare head from Old Clo’,
who again ran a great mare. Spark, a bay gelding by
Antseus from Mondaine, won the Nursery Handicap ; and
Australian, starting at the nice price of 10 to 1, won the
A.J.C. Handicap from the favorite, Sayles. The Kelso
stable had a great day out, winning no less than three
events out of the six on the programme.
• • * *
At Randwick, on Boxing Day, loud and deep
were the execrations of thousands at the withdrawal of
several very popular and well-backed candidates for
Villiers and Summer Cup. It is high time the various
authorities passed a drastic law repressing these tactics.
If a horse be not scratched for a race 48 hours before
noon of day of race that horse should be deemed a
starter—barring the sudden death of the animal and a reli-
able medical certificate of his decease, or some equally
strong reason for his eleventh-hour withdrawal.
» * * *
At Randwick on Boxing Day the flat and
Leger were peculiarly free from balancers and spielers,
and the new registration rules answered well. Perhaps
now there is no better-managed course in the world than
Randwick ; but, to perfect matters, cannot the A.J.C.
executive just manage to issue 3d. or 6d. race-cards to
suit the pockets of the thousands who won’t run to a
“ bob ” for the elaborate race-book ?
* * * *
The defeat of the handsome little Essex in the
rich December Stakes is difficult to account for. It will
probably not be long before he will prove that the form
was all wrong by beating all the animals that finished in
front of him.
* * * *
Tatt.’s meeting commences to-day (Sat.), at
Randwick, and concludes on Monday. Sweeps on the
two principal events are bound to ensure big fields. The
Carrington sprint may fall to Precaution or El Norte (if
started in preference to Astronomer), whilst Tatt.’s Cup
seems a fair thing for Coil, next to whom may come
Voyou and Bonafide.
» * * •
Tornado (Cadogan [imp.]—Avalanche), the
winner of the Summer Cup, was bred in Queensland, and
put up a good performance there when he dead-heated
for Brisbane Cup in 3.34 J. Like that rare old ex-Queens-
lander, Battalion, he seems to improve with age, and will
assuredly be heard of again shortly.
* * * •
“ In the Cambridgeshire, Maluma was never
seen in the fighting van after the last 200vds. were entered
upon,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
“ At the finish she ran through beaten horses and was
sixth. The first six were in order, English, two Ameri-
cans, English, Irish and Australian horses, and a head
only separated the leading four. None of the six carried
over 7st. 31b., and the pace from the jump killed the
heavy guns. Comfrey at 25 to 1 was a very nice win for
111. Lon. News Ingrain on his 50th birthday—and the
‘ books.’ Joe Thompson made a * tremendous’ book, and
was a big winner. This was the first of the four chief
autumn handicaps to be won by an English horse. ‘ln
respect for her late husband ’ Mrs. Langtry was absent,
and there was really no genuine sign of the stable-money
being on Maluma.”
* * * •
Bill Martin made a meteoric descent upon
Adelaide recently with alarming results. All the cycling
officials, who regard Bill as worse than the Evil One,
immediately sought refuge in back alleys and other un-
likely places, and a detective was put on to follow
William on a bicycle. Bill was in fair form, and it was a
sight for the gods to see the ’Murkan cutting out a fast
mile with a fat, perspiring detective in the dim distance.
Then it turned out that the Plugger was over on business
re a mere libel-action and not on any bloodthirsty wallop
bent, so everyone breathed again.
* * » *
Cycling has developed a form of sweating beside
which the yoking of human beings to ploughs was com-
paratively humane. Some enterprising individual hav-
ing invented a cycle delivery dray, niggardly firms deal-
ing in small articles are doing away with their horses,
carts and drivers and, instead, hire some unfortunate
who, for a few shillings a week, has to plug round with a
cumbrous combination of a tricycle and a delivery van,
weighing 2cwt. or more, over suburban hills and against
head winds and all sorts of weather. Many of these
machines, for the sake of cheapness, are fitted with solid
tyres. So far, they are only used by firms dealing in com-
paratively light goods, but the machine is eheap and the
miserable human plugger is oheap also, so the practice is
sure to spread.
* * • •
Three “scorchers” got off scot-free in Melb.
police-court the other day, because Nicholson, P.M., did
not think the constables had “fitted themselves for the
position of taking the rate of speed, and of judging dis-
tances." In the case of a solicitor, the constable “ saw
defendant pass a tram (travelling, according to a tram
official, at 12 miles an hour) about 400 yards off, and
when he reached witness he was 200 yards ahead of it.”
Defendant pointed out that if that were true he must
have been riding 24 miles an hour. Constable: “ No,
you were riding about 16 miles an hour.” The bench
advised the police to go and “ measure off distances and
practise judging them, and not to be discouraged.” If
there is any decent show of a conviction ahead the force
will probably struggle against all the discouragement that
comes in its way.
» * * *
Melb. Herald asks Parliament to decree that
every bike shall carry an apparatus which will give
audible warning, by automatically ringing a huge bell or
otherwise, when the rider’s pace exceeds, say, 12 miles an
. hour.
* * * *
Swan, who gave Adelaide Advertiser the para-
graph out of which Bilmartin obtained £5O damages
(the costs of the paper being £lOO more), stuck to his
story in court to such an extent that he has now been
committed for trial for perjury. Said story was to the
effect that Bilmartin and another man had a very willing
scrap in a retired corner, and that the Plugger was
banged into a state of ridiculous wreck.
* * * *
The fiasco which the grass-track falls made of
the last Austral has stirred up the League of Vic. Wheel-
men. A committee has been appointed to “take evi-
dence as to the safety or otherwise of the M.C.C. Ground
as a cycling track,’’ and on the report submitted will de-
pend whether or not the league will sanction future Aus-
trals there. Presumably some of the riders who fell dur-
ing the two Austral days will be called to give evidence.
* * » *
It was officially announced at the last meeting
of the Vic. League of Wheelmen that its net credit
balance was only £546 2s. 5d., exclusive of furniture and
such. One member advised caution in order to avoid
insolvency, and the expenditure of £BO on finger-posts
was, among other things, “ deferred.”
• * * «
J. G. Ward, of M.L. Parliament, has drafted a
Cycling Traffic Bill with the object of imposing a license
fee of ss. per year, the proceeds to be expended in laying
down cycle tracks and improving the highways.
• * * »
Beyond providing some casual hanger-on of
the game with a free trip ’Ome to dear hold Hingland as
manager, the sending of an Australian team of athletes
to the other end of the world can serve no useful or sen-
sible purpose. In the first place, it would be almost im-
possible to get a representative crowd ; in the next, even
the best team would be donkey-licked. The Maorilanders
who went on a similar errand, a few years ago, fared
badly, and, certainly, Hempton and co. were far
superior to any combination that could now be raked
together here. Barring Roseingrave in the long jump,
not one of the proposed lot would score, and it is doubt-
ful whether an attack of fever, some time ago, has not
left the usual after-effect on Roseingrave.
* * * »
On the eve of the races of a W.A. town, re-
cently, an imported gaby, having bought a particular
horse’s chance in a Calcutta sweep, hastened off to the
owner’s place with about 10ft. of rope, and requested the
stable-boy to hand over the moke forthwith, “because
I’ve just bought him for £2. 10s.” Stable-boy—after re-
covering his breath —“ Oh, orl right. The boss is just
goin’ to put ’im in the dray to cart the milk to the lunatic
asylum- an’ I dessay ’e’ll give yer a lift. ”
* * » *
The “ special correspondent ” of a Sydney
sporting-paper says Peter Jackson, in appearance, looks
as well as ever he did, dressed immaculately in frock-coat
and silk-hat.” “ Our own correspondent ” of a Melb.
ditto ditto says, “Peter Jackson, in New' York, was
drunk all the time, and is keeping it up at ’Frisco.” Hard-
swearing on one side or the other,
» * * *
John L. Sullivan is now a white-haired man
with a shaven upper lip and elderly mutton-chop whiskers,
and his only ambition is to settle down into a municipal
billet. To which end he has encased his bull neck in a
tall collar of the most respectable shape, and he probably
refrains from drinking whisky-hot before luncheon.
When Jonel came to Australia with a dramatic show he
was a “ whale ” for whisky-hot before luncheon.
• * * •
The English Amateur Rowing Association has
liberalised to the infinitesimal extent of deciding that a
boat builder is not necessarily a pro. If, however, he
builds or lets boats for “ money or wages,” he is barred
along with the common manual laborer, which term is
understood to include the counterjumper, the tailor, and
the man who rows with bare arms. Still, it isn’t Austra-
lia’s place to poke borak when its own shamateurs decide
that professional cyclists and axemen are not amateur
oarsmen, whilst professional yachtsmen and pigeon-shots
(and pigeon-shooting is, perhaps, the cronkest and most
debasing of all sports) are to be welcomed with open arms.

Government Notices.
Health Department of N.S.W.,
Sydney, December 24th, 1897.
The following particulars in connection with the NOTI-
FICATION OF INFECTIOUS DISEASES under the
Public Health Act, 1896, Part 111., from the FIRST DAY
OF JANUARY, 1898, are published for the information of
the medical profession, and of the public generally.
Forms for the notification by MEDICAL MEN of the
diseases named are to be obtained, free of charge, on
application to the Local Authorities of the Districts
WITHIN WHICH THEY RESIDE.
By Order of the Board of Health.
O. A. SIMMS,
Secretary.
PROCLAMATION.
New South Wales,
to wit.
By His Excellency The Honourable
Sir Frederick Matthew Harley,
(L.S.)
Fkedk. M. Darley,
Lieutenant-Gevernor.
Knight Commander of the Most
Distinguished Order of Saint
Michael and Saint George, Lieu-
tenant-Governor of the Colony of
New South Wales and its Depen-
dencies.
"W7HEREAS by an Act passed in the sixtieth year of the
’ ’ reign of Her present Majesty, intituled the “ Public
Health Act, 1896,” it is enacted by section 20 that the
Governor may, by Proclamation, declare that any disease
therein named is an infectious disease within the meaning
of the said Act: And whereas it is deemed expedient that
the following diseases be declared infectious diseases
within the meaning of the said Act, namely :
Scarlet Fever.
Diphtheria and Membranous Croup.
Typhoid Fever.
Now, therefore, I, Sir Frederick Matthew Barley, the
Lieutenant-Governor aforesaid, with the advice of the
Executive Council, do hereby declare the abovementioned
diseases to be infectious diseases within the meaning of
and for the purposes of the said Act, on and after the first
day of January, one thousand eight hundred and ninety-
eight.
Given under my Hand and Seal, at Government
House, Sydney, this fourteenth day of December,
in the year of our Lord one thousand eight hun-
dred and ninety-seven, and in the sixty-first year
of Her Majesty’s Reign.
By His Excellency’s Command,
G. H. REID.
GOD SAVE THE QUEEN !
SMALL-POX and LEPROSY.—The notification of these
two diseases is provided for in their respective Acts,
namely, 45 Vic., No. 25 ; and 54 Vic., No. 20.
The Treasury, New South Wales,
10th December, 1897.
T’HE following Regulations for carrying into effect the
A provisions of the “ Public Health Act, 1896,” having
been approved by His Excellency the Lieutenant-Governor
and the Executive Council, are published in accordance
with the requirements of that Act.
G. H. REID.
GOVERNOR’S REGULATIONS UNDER “PUBLIC
HEALTH ACT, 1896.”
Part 111.
1. The form of certificate for notification of cases of
infectious disease shall be the form in Schedule A.
2. Medical officers to public institutions shall, in addi-
tion to giving the address at which the case is, give the
address whence it was removed.
3. The Local Authority on receiving a notification certifi-
cate signed by a legally qualified medical practitioner, after
ascertaining that the house referred to in it is within the
district of the said Local Authority, shall copy the certifi-
cate in the prescribed register, and shall then transmit it
by the first post to the Secretary to the Board of Health.
4. If the certificate has been received by the wrong
Local Authority it shall be forthwith sent by the said
Local Authority to the Local Authority within whose dis-
trict the house named in it is situated.
5. Legally qualified medical practitioners shall prepare
their claims for fees in the usual official voucher forms,
and shall state therein the name of the district in respect
of which each claim is made, the date of report, and the
patient’s name, and shall forward Jsuch vouchers to the
Secretary to the Board of Health at the close of each
month.
6. The form of register to be kept by the Local
Authority for a Municipal District shall be the form in
Schedule B.
7. The form of register to be kept by the Local
Authority for a Police district shall be the form in
Schedule G.
8. When the case of infectious disease notified is that of
a person of school age, the Local Authority shall forth-
with notify the head teacher of the school usually attended
by such person, in the form in Schedule D.
9. The Local Authority shall deliver at every premises
on which the presence of a case of infectious disease has
been notified, the notice ordered to be given in section 26
of the Act in the form in Schedule E.
10. The form in which Registrars of Deaths shall notify
the Board of Health that they have registered a death
ascribed to an infectious disease shall be the form in
Schedule F.
11. The owner or occupier of infected premises on
whom notice has been served by a Local Authority to
cleanse and disinfect the same or part thereof, and to dis-
infect or destroy infected articles thereon, who has in-
formed the Local Authority that he will carry out the said
cleansing and disinfecting himself, shall, if he fails to com-
plete it to the satisfaction of a legally qualified medical
practitioner, as certified by the latter to the Local
Authority in writing, within the time named in the said
notice, be liable to a penalty not exceeding (£2O) twenty
pounds.
12. The body of every person who has died of an in-
fectious disease shall be wrapped as soon as may be after
death in a wrapper so as to envelope it completely, which
wrapper shall be wet with a solution made by mixing five
parts of carbolic acid with 95 parts of water.
13. Every such body shall be coffined, and the lid of the
coffin shall be permanently fastened down, within 24
hours after death.
14. The joints of the lower part of every coffin used to
contain the body of a person who has died of an infectious
disease shall be watertight.
15. Any person wilfully offending against any of the
three last preceding regulations shall be liable to a penaltr
not exceeding (£6) pounds. '

Religion Condensed.
Dr. Betz, of Heilbronn, finds that acne
rosacea (red nose and cheekbones due to drink)
fades right away, if the patches be well rubbed
night and morning with oil of turpentine. So
says the Journal des Praticiens.
North Otago Times (Oamaru, M.L.),
reporting a local function, remarks :—“ Several of
the names in the foregoing list have been
omitted.” Which is about as beautiful as that
historical Irishism : “ I hit him once and missed
him ; then I hit him again in the same place.”
A W.A. teamster agreed to take prospec-
tors out to a new find for so much a head, and with
each lcwt. of baggage. On the day fixed for
starting, each man's lot was weighed and packed
up, but it so happened that the team did not start
till next morning, and the load was found to have
grown considerably during the night. At the end
of the journey, the teamster pulled up at the local
store and borrowed the scales. “ Now,” said he,
“each man weigh bis hundred; anything over,
he’ll have to pay me Is. a pound for or forfeit.”
And, as most of the passengers were hard-up, that
carter had about two tons of provisions for sale
cheap.
A “Sailor Man ” :
There was lately an Italian barque at a wharf in an
Australian metropolis, from whence proceeded so sicken-
ing an odor of bilge-water that anyone who went near her
could easily be seasick on land. It was glorious to see
her crew at their mid-day meal on deck. The cook
brought out the lobscouse, or whatever it might be, in a
big bowl, and the men squatted around anyhow, and
helped themselves promiscuously with each other’s slob-
bered sheath-knife, or spoon, or other utensil. On the
day before her departure the cook braced himself up for
his annual wash —and performed his ablutions in the
mess-bowl. Just as he was dressing the dinner-hour
struck, and the men came trooping forward for their grub.
Cook ran to the side with the bowl, pitched out the slops,
and, ten seconds later, had the feast before them in his
late washing-tub.
Some gruesome speculations :
Sydney pawn-shop windows are full of second-hand
teeth—some from the dead, no doubt, others from the
living. In the latter case hope must be far from the
breast when people conclude that, as they have nothing
for the teeth to do, they may as well pawn them and
take a drink. But who buys them ? Like the opera-
glasses, violins, revolvers and concertinas, always on view
in pawn-shops, they seem to be put there for ornament,
as no one ever seems to want any. It is said they are
worked up by dentists into new sets. If so, it must be
consoling to the millionaire who has a false set to reflect,
when he is getting in his fine work on turkey and trufrles,
that the very same grinders he is using on such dainties
once watered at the smell of a hot saveloy, or gnawed a
chop-bone at “the eight o’clock rush.” And how the
lover must enjoy a kiss from the lips of his Potts Point
darling whose new set may have been bathed in the gin-
and-cloves loved by their former ow ner—the pride of the
Chow’s harem ; or they may be the same ivories that
have been alternately lipped and bashed by the “bloke”
when she wouldn’t give him up all of her sinful earnings.
They are, indeed, false teeth.

DRAPERY GOODS of the value of £2 and
upwards that can go by Parcels Post, with the ex-
ception of certain heavy or bulky low-priced lines,
are sent by us to any Parcel Post Office in the

During Closing-Out Sale it is often useless to order from
samples. When once lines are sold, they cannot be re-
placed. In sending samples, therefore, a first, second,
and third choice should be made in each instance, and, in
the event of all being sold, authority should be given for
us to substitute the nearest. In substituting goods, our
best judgment would be used in selecting the nearest, both
as regards pattern and price.

Viator: “St. Patrick’s, Melb., though
bolder, and infinitely, more effective in situation,
recalls Canterbury Cathedral more than any other.

Dear Bulletin, —How’s this for a bit of
solid butting: My uncle (living near Hamley
Bridge, S.A.) owns some half-dozen pet sheep and
a crossbred merino ram, usually called “ Billy.”
Not having a rival in the neighborhood to try
conclusions with, Billy attacked an English
waggon in the shed, and drove the hind part 2ft.
out of the straight line. The waggon then became
locked against the front wheel, and I suppose
Billy thought he’d had enough of it. The nave of
the hind wheel, which was the part assaulted, bore
marks as if it had been struck with a sledge-
hammer. The waggon weighs 18cwt. Billy is all
right.—W.O.R.
R. : “ All over Australia an idea exists
that human fat has special medicinal virtues,
giving extra strength, extra fighting power, extra
resistance to disease, and the like. Apropos hereof
a quotation from the Practitioner (p. 505-6,
Nov. 1897) may come in. ‘ Human fat was also
in demand for embrocations to be applied in
rheumatism. This appears to have been one of
the perquisites of the hangman, who doubtless
made a good profit out of it. At any rate the
commercial value of the article was sufficiently
great to make it worth while to produce artificial
imitations of it. Pomet says—' Everyone knows
in Paris that the public executioner sells it, the
druggists and apothecaries a little; nevertheless
they vend a sort of it prepared with aromatic
herbs, and which is without comparison better
than that which comes from the hands of the
hangman.’ The trade in human fat continued at
least till the middle of the eighteenth century.
In the Universal Spectator and Weekly
Journal of May 20th, 1732, is a reference to the
case of a man imprisoned for plundering dead
bodies of their fat.”
“ Fatman ” sends The Bulletin a neat
and genial little rhyme :
I love the noble working-man
Who slaves so hard for littl? pelf,
Yet always has a private shelf
Whereon he keeps his boozing-can.
I dearly love the rabid ghost,
The honest, ’earty, ’orney-’and,
Who has an awful down on “ land,” j
But hates the sisrht of water most.
I love the humble, simple son
Of toil, who, in domestic life,
Prefers to jump upon his wife
To any other kind of fun.
I love the poor, down-trodden lout,
Who never gets a chance to speak,
Jest ’cos of that there wealthy clique,
Yet’s always sponging for a “ shout."
« love the grimy Democrat
Who always yahs at propertee
As so much “ dirt,” what time that he
Has half the earth beneath his hat.
I’m always longinc to embrace
The cove who, when you can’t agree
With him, just curses loud and free,
And spits tobacco in your face.
And when at certain times I find
I’ve got to take his hand and shake it,
It always makes me feel inclined
To fall upon his neck and—break it.

A Wimmera paper tells the following
story:—
“ Up in prohibition Mildura, before the introduction of
the club system, and when it was really hard to get a
liquor, an enterprising barber opened shop. His charges
were fixed at shaving 9d., haircutting Is. with a glass of
strong thrown in. He would give a customer any
drink i. . :o whisky. The scheme caught on
splendidly. Visitors marvelled, and used to remark what
a clean shaven place Mildura was. The community soon
began to look as if it had been struck bald-headed.
Strangers not in the know used to-wonder why a man
got his hair out half a dozen times a day, or almost had
the skin shaved off his face. Rechabites took to wearing
beards, and letting their hair grow long. If one was seen
with a close crop, or newly shaven, scoffers would ask
him if it had been ‘wine crop,’ or ‘whisky shave.’ But
Mildura knows that barber no more. The clubs simply
ruined him, for the people can now get liquor ad lib
without having their hair cut every time.

Suffield is still in his living grave, and the
N.S.W. Justice Department is jumping on him
with all its feet to make him fit that grave.
Suffield is a victim not only of Windeyerism, but
of Government by Contradiction. Both N.S.W.
political gangs, the ins and the outs, are interested
in justifying the actions of their respective Minis-
ters of Justice. A pamphlet fully dealing with
this monstrous wrong has just been published.
Post-free from The Bulletin office for 7d. in
stamps.
Dr. Stephen H. Emmens, the Yankee
chemist, who invented the explosive, “emmen-
ite,” adopted by the U.S. for coast defence, now
claims to have found how to make gold out of
silver. Says he has sold an ingot of it to the
“Bureau of Assays” for coining purposes. He
keeps his secret for the present, except to state
that by a chemical treatment of the silver he pro-
duces an intermediary metal, “ argentaurum,”
then by a further treatment he obtains the gold.
One drawback about it at present is that the arti-
ficial gold is so expensive that, like the diamond,
it can only be manfactured at a slight loss.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opJ direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

LA6ER B€€R.
The Family Skeleton.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a carriage by the gateway, there’s a flun-
key at the door,
There’s another cooing softly to the satin-coated
bays ;
There’s “an architectural poem,” as the papers
said before,
When I built my swagger villa in the boasting,
booming days.
On the lawn a leaping naiad holds a fountain to
the sky ;
There are marbles made to order in Genoa years
ago,
And my wife goes down the terrace in the best
that gold can buy
To complete the striking tableau—meaning
Somerville and 00.
Neatly tailored, smartly hatted, step up Somer-
ville and Co.
Fourteen stone, five-foot-eleven, sound of limb,
as good as new ;
Rich as Croesus, so they tell me, when my wines
unstinted flow,
And the eyes of envy stab me in the market and
the pew.
Silk hats rev’rently salute me, doing homage mete
to cash,
And the rose-leaf lips of maidens smile a greet-
ing sweet to see,
For I hold the whip securely, and the world spins
n’eath the lash,
And a grinning, lanky goblin makes a hollow
sport of me !
Bare and bleached is he—his sapless joints like
rusted hinges creak,
And a wisp of hair clings quaintly to his bony,
hollow skull.
In my wife’s eyes lurks the terror that her lips re-
fuse to speak,
But he is my dear, damned brother, and we two
are never dull.
In our home he bides contentedly ; its beauty
pleases him —
He exults in lavish splendor, and he loves to
come and sit
On an old black carven bureau when the lamps are
burning dim, *
Making mouths for me, and mumming like a
jester from the Pit.
When the waltzers swing together, and the lips of
women glow,
And the low, glad laughter lingers ’mid the
blossom-burdened trees,
He is barred and double-bolted in his cupboard
down below,
And my girl and I look happy, and are almost
at our ease.
But I’d rather be alone with him. My den is dark
and small.
Up I take him when the guests are gone, and
give a curse to care,
For the sweet, old mellow whisky makes him
human, after all,
And we fraternise, and gaily we get drunk
together there.
One time Kate and I, to fool him (God! I love her
and forget!)
In the moaning winter evenings tried the old
home custom o’er;
Sitting snugly in the firelight, read, as ’twere a
lesson set,
But he crept up through a crevice, and he
capered on the floor.
Then the story was forgotten, and I glowered on
the flame,
Whilst she wept, crouched low in terror with
her face upon my knee.
That was long ere we were comrades, ere this
fierce abandon came,
And I knew the poignant humor of his hell-
born comedy.
Now he roams the whole house over—we alone
can see him there.
When I come at eve he answers to the summons
of the bell;
Waking strangely in the night, I hear his footstep
on the stair,
But I know he’ll never leave me, we’ll foregather
deep in hell.
I had once a child, and loved her, but her eyes
grew wild and old—
Oh, my God ! the crazy laughter of a babe ! —it
haunts me still!
But the spectre wearied shortly, and he stretched
her, white and cold,
And I blessed him and was grateful for the
mercy that could kill.
Things are rosy in the city, wondrous rumors go
the rounds
Of the clever coups that Somerville and 00.
have made of late.
We’ve just worked a little corner good for thirty
thousand pounds,
And the firm and wife are dining with the
Governor at eight.
It is sport, all sport for him, and when at last he
tires, I swear,
He will take me by the arm one day; exposed
beneath the sky
We will walk the Block together, see the blank,
white faces stare,
And, ’fore God, I cannot say who’ll laugh the
louder, he or I.
EDWARD DYSON.
The School at Sergeant's
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of all the small bush schools I ever had
the misfortune to be in charge of, I think with
least regret of the school at Sergeant’s. And this,
with other things, leads me to say that the experi-
ence of teachers does not vary so much with the
schools as the places they board at. Practically,
the schools are alike—little wooden boxes with
iron roofs—cold as Kiandra in winter and hot as
Booligal in summer. Nowadays the buildings are
even more uniform—built to style, as it were ; but
in my day the only difference was in the width of
the cracks. The children are always the same—
God forgive them ! —the results never vary ; and
the same loneliness pervades all. But the chief
difference in the place you board at is that each is
the worst. All except Sergeant’s.
* * * *
Old Sergeant was one of the finest men I
ever saw—six-feet-two at sixty-three ! A rugged
and seamed old face, clean-shaven but for a fringe
of white hair about his neck; eyes clear as a child’s;
and a mouth that expressed determination except
when he was in company and couldn’t get rid of
the superfluous saliva engendered.by his inactivity.
He was a rough old customer, with many quaint
and truthful sayings—more or less concerning
women, and more or less unprintable. But his
veneration for his wife was almost pathetic. It
showed in his speech. With a total disregard for
grammar, it was, “ When me and Tom was on
Lambing Flat,” or “ The time me and Bill Wade
was overlandin’,” but every reference to her was
“Mother and me”; and “Wasn’t it, mother?”
O omprised half of his yarns.
* * * *
The family were all married ; and, in the
long winter evenings, after my solitary tea —my
mouth waters even now at the memory of those
preserves—l would go into the kitchen and find
the old woman knitting his socks, and old Ser-
geant making picture-frames of fruit-stones and
cones. A steaming glue-pot by his side, and pliers
in his hand, he cracked the nuts and fitted the
pieces with such minute care that to cover three
square inches of frame was a long night’s work.
After a while, I drew designs for him to pick out
with different kinds of stones, and even essayed
one myself—that was picked to pieces in more
ways than one. And then, in front of the log fire,
he would tell stories of the old time, or I would
play the fiddle.
* * * *
He would never let me play my best piece
—“ Home, Sweet Home ” —because : “ When me
and Tom was on Araluen—that was before mother
and me took up, wasn’t it, mother ? we got word
that a great fiddler, called Something-Whisky, was
cornin’. There was a bit of a hall at the pub.,
and me and Tom was often there, doin’ a
bit of steppin’ and such. Me and Tom was
counted the best steppers on the diggin’s—
leastways, Tom always played,” he added, with
characteristic modesty. “ Well, Whisky took the
hall, and we all dressed up to take out our ten
bobs’ worth ; and, my word, there was a mixed
crowd there—wasn’t there, mother ? Well,
Whisky came out, and as soon as he starts we
could see he was a fine player; but there-was too
much shakes and such for us. He ran up and
down like winkin’ till big Tom M'Grath—you re
member Tom, mother?—stands up on a seat and
says : * I move Jack Sergeant does a step.’ That
was me; and before old Whisky knew where he
was I was at it. Whisky stood on one side
pattin’ his fiddle and speakin’ some language to
himself, while I gave a new step me and Tom
had been practisin’ on the quiet. Well,
of course, there was drinks; but no one re-
membered old Whisky, till he ups and
starts playin’ ‘ Home, Sweet Home,’ just plain
like. Sometimes now, when I’m lyin’ in bed, it
comes to me ; don’t it, mother? Well, you never
heard a lot of fellows cough and clear their
throats like we did. After that we cleared the
stage, and made him promise only to play plain
tunes we knew, and then we all goes out and paid
afresh to come in ; didn’t we, mother ? ”
* * * •
When the old woman died I offered to
move, but old Sergeant wouldn’t hear of it. -‘You
stay along with me and we’ll rub along—you was
a favorite of hers.” One night he selected one
from a number of tombstone photographs. and
asked me to write the inscription. He looked
over my shoulder and spelled the words as I wrote
“ Sacred to the memory of Jane the beloved wife
of— ” Then he stopped me. “ What’s wrong? *'
I asked. “ Well,” he said, “ her and I was never
properly married ; best rub out wife and put help-
mate. She was that— true—wasn’t you, mother ? ”
said the old man, dreamily.

The Australian.
(New South Wales variety.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His clothes are West of England tweed,
His boots are from the Strand,
The bike which he propels with speed
Was made in Yankeeland.
He drinks a glass of Belgian gin—
Jamaica rum, perchance—
And smokes the “ best Virginia ” in
A pipe that’s “ Made in France! ”
He looks at his imported watch to see the time of
day
And hurries, for he wants to see a new, imported
play.
The lamp is “ Made in Germany ” that lights him
on his way.
He’s a patriotic, thoroughbred Australian !
He’s a patriotic, thoroughbred Australian !
And he sticks up for his country like a man !
For it’s good for growing mutton,
But it couldn’t make a button
For the trousers of a true Australi-an !
He comes up to his cottage, where
There’s lager from the Rhine,
And seats himself upon a chair
Of Austrian design !
His English hat he places on
The Chinese cheffomer,
And drinks from his Italian glass
His German lager beer !
He strikes Italian matches and he lights the
German lamp;
He sees the jam and pickles have the real im-
ported stamp;
He tries the Dutch piano for the latest foreign
vamp—
He’s a patriotic, thoroughbred Australian !
He’s a patriotic, thoroughbred Australian !
And he sticks up for his country when he can.
It’s the champion for cricket
When MTCibbin beats the wicket ;
That’s sufficient for the true Australi-an 1
The Chinese washstand in his room
Is near the Russian rug;
He fills the ’Murkan basin from
The German water-jug ;
He takes his German razor
And he shaves himself with ease }
He reaches for the towel-stand —
The latest—Japanese !
With Baris soap he washes off Australian dust
and dirt,
Puts on his Irish collar and his English under-
shirt ;
He laces up his London boots, which very seldom
hurt
A patriotic, thoroughbred Australian !
He’s a patriotic, thoroughbred Australian !
And he sticks up for his country like a man !
And he buys of all creation
But the land of his location,
He’s a thorough-going, true Austral-ian !
Orange.
W. T. GOODGE.
Another “Light That Failed.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A gaolj in Borneo ; on a syn-baked patch of clay
stood the triangles, near which were a group of
half a dozen —the doctor, the jailer, the hospital
dresser, the native warder or mandore, and his
two assistants. At the back of the group stood
the gallows, from the cross-beam of which dangled
the fraying ends of three ropes, “the trinity,” as
the Doctor sneeringly called them, from which, on
the previous day, had dangled and been cut down
the emancipated bodies of as many natives. In
front, the grim black prison wall. To the left the
women’s ward, in front of which a few unfortun-
ates strutted, in a vain endeavour to catch the
sympathy of some member of the group with
wiles they had exercised in their youth. To the
right was the gaol hospital, in front of which a
few miserable vagrants were apathetically watch-
ing a couple of good-conduct men tying up in
mats the bodies of those of their companions
whom Providence had mercifully released from
suffering.
The door of the main prison opened ; through it
came Mandore Nyam, the hangman, followed by
a dozen or so of coolies, after whom were half that
number of Pathan guards.
The corporal of the guard saluted, and he and
his men fell back ; the doctor lit his pipe; the
dressers began smearing the lint with vaseline
and iodoform, while Mandore Nyam, who had
relieved the warder of his canes, was squatting
close to the ground and cutting deep grooves in
the clay with his forceful and accurately delivered
blows. The prisoners had, meantime, stripped,
and were waiting in misery their turns. The
warder handled a bundle of warrants to the gaoler,
and the later stepped forward, and, in quick,
careless style, told the first man in the row, Suli-
man, that, having been convicted of a breach of
the Labour Ordinance, in leaving the estate on
which he worked without the manager’s permis-
sion, he had been sentenced to 50 cuts; had he
anything to say ?
Suliman, the wreck of a fine specimen of a
native, stepped out of the rank, and with his
hand to his forehead said, “ Tuan, I left the estate
to complain of my master’s treatment of me. He
overworked me till I nearly died of beri-beri, and
when too ill to work or defend myself or her, he
ruined Russina, my daughter. I could get no
hearing from anyone there, so I left the estate to
see if anyone in the town would listen. I take
my 50 strokes, if Allah spare me, willingly, if I
can but feel that my daughter’s ruin will be
avenged. Tuan, I am ready.” And Suliman
walked up to the triangles.
.“L n this pipe!” said the doctor, as he
tried again to light it.
“D n that fool of a coolie ! Just as though a
daughter of his being taken up by Sayers, was
going to get him off a flogging for clearing out to
complain, as he calls it. Ready, Doc. ? ”
“Yes, goon, Nyam.” This from the Doctor,
who, with his mouth to his pipe, and his ear to a
stethoscope, had been reckoning how many cuts
the prisoner could stand. Suliman was soon fast
to the triangles. Nyam flicked him playfully
with the end of five feet of rotan, as he planted
his feet firmly and waited for the signal. The
gaoler raised his hand ; Nyam drew back ; the
swish of the rotan through the air was followed by
a dull “ tack ” as it came with frightful force
against Suliman’s naked flesh. Suliman gave no
sign of pain, and the Mandore slowly counted
“satu.” Again was this repeated, and again till
the gaoler dropped his hand when the Mandore
counted “sapuloh.”
The Doctor reluctantly interrupted his yarn to
the gaoler, and cast a look towards Suliman.
“ Go on,” he said, as he resumed his yarn.
Again the Mandore said “ talli,” and the gaoler
noted twenty on the back of the warrant.
A nod from the Doctor started Nyam on his
third ten. The first cut drew blood, but no sound
came from Suliman.
“ Dua,’’ said the Mandore aloud, and an atten-
tive listener might have heard a faint moan from
the poor man’s lips, mingling with a snigger from
the Doctor, as he recounted some scandal which
had come to his professional ears.
“ ‘ Ampat; ’ ‘ lima ; ’ ‘ anam; ’ ” and the moans
grew more pronounced; “ ‘tujoh ; ’ ‘delapan;
‘simbilan;’ ‘sapulah;’ that is thirty,” said the
Mandore. The quivering, bleeding, length of
emancipation gave one piteous glance towards the
Doctor, who merely signed with the stem of his
pipe to go on. Again were the cuts from one to
ten, from “satu ” to “sapuloh,” monotonously re-
corded in a droning sing-song voice by the Man-
dore. Once more the Mandore awaited a sign
from the Doctor to go on. This time it was
longer coming ; the pipe would not draw. “ Might
sound him again, Doc., eh ? ” asked the gaoler,
“he begins to look bad on it.” “ Right, when
my pipe is alight,” said the Doctor, as he struck
his last match, signing at the same time for the
Mandore to go on.
“Another light that failed,” laughed he as the
action put out the match, “ the beggar can wait
till next ‘ talli ’ for stethoscoping—and, meantime,
give me a light off your cigar.”
“Satu,” counted the Mandore, “dua, tiga, am-
pat, lima,” and the moans, which had become
more and more audible, now ceased ; the Mandore
almost lost count in his vain endeavour to attract
the gaoler’s attention. That functionary was too
busy recounting to the Doctor his not too reput-
able escapade with the wife of a certain big
official, to pay attention to the moans of a coolie,
and Nyam stopped of his own accord when the
Mandore counted “sapuloh.
With a laugh at the last coarse jest, the Doctor
drew out his stethoscope and listened. “ Heart
disease,” said the Doctor. “Another light that
failed,” added the gaoler, throwing down the
stub of his cigar.

Holdar M. : “ Sweet woman’s clinging
nature has been as widely commended as her
generally noble resignation in the face of adversity.
The former attribute stands good, but the latter
is doubtful. For instance : It was a wet and wail-
ing night, and a Sydney suburban bus was making
for the city. A damp, disordered crowd filled
every inside seat. The collection of twopenny
fares proceeded smoothly until one fair female was
challenged. Her contribution to the fare-box, she
admitted, was one brown ; the other brown, as she
shrilly explained to a cynical driver, had slipped
amidst the straw, whence he could afterwards
recover it. The Jehu was obdurate, but the lady
protested, and this impecunious scribe, desiring
yieace, struck matches whilst the lady searched.
The lost coin refused to appear, but a pint of rain-
water came out of the hollow crown of the lady’s
hat, and flooded the search-party. The driver
outside grew blasphemous, and objurgated in the
midst of the driving rain and the furious south
wind. One horse went down on the slippery blocks
while he was ‘driving blind’ with his mouth
down at the trap threatening battle, murder and
sudden death if the other brown wasn’t forth-
coming. The girl dissolved into tears and still
looked for her lost penny in the straw. The damp
crowd inside took the matter cynically, and only
made strong remarks about the fallen horse.
i inally that animal was got on its feet and held
up by a strong volunteer force, and then the
sodden driver came to the door and announced
that the bus wouldn’t move another step till the
copper was forthcoming. Upon which the dis-
tracted female got out a good-sized roll of notes,
and extracted a well-filled purse from underneath
the notes, and paid. The clinging nature of
woman hasn’t been over-rated in the least. For
clinging to a penny on a wet night I don’t know
her equal.”
Witnesses before Vic. Law Commission
are most unanimous when they complain of the
system that enables crown officials to charge
heavy fees for doing the work for which they are
also heavily salaried. Cases are instanced of a
judge’s associate charging six guineas for a two-
hours’ job, and the prothonotary demands two
guineas from every citizen who wants to look at
one of the documents for the care of which the
official is already paid a fine, fat screw by the
State. “ Why,” wails one witness, “ should there
be any fee paid to Government servants for doing
work that comes within the scope of their ordinary
duties?” This is so innocent that even echo is
not expected to reply, excepting with a casual
gibber of mirth. Why should a crow pick out the
eyes of the gentle jumbuck when he gets a chance 1
The Licensing Bench in Melbourne is com-
posed of Judge Molesworth, Nicholson, P.M.,
and Panton, P.M. Molesworth and Nicholson
are always in accord. Panton is always a dis-
sentient and indignant minority of one.’ In old
times in Sydney something of a similar kind was
represented by the Full Court. Judge Cheeke
always agreed with the Chief Justice, and so saved
all the bother of delivering an opinion. Fawcett
and Hargrave always disagreed with the Chief,
and took a lot of trouble to explain wherefore.
Ihe bar used to loot inexperienced juniors by
temjiting them with long odds to bet that Cheeke
would disagree. Cheeke’s “I agree with the
Chief Justice ” was as sure a principle to back
as the setting of the sun—and surer, since the sun
doesn t. set. “ Agree ! ” cried Dr. Bellinfante.
“ What an agree-able man is Judge Cheeke. If I
see him not agree once—only one e—parbleu I I die
happy.”
He was the least trifle seedy, and looked
like a British swell tourist whose fortunes had
just started on the down grade. Still they don’t
draw very tine distinctions in the seedy, dusty,
fly-infested, sere and yellow town of YVi cannia,
and when he drifted into the bar and had a drink,
and then ordered dinner, “including a bottle of
the best wine you have in the house, landlord,” he,
was served with alacrity. Having finished dinner
and an extra bottle of the wine, he sauntered on
to the verandah where the boss was walking up
and down and fighting with the flies. “ I have-
haw—had an excellent dinner, landlord ; very ex-
cellent dinner. Even the wine was not- haw—
bad for the bush. I—haw—thank you.” Then a
trifle louder : “ I regret, landlord, that I really
can’t—haw—pay you. lam stone-broke. I really
am—haw. But ” —here he turned round and
cleared his coat-tails for action—“ you may—er —
kick me if you like.” And the landlord kicked
enthusiastically without a word.
